1405 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Quiroga-Puma


LX6701631


JUSTICE COURT OF NEW YORK, VILLAGE OF WESTBURY, NASSAU COUNTY


18 Misc. 3d 731; 848 N.Y.S.2d 853; 2007 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 8379; 2007 NY Slip Op 27527; 239 N.Y.L.J. 8



December 20, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Later proceeding at People v. Quiroga-Puma, 18 Misc. 3d 1130A, 859 N.Y.S.2d 897, 2008 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 411 (2008)
Reversed by, Remitted by People, 2009 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 975 (N.Y. App. Term, Apr. 24, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State charged defendant with violating Vehicle and Traffic Law § 509(1), unlicensed operation of a motor vehicle, and Vehicle and Traffic Law § 319(1), failure to provide proof of valid insurance. The trial court then asked the State and defendant to prepare memos on the disposition of those charges and the sentence that the trial court might impose, if any.


OVERVIEW: The State charged defendant with violations of Vehicle and Traffic Law § 509(1), unlicensed operation of a motor vehicle, and Vehicle and Traffic Law § 319(1), failure to provide proof of valid insurance. The trial court presumed that defendant was in the United States illegally. It then found that Vehicle and Traffic Law § 509(1) was defined by Vehicle and Traffic Law § 502, the requirements for licensing. It further found that Vehicle and Traffic Law § 502(1) provided that an applicant had to furnish such proof of identity as the state motor vehicles department commissioner required. However, it also noted that the department's point system made it mathematically impossible for an illegal immigrant to prove identity. The trial court then found Vehicle and Traffic Law § 502 and Vehicle and Traffic Law § 509(1) violated the state and federal Constitutions' Equal Protection Clauses, and the state and federal constitutions' Privileges and Immunities Clauses, because national security interests were not furthered by them and not being able to get driver's licenses would keep some residents from earning a living. It also found that Vehicle and Traffic Law § 319(1) had no such problems.


OUTCOME: The trial court dismissed the charge against defendant of unlicensed operation of a motor vehicle. It found that the charge against defendant of failure to provide proof of insurance remained intact and adjourned a hearing on that charge to give defendant and the State time to consider the trial court's decision and any plea arrangements that could arise from that decision.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Proof of Identity Requirements for Obtaining Driver's License -- Undocumented Aliens

Vehicle and Traffic Law § 509 (1), which proscribes the unlicensed operation of a motor vehicle, and Vehicle and Traffic Law § 502 (1), which requires an applicant for a driver's license to "furnish such proof of identity . . . as may be required" by the Commissioner of the Department of Motor Vehicles, were unconstitutional as applied to defendant, an undocumented alien who was precluded from obtaining a driver's license in New York by virtue of being unable to provide the proof of identity documents required for licensing under the system adopted by the Commissioner in compliance with the Real ID Act of 2005 (Pub L 109-13 division B, 119 US Stat 302, 312). Undocumented aliens are deemed to be "persons" for due process and equal protection purposes. New York's otherwise facially-neutral licensing system is violative of equal protection as applied under a strict scrutiny analysis to the extent that it makes it impossible for undocumented aliens to prove their identities and thus obtain driver's licenses. It could not be demonstrated that precluding undocumented aliens from obtaining driver's licenses constituted a necessary means for achieving the state's legitimate national security concerns. Furthermore, New York's driver's licensing scheme violates the Privileges and Immunities Clauses of the federal and state constitutions (US Const, art IV, § 2; NY Const, art I, § 1) by discriminating against undocumented aliens with regard to privileges and immunities accorded to New York citizens.

1407 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Walton v. New York State Dept. of Corr. Servs.


01-04-ST4340


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, ALBANY COUNTY


18 Misc. 3d 775; 849 N.Y.S.2d 395; 2007 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 8605; 2007 NY Slip Op 27547



December 14, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed by Matter of Walton v. New York State Dept. of Correctional Servs., 2008 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9558 (N.Y. App. Div. 3d Dep't, Dec. 18, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On remand from the Court of Appeals (New York), the court was to consider whether constitutional claims by petitioners, family members and/or friend of inmates and nonprofit organizations that provided legal services to state inmates, regarding the charges for collect telephone calls from the inmates should be dismissed for failure to state a cause of action pursuant to CPLR 3211.


OVERVIEW: The action was brought against respondents, the state department of correctional services (DOCS) and a telephone communications provider. The DOCS and the provider had instituted a collect call system with the DOCS to receive a 57.5% commission. On remand, the court dismissed the petition. While petitioners claimed that the commission to the DOCS was an unlawful tax under N.Y. Const. art. III, § 1 and art. XVI, § 1, the Public Service Commission's failure to fulfill its statutory responsibilities under Public Service Law §§ 92 and 97 by declining to review the DOCS commission did not operate to transmute what was a tariff into a tax. Equal protection under N.Y. Const. art. I, § 11 was not violated because recipients of inmate calls could not be deemed to be similarly situated to the members of the public at large. Petitioners' rights to free speech and association under N.Y. Const. art. I, § 8 were not violated because limited telephone access by inmates had been found not to rise to the level of a constitutional violation and the rights of non-inmates to communicate with inmates had to fall within the contours of the rights possessed by the inmates themselves.


OUTCOME: The court granted respondents' motion to dismiss and dismissed the petition.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- State Constitutional Law -- Constitutionality of Single-Provider Collect Call System for Prison Inmates

Petitioners failed to demonstrate in a combined declaratory judgment/CPLR article 78 proceeding that the single-provider inmate collect call telephone system implemented by respondent Department of Correctional Services violated any constitutional rights of state prison inmates and their families. Petitioners failed to demonstrate that respondent imposed an unconstitutional tax upon them (see NY Const, art XVI, § 1) through the collection of a 57.5% commission from the telephone service provider. Since the Public Service Commission (PSC) possessed full authority to review the proposed rates to be paid to the service provider, including respondent's commission (see Public Service Law § 97), the portion of the PSC-approved tariff attributable to the commission did not constitute a tax. The fact that the PSC declined to review the reasonableness of respondent's commission did not operate to transform the commission into a tax. Furthermore, respondent's inmate telephone system did not unconstitutionally infringe upon petitioners' substantive due process rights or petitioners' rights to freedom of speech or association. In addition, the mere fact that inmate families and friends paid comparatively more for telephone calls than the general public did not result in an unconstitutional taking for which just compensation had to be paid or violate petitioners' rights to equal protection. The recipients of inmate calls cannot be deemed to be similarly situated to members of the public at large.

1409 of 1817 DOCUMENTS



Maron v. Silver


4108-07


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, ALBANY COUNTY


2007 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 8086; 238 N.Y.L.J. 109



November 30, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at Larabee v. Spitzer, 19 Misc. 3d 226, 850 N.Y.S.2d 885, 2008 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 276 (2008)
Affirmed in part and modified in part by, Motion granted by, Petition dismissed by Maron v. Silver, 58 A.D.3d 102, 871 N.Y.S.2d 404, 2008 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9745 (N.Y. App. Div. 3d Dep't, 2008)
Related proceeding at, Decision reached on appeal by Silverman v. Silver, 119 A.D.3d 449, 989 N.Y.S.2d 839, 2014 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5167 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, the State Comptroller and various governmental officials and offices, moved to dismiss plaintiff judges' action to compel the Comptroller to disburse funds appropriated in the state budget for judicial salary reform, and to test the constitutional adequacy of judicial compensation for the judges of the Unified Court System under N.Y. Const. art. VI, § 25(a).


OVERVIEW: The court found, inter alia, that inasmuch as Judiciary Law §§ 221 through 221-I were not amended, no increase in judicial compensation could be effected despite the appropriation of funds for that purpose. The governmental officials were entitled to immunity under N.Y. Const. art. III, § 11. The judges' petition did not support a claim that judicial salary levels were unconstitutional because they had been singled out. However, a trial had to be held on their claim of a violation of N.Y. Const. art. VI, § 25(a) based on a failure to maintain judicial salaries apace with inflation due. Because the alleged linkage between increases in legislative and judicial salaries did not create a suspect classification, or involve a fundamental right, the Equal Protection Clause was not violated.


OUTCOME: The motion to dismiss was granted with respect to certain causes of action, and as against the governmental officials, the State was awarded judgment declaring that judicial salaries as set forth in Judiciary Law §§ 221 through 221-I were not unconstitutional, and did not violate the Equal Protection Clause of the New York Constitution.


 

1410 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Voltaire


2006KN075810


CRIMINAL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


18 Misc. 3d 408; 852 N.Y.S.2d 649; 2007 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 7699; 2007 NY Slip Op 27486; 238 N.Y.L.J. 117



November 21, 2007, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant moved for a declaration that Penal Law § 265.01(1) was unconstitutionally vague and overbroad as applied to his conduct in allegedly possessing a gravity knife.


OVERVIEW: According to defendant, Penal Law § 265.01(1) was constitutionally defective because he could be convicted of violating it even though he did not know that the knife in his possession was a gravity knife. The court found, inter alia, that § 265.01(1) provided clear notice as to the specific characteristics that defined an illegal gravity knife. Furthermore, there was a rational connection between the regulation of dangerous knives and public safety. The police officer's action in recognizing the unique handle of a gravity knife jutting out of defendant's pocket was not arbitrary. There was no evidence in the record to support defendant's claim that he did not know his knife was a gravity knife. Consequently, the court declined to declare § 265.01(1) unconstitutional based on unsworn allegations regarding defendant's knowledge or ability to operate the gravity knife.


OUTCOME: The motion was denied.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Possession of Weapon -- Gravity Knife --- Constitutionality of Statute 

Penal Law § 265.01 (1), which criminalizes possession of any of a number of listed weapons, including a gravity knife, without proof that the defendant knew that the weapon he or she possessed fit the statutory definition, was not unconstitutional as applied to defendant, who was found in possession of a gravity knife near the entrance of a public school while students were leaving. The legislature has the power to declare possession of weapons seldom used for justifiable purposes unlawful without proof of criminal intent, provided there is a reasonable relationship between the public safety, health or welfare and the act prohibited. A gravity knife, which is specifically defined in Penal Law § 265.00 (5) as any knife having "a blade which is released from the handle or sheath thereof by the force of gravity or the application of centrifugal force which, when released, is locked in place by means of a button, spring, lever or other device," can seldom be used for legitimate purposes and is clearly used unlawfully as a weapon. Moreover, conviction under the statute requires the People to prove that a defendant knew he or she possessed a knife, and that the knife was an operable gravity knife containing a locking mechanism that does not require any effort by the user. Thus, the statute provided defendant with adequate notice and the police with clear criteria as to the characteristics that distinguish a gravity knife from other types of knives.

 

1411 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Dillenburg v. State of New York


K1-2003-1208


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, CHAUTAUQUA COUNTY


18 Misc. 3d 789; 849 N.Y.S.2d 382; 2007 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 8568; 2007 NY Slip Op 27548; 238 N.Y.L.J. 112



November 14, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reversed by, Summary judgment granted, in part, summary judgment denied, in part by, Complaint dismissed at Dillenburg v. State of New York, 2008 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7300 (N.Y. App. Div. 4th Dep't, Oct. 3, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer, individually and in his capacity as a town supervisor, brought an action against defendant State, and others, challenging the constitutionality of various New York statutes relating to the State's payment of real property taxes. Following an earlier appeal and remand, the taxpayer filed an amended complaint alleging a violation of State Finance Law § 123-D(1). The parties moved for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The State claimed that the taxpayer lacked standing or capacity to sue. The court held, however, that State Finance Law § 123-B did not require an individual bringing the action to be affected or specifically aggrieved by the activity at issue. Also, the interests of those municipalities which might have been inequitably affected by a judgment were adequately represented by the State. Thus, the State's claim that the taxpayer failed to join indispensable parties was meritless. The taxpayer argued that the State's disbursement of funds by making tax payments to municipalities whose jurisdictions included State-owned land, while not also making tax payments to other similarly situated municipalities was unconstitutional. The court agreed. The granting of taxing authority to include State-owned land in certain communities, while continuing the exemption for State-owned land with the same characteristics or the same use to other communities divorced the rational relationship from any legitimate State purpose. The distinction made between State-owned land where taxation was allowed and land which was tax-exempt was arbitrary and had no rational relationship to any state purpose.


OUTCOME: The taxpayer's summary judgment motion was granted. The court entered judgment declaring that the State's disbursement of funds at issue was unconstitutional and permanently enjoining the State from paying taxes and/or payments in lieu of taxes at issue.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Equal Protection of Laws -- State Permanently Enjoined from Disbursing Funds to Municipalities under Unconstitutional Taxation System with Regard to State-Owned Lands

In a citizen-taxpayer action pursuant to State Finance Law § 123-b brought by plaintiff Town Supervisor in his individual capacity, defendants were permanently enjoined from disbursing state funds to municipalities under the State's taxation system with regard to otherwise tax-exempt state-owned lands. The existing haphazard taxation system is unconstitutional as violative of equal protection by permitting taxation of state-owned lands and providing payments in lieu of real property taxes for some municipalities but not for other similarly situated municipalities. The disparity in taxes imposed upon state-owned lands located within some municipalities but not on state-owned lands located in others (see RPTL 532, 534, 536; ECL 15-2115) is arbitrary and bears no rational relationship to any legitimate state purpose, resulting in invidious discrimination. While the State Constitution does not prohibit dual rates or require that all taxpayers be treated the same, it does require that those similarly situated be treated uniformly.

1413 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Handsome


2006KN076479


CRIMINAL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


18 Misc. 3d 543; 846 N.Y.S.2d 852; 2007 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 7704; 2007 NY Slip Op 27483; 238 N.Y.L.J. 91



October 26, 2007, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant made a motion to dismiss a complaint charging him with criminal possession of a weapon in the fourth degree under Penal Law § 265.01. Defendants also moved for dismissal of the accusatory instrument for facial insufficiency pursuant to CPL 170.30(1)(a), 170.35(1)(a)-(b), 100.15, and 100.40; to controvert the warrant under CPL art. 690; and to reserve the right to make additional motions pursuant to CPL 255.20(3).


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought to dismiss the complaint against him, alleging that § 265.01 was unconstitutional under the Second Amendment of the United States Constitution. Upon consideration, the court denied defendant's motions except that to reserve his rights to make further motions. Reviewing the existing precedent, the court held that any right under the Second Amendment was not applicable to the states and that the Second Amendment did not protect an individual and personal right to bear arms but only the collective right to bear arms within the context of a well-regulated militia. The accusatory instrument was not facially insufficient under CPL 100.40(4)(b) because the State was not required to allege that defendant was not licensed to possess the weapon; because the exemptions in Penal Law § 265.20 from criminal liability for weapons possession were separate from Penal Law § 265.01, those exemptions were defenses to be raised at trial. The search warrant was not invalid because the fact that a confidential informant observed a weapon in defendant's home shortly before the search warrant application was not a sufficient delay to render the evidence stale.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motions in their entirety with the exception that defendant's motion to reserve his rights to make further motions was granted to the extent provided for by CPL 255.20(3).


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Right to Bear Arms -- Validity of Penal Law § 265.01 (1)

1. Penal Law § 265.01 (1), which imposes liability for the possession of "any firearm," does not violate the Second Amendment of the United States Constitution. The Second Amendment has never been found applicable to the states, either directly or through the Fourteenth Amendment, and provides no bar to state regulation such as Penal Law § 265.01 (1). Even if it were made applicable to the states, it does not confer an individual right to bear arms that would invalidate Penal Law § 265.01 (1). The Second Amendment's right to bear arms is not an individual right but a collective right, attaching only to state militias.

Crimes -- Possession of Weapon -- Penal Law § 265.01 (1) Not Unconstitutional as Applied to Defendant

2. Penal Law § 265.01 (1), which imposes liability for the possession of "any firearm," was not unconstitutional as applied to defendant, a 19-year-old suspected gang member who was arrested and charged with criminal possession of a weapon in the fourth degree after police executing a search warrant discovered a loaded .44 caliber Magnum revolver in his apartment located in a large public housing property. The Second Amendment does not confer an individual right to bear arms, and does not affect state legislation. The Penal Law restriction prohibiting possession of firearms by defendant, a teenaged suspected gang member who asserted no purpose for wanting to possess a firearm, was reasonable.

Crimes -- Possession of Weapon -- Facial Sufficiency of Complaint

3. The complaint charging defendant with criminal possession of a weapon in the fourth degree in violation of Penal Law § 265.01 (1) was facially sufficient even though it did not allege that defendant was not licensed to possess the loaded .44 caliber Magnum revolver found in his apartment. Although Penal Law § 265.20 (a) (3) exempts those licensed to possess a pistol or revolver from criminal liability under the statutes criminalizing weapons possession, that exemption is contained separately from the statute under which defendant was charged, and was a defense to be raised at trial. There are 23 exemptions concerning firearms listed in Penal Law § 265.20, and requiring the People to disprove them in every case brought under Penal Law § 265.01 (1) would defy common sense and reasonable pleading.

 

1414 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Sherwood v. New York State Bd. of Elections


6786/2007


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, DUTCHESS COUNTY


17 Misc. 3d 922; 847 N.Y.S.2d 428; 2007 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 6940; 2007 NY Slip Op 27419



October 16, 2007, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner nominee filed an application to order respondent New York State Board of Elections to certify his name to appear among the candidates for the Right to Life Party in the 2007 General Election, and to enjoin, restrain, to prohibit respondent county election boards from distributing the official ballots without his name, and to declare Election Law § 7-104 unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The nominee was a candidate for re-election to the public office of justice of a supreme court. The State Board of Elections did not provide the nominee a separate, fourth voting position on the ballot. The nominee alleged that the Board was forcing the emblem for the Right to Life Party to be placed on the Conservative party line and leaving the ballot position for the Right to Life Party blank. The court found that a side-by-side comparison of the two ballot layouts showed that the only candidate being affected was the nominee and that the Board's ballot layout made it appear that the nominee was running against the candidates of the Right to Life Party rather than with them. Since a separate line was reserved on the ballot for the justice candidates by the Right to Life Party, it would be unfair to the nominee, his running mates, and the voters to have a blank space where the nominee's name would otherwise appear. The court could not condone a remorseless reading of § 7-104, and the absurd result it would produce. Therefore, it was unconstitutional as applied to the nominee.


OUTCOME: The application was granted, the State Board was ordered to place the nominee's name on the ballots, the county boards were ordered to print and distribute ballots with his name on them, and § 7-104 was declared unconstitutional as applied to the nominee; all other requested relief was denied.


HEADNOTES

Elections -- Position on Ballot -- Exclusion from Independent Line 

 [***1] Petitioner, a candidate for reelection to the public office of Justice of the Supreme Court, Ninth Judicial District, and a nominee of the independent body known as the Right to Life Party, was entitled to a separate, fourth voting position on the ballot on the row associated with the Right to Life Party. Respondents' ballot layout made it appear that petitioner was running against the candidates of the Right to Life Party rather than with them. Although respondents were literally following Election Law § 7-104 when they excluded petitioner from the row, to do so was discriminatory and unconstitutional, both as to petitioner and as to the voters allied with the independent movement that wished to support the candidates only on the independent line. As the independent body's line already existed, the leaving of a blank space where the name of petitioner would otherwise appear would be nonsensical.

1417 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Brian L.


YO 35648


CITY COURT OF NEW YORK, WATERTOWN


17 Misc. 3d 724; 842 N.Y.S.2d 874; 2007 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 6582; 2007 NY Slip Op 27393; 238 N.Y.L.J. 74



September 28, 2007, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant admitted a violation of attempted forcible touching, Penal Law §§ 110.00, 130.52. The defense argued that the trial court should not assess the various fees outlined in Penal Law § 60.35 at the time of sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant had an I.Q. of less than 60. The defense argued that because defendant had a permanent mental or physical disability that kept him from ever engaging in income-producing activity, the court should not assess the various fees outlined in Penal Law § 60.35. The court found that the legislature overlooked persons too disabled to engage in gainful employment when it amended CPL 420.35(2) to no longer allow a waiver of Penal Law § 60.35 fees. The allowance of a deferral in payment of such assessments did not deal with the fact certain permanent disabled individuals would never be able to earn income from which to pay such assessments at the time of sentence or in the future. Defendant was thereby deprived of his 60 days of opportunity to earn the money, and thereby avoid jail or the consequences of a civil judgment. The court found that in such cases Penal Law § 60.35 was an unconstitutional violation of the Due Process Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution. The court found that defendant was entitled to a hearing to determine if his disabilities permanently barred him from engaging in income-producing activity.


OUTCOME: Defendant was granted a hearing to determine if defendant's mental or physical disabilities permanently barred him from engaging in income-producing activity.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Sentence -- Mandatory Fees -- Waiver -- Permanently Disabled Persons with No Income-Producing Capacity

Defendant was granted a hearing prior to sentencing on his conviction of attempted forcible touching (Penal Law § 130.52) to determine whether his mental and/or physical disabilities would prevent him from ever engaging in income-producing activity from which to pay the mandatory fees upon sentencing or within 60 days thereafter (see Penal Law § 60.35), notwithstanding the explicit statutory prohibition against any waiver of those mandatory fees (CPL 420.35). The Legislature, in amending the statutory scheme in 1995 to prohibit a court from waiving the mandatory fees, merely overlooked indigent persons too disabled to engage in gainful employment who would have previously been granted a waiver. In any event, application of the statutory prohibition would violate due process by denying permanently disabled persons with no income-producing capacity the benefit available to nondisabled persons who are able to pay the mandatory fees within 60 days of sentence and thus avoid the consequences of nonpayment.

People v. Garcia

SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, BRONX COUNTY

1419 of 1817 DOCUMENTS

Matter of Ryan v. Department of Social Servs. of Albany County


4685-01

1421 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. McGarghan


30043-2007


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


18 Misc. 3d 811; 852 N.Y.S.2d 615; 2007 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 8889; 2007 NY Slip Op 27555



September 5, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed by People v. McGarghan, 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2662 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, Apr. 5, 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant made a motion to dismiss a petition brought under the Sex Offender Registration Act.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted in Vermont of lewd and lascivious conduct under Vt. Stat. Ann. tit. 13, § 2601 and was required to register as a sex offender there. After defendant established his residence in New York, the New York board of examiners of sex offenders determined that defendant was required to register as a sex offender in New York. Upon consideration, the court denied defendant's motion. While the court had no jurisdiction to determine whether defendant was required to register as a sex offender in New York, the board was authorized to do so under Correction Law § 168-k(2); a CPLR art. 78 proceeding was the appropriate means to obtain a review of the board's decision. Because defendant was convicted of a felony in another jurisdiction for which he was required to register as a sex offender in that jurisdiction, Correction Law § 168-a(2)(d)(ii) applied, and no comparison of the elements of the foreign crime and a New York sex offense were required under Correction Law § 168-a(2)(d)(i). Thus, the court did not have jurisdiction to review the board's determination.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion in its entirety.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Sex Offenders -- Sex Offender Registration Act -- Registration by Sex Offenders Convicted in Other Jurisdictions -- Judicial Review of Registration Determination 

1. Supreme Court lacked jurisdiction in a Sex Offender Registration Act (SORA) proceeding to review the determination of the Board of Examiners of Sex Offenders requiring defendant to register as a sex offender in New York by virtue of his felony conviction in Vermont of lewd and lascivious conduct for which he was required to register as a sex offender in that state (see Correction Law §168-a [2] [d] [ii]). Under the statutory scheme, the Board was empowered to make that initial determination. The role of the hearing court is limited to assigning defendant a risk level classification and determining whether he is a sexual predator. There was no need for the court to determine whether the Vermont statute was equivalent to a comparable New York registerable sex offense. SORA expressly required that defendant be classified as a sex offender because of his conviction of a registerable felony in Vermont.

Crimes -- Sex Offenders -- Sex Offender Registration Act -- Registration by Sex Offenders Convicted in Other Jurisdictions -- Statutory Scheme Not Violative of Equal Protection 

2. The provision of the Sex Offender Registration Act (SORA) that required defendant to register as a sex offender in New York when he established residency in New York by virtue of his prior felony conviction in Vermont of lewd and lascivious conduct for which he was required to register as a sex offender in that state (see Correction Law § 168-a [2] [d] [ii]) was not violative of equal protection. The challenged SORA provision does not discriminate between out-of-state and New York residents. Rather, a Vermont resident and New York resident would be treated no differently under SORA. A Vermont resident who moved to New York after being convicted of a registerable felony in Vermont would be treated the same as a New York resident convicted of a registerable felony in Vermont. Since defendant would have been required to register in New York had he been a continuing resident of New York when he was convicted of the Vermont felony, he was not discriminated against based upon his state of residency. Furthermore, the challenged provision did not in any way unconstitutionally impede defendant's right to travel.

 

1422 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Tatiana R.


B-16930/06


FAMILY COURT OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


17 Misc. 3d 443; 841 N.Y.S.2d 834; 2007 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 6046; 2007 NY Slip Op 27351; 238 N.Y.L.J. 59



August 29, 2007, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: An unwed father filed two motions objecting to petitioner foster care agency's application to terminate his parental rights (TPR); the father claimed, inter alia, that Domestic Relations Law § 111(1)(d), which set out the requirements for an unwed father to have veto rights over his child's adoption, was constitutionally infirm because it denied him equal protection and due process based on distinctions by gender and marital status.


OVERVIEW: After the child was born, she was placed with foster parents. The biological parents were warned and notified that if the child remained in foster care for 15 of the most recent 22 months, the foster care agency might be required to file a TPR petition. The father did not maintain regular contact with the foster care agency or the child during his imprisonment. Following his release, the father tested positive again for opiates. The appellate court found that the child would be prejudiced if the father's motion to hold the TPR in abeyance were granted, since she had been in foster care for three years and deserved permanency. The father did not meet the requisite statutory requirements in Domestic Relations Law § 111(1)(d) to be determined to be a father whose consent was required for the child's adoption. Despite the fact that the father publicly acknowledged his paternity, his actions did not rise to the level of a consent father. The statutory distinction between married and unwed fathers did not violate the father's due process or equal protection rights because it was based on the father-child relationship rather than the gender or marital status of the father.


OUTCOME: The motions were denied in their entirety.


HEADNOTES

Parent and Child -- Termination of Parental Rights -- Termination Petition Not Held in Abeyance Pending Conclusion of Permanency Hearing

1. The proceeding to terminate respondent mother's parental rights to her three-year-old daughter, who had been in foster care since birth, did not have to be held in abeyance pending the conclusion of, and appeals from, the permanency hearing, and pending the court changing the permanency goal from "return to parents" to "free for adoption." The child would be prejudiced if the court granted the motion to hold the termination proceeding in abeyance made by her father, who had never been married to respondent mother and had been placed on notice of the termination proceeding, as the child had been in foster care for three years and deserved permanency. The father, on the other hand, was not prejudiced, as petitioner agency would still be required to prove the termination cause of action by clear and convincing evidence, and then, at disposition, prove that it served the best interest of the child to be freed, all of which would be appealable. Moreover, to hold the termination proceeding in abeyance would be contrary to the intent of the state and federal permanency laws.

Judgments -- Collateral Estoppel -- Effect of Prior Permanency Hearing Order on Termination of Parental Rights Proceeding

2. A permanency hearing order stating that the goal for the child, who had been in foster care since birth, was "return to parents" did not collaterally estop petitioner agency from filing, eight months later, a petition to terminate respondent mother's parental rights with notice to the child's father. Pursuant to Family Court Act § 1089 (a) (3), a permanency hearing must be held every six months. If a termination of parental rights petition is filed more than six months after a permanency hearing order was issued, the doctrine of collateral estoppel does not apply, as there no longer is an identity of issues and the party against whom collateral estoppel is sought did not have a full and fair opportunity to be heard.

Adoption -- Consent -- Unwed Father

3. In a proceeding to terminate the parental rights of respondent mother and free for adoption her three-year-old child, who had been in foster care since birth, Domestic Relations Law § 111 (1) (d) was the applicable statute for determining whether the consent of the child's father, who was never married to the child's mother, was required. Domestic Relations Law § 111 (1) (d) is applicable when a child born out of wedlock is "placed with the adoptive parents more than six months after birth," while Domestic Relations Law § 111 (1) (e) applies when a child born out of wedlock is "placed for adoption" under the "age of six months." Here, the child was remanded into foster care when she was less than six months old, but her foster care placement, at that point, was temporary and not the equivalent of being placed for adoption.

Adoption -- Consent -- Unwed Father

4. In a proceeding to terminate the parental rights of respondent mother and free for adoption her three-year-old child, who had been in foster care since birth, the consent of the child's father, who was never married to the mother, was not required in order for her to be adopted. Under Domestic Relations Law § 111 (1) (d), the consent of the father of a child born out of wedlock and "placed with the adoptive parents more than six months after birth" is required in order for the child to be adopted only if the father had maintained substantial and continuous contact with the child as manifested by both financial support and visitation at least monthly with the child, or regular communication with the child's custodian if visitation was not possible. Here, the child's father conceded that he did not provide any financial support for her at any time despite the fact that he was employed both before and after his incarceration, and the occasional gifts he did buy for her did not rise to the level of a fair and reasonable sum based on his means. The fact that she was in foster care did not excuse him from providing financial support, as he still had an obligation to offer assistance or inquire of the agency about his financial responsibility. Moreover, while the father did maintain some visitation with her prior to, and after, his incarceration, that minimal visitation alone was not sufficient to establish his consent status. Although he was unable to visit with her during the 20 months he was incarcerated, he failed to maintain contact with either the child or the agency during his incarceration.

Adoption -- Consent -- Unwed Father -- Constitutionality of Domestic Relations Law § 111 (1) (d)--Due Process

5. In a proceeding to terminate the parental rights of respondent mother and free for adoption her three-year-old child, who had been in foster care since birth, Domestic Relations Law § 111 (1) (d), which sets forth the criteria necessary for an unwed father's consent to be required for the adoption of his child, did not violate the due process rights of the child's father by denying him a right to veto the adoption. The father did not maintain substantial and continuous contact with the child as required by the statute, and therefore did not have a fundamental liberty interest that was protected by the Fourteenth Amendment Due Process Clause. Although a natural parent has a liberty interest in the care and custody of his or her child, that liberty interest may be limited where, as here, an unwed father does not establish a significant and substantial relationship with his child.

Adoption -- Consent -- Unwed Father -- Constitutionality of Domestic Relations Law § 111 (1) (d)--Equal Protection

6. In a proceeding to terminate the parental rights of respondent mother and free for adoption her three-year-old child, who had been in foster care since birth, Domestic Relations Law § 111 (1) (d), which sets forth the criteria necessary for an unwed father's consent to be required for the adoption of his child, did not violate the equal protection rights of the child's father by denying him a right to veto the adoption. The father did not maintain substantial and continuous contact with the child as required by the statute. Nor is the statute, which sets out requirements for unwed fathers to establish a right to veto an adoption while not requiring such a showing for married fathers or any mothers, unconstitutional on its face. Statutes that draw gender-based distinctions must serve important governmental objectives and must be substantially related to achievement of those objectives in order to withstand judicial scrutiny under the Fourteenth Amendment Equal Protection Clause. Here, the statute's requirement that unwed fathers demonstrate substantial and continuous contact with their child substantially relates to the government's interest of ensuring the welfare of a child born out of wedlock.

 

1423 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Western NY Dist., Inc. v. Village of Lancaster


2007-4296


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, ERIE COUNTY


17 Misc. 3d 798; 841 N.Y.S.2d 740; 2007 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 6126; 2007 NY Slip Op 27354; 238 N.Y.L.J. 60



August 28, 2007, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, a church and a district, initiated a proceeding against respondents, a village, the village board of trustees, a village code enforcement officer, and a village community development corporation, for declaratory and other relief under CPLR art. 78 regarding the board's determination to deny petitioners' request for a special use permit to locate a church in an industrial park.


OVERVIEW: The board properly denied the church's request because, after balancing a church's presumed beneficial effects against industrial uses in the industrial park, it found it was in the over-all public interest to deny the request based on the following legitimate interests impacting the public welfare: (1) creating an industrial park to advance economic development; (2) use of public investment and development of the industrial park for industrial uses, including economic development through job creation, property taxes and related economic activities; and (3) use of the industrial park consistent with a comprehensive plan. Economic development was a legitimate consideration to balance against the church's presumed beneficial impact. There was a substantial relationship between the denial of the request for a special permit and legitimate considerations limiting use of the industrial park to industrial and related uses to advance economic development and compliance with a comprehensive plan. Village of Lancaster, N.Y., Code § 152-26, addressing industrial park zoning, was, on its face and as applied, unconstitutional as applied to a church without using a balancing approach.


OUTCOME: The church's and the district's application for a special use permit was denied, and respondents' determination was upheld. The petition and the complaint were dismissed.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Special Use Permit -- Construction of Church in Municipally Zoned Industrial Park -- Balancing Approach 

The determination of respondent Village Board denying petitioner's application for a special use permit to construct a church on commercial property located in a municipally created industrial park restricted to industrial and related uses was upheld as having a rational basis and being supported by substantial evidence (see CPLR 7803 [3]). In assessing a special use permit application to expand a religious use into such a municipally zoned area, zoning officials are required to utilize a balancing approach that weighs the presumed beneficial effects of the religious use against the municipality's "other legitimate competing interests," with primary consideration being given to the overall impact on the public welfare. Here, although construction of a church would not have adverse effects on traditional police power concerns such as traffic congestion, respondent otherwise demonstrated that its determination that it was in the overall public interest to deny petitioner's application bore a substantial relation to the Village's legitimate economic development interests in maintaining the integrity of the industrial park in conformance with a comprehensive regional plan.

Matter of MHC Greenwood Vil. NY L.L.C. v. County of Suffolk


6688/07


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NASSAU COUNTY


18 Misc. 3d 312; 852 N.Y.S.2d 599; 2007 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 6405; 2007 NY Slip Op 27376



August 10, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed in part and modified in part by Matter of MHC Greenwood Vil. NY, LLC v. County of Suffolk, 2009 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 362 (N.Y. App. Div. 2d Dep't, Jan. 20, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, a planned retirement community, brought a CPLR art. 78 proceeding, seeking to annul Local Law 1534-2006, which was enacted by respondent, a county legislature, to extend to residents of planned retirement communities protections similar to those enjoyed by mobile homeowners and multiple dwelling tenants. Petitioner also requested an order permanently enjoining the law's enforcement.


OVERVIEW: The court first addressed a procedural matter and determined that petitioner's art. 78 petition was dismissed to the extent that it challenged Local Law 1534 on the ground of insufficient notice of public hearing. To the extent that the petition challenged Local Law 1534 on substantive grounds, the court converted the art. 78 proceeding to a declaratory judgment action. As to petitioner's equal protection challenge, the court found no evidence that petitioner was the sole target of Local Law 1534 and petitioner failed to allege how it was being treated any differently from other planned retirement communities. The court further held that respondent was fully authorized to enact the legislation and no evidence was presented that established that Local Law 1534 was preempted by state law. The court noted that the state legislature had not expressed any intent to preempt local regulation of retirement communities. Lastly, the court found no merit in petitioner's challenge that proper notice of the public hearings was not given as substantial compliance with notice requirements was made and notice was published in various county newspapers.


OUTCOME: The court granted a declaratory judgment declaring the local law valid in all respects. The court dismissed the proceeding to the extent that it challenged the local law on the ground of insufficient notice of public hearing. The court denied petitioner's request for injunctive relief.


HEADNOTES

Local Laws -- Preemption by State -- Federal Preemption -- Regulation of Retirement Communities 

1. A 2007 Suffolk County local law that extended to residents of "planned retirement communities" restricted to tenants 55 years of age or older protections similar to those enjoyed by owners of mobile homes (see Real Property Law § 233) and multiple dwelling tenants (see Real Property Law art 7), including a requirement that charges for rent and utilities be reasonably related to value, was declared to be valid and not preempted by state or federal law. It could not be inferred that the State, by addressing only mobile home parks, intended to preempt regulation of other "ground-lease dwelling communities," such as retirement communities. The county legislature was authorized under Municipal Home Rule Law § 10 to enact the local law in recognition of the similarity between the plight of retirees and mobile home owners. Furthermore, the local law was not preempted by the State's rent stabilization laws or by the federal Housing for Older Persons Act of 1995 (42 USC § 3607 [b] [2] [C]), a civil rights statute designed to create a senior housing exception to the general prohibition of age discrimination that was not intended to displace nondiscriminatory state or local regulation of housing accommodations.

Local Laws -- Validity -- Regulation of Retirement Communities -- Compliance with Notice Requirement 

2. The notice of public hearing concerning a 2007 Suffolk County local law that extended to residents of "planned retirement communities" restricted to tenants 55 years of age or older protections similar to those enjoyed by owners of mobile homes and multiple dwelling tenants was sufficient. The posting of the notice of public hearing to be conducted by the county legislature on the bulletin board of the clerk of the legislature, as set forth in the affidavit of the clerk stating that the notice was posted not less than three nor more than 15 days before the date of the meeting, complied with the statutory notice requirement (see Municipal Home Rule Law § 20 [5]), notwithstanding that the affidavit did not give the exact date of the posting. Even an untimely posting could still be in substantial compliance with the notice requirement. Furthermore, the notice of public hearing published in both official county newspapers stating that the local law "would enact protections for those who reside in planned retirement communities within the County" complied with the substance and purpose of the notice provision.

E. Meadow Union Free Sch. Dist. v. New York State Div. of Human Rights


6475/07


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NASSAU COUNTY


2007 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 6004; 238 N.Y.L.J. 36



August 7, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at Matter of East Meadow Union Free School Dist. v. New York State Div. of Human Rights, 2009 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6675 (N.Y. App. Div. 2d Dep't, Sept. 29, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner school district filed a proceeding pursuant to CPLR art. 78 for a judgment in the nature of prohibition permanently enjoining respondents, the New York State Division of Human Rights (SDHR) and its regional director, from holding a public hearing regarding a probable cause determination as to a complaint that the district engaged in an unlawful discriminatory practice in violation of Executive Law § 296(14).


OVERVIEW: The district had refused to allow a hearing impaired student to use a service dog at school. The student's parents filed a separate action in federal district court against the district in which SDHR was not a party. SDHR found probable cause to believe that district had violated § 296(14) by preventing the use of service dogs by disabled persons in an educational facility. The parents were denied preliminary injunctive relief in their action on the basis that they had not exhausted their administrative remedies under the Individuals with Disabilities Education Act (IDEA), 20 U.S.C.S. § 1400 et seq., and their failure to prove that the student had a speech discrimination score of 40% or less with the appropriate correction under Executive Law § 296(14). Based on that decision, the district sought reconsideration by SDHR of the probable cause determination, which was refused. The court determined that the district had not exhausted its administrative remedies under Executive Law § 298 as the public hearing had not yet been held. The district could also not claim res judicata or collateral estoppel based on the parents' action as SDHR was not a party to that proceeding.


OUTCOME: The court denied the district's petition and dismissed the proceeding.

 

1427 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Fox


8607/06


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


17 Misc. 3d 281; 844 N.Y.S.2d 627; 2007 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 5364; 2007 NY Slip Op 27317



August 2, 2007, Decided


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Hate Crimes - Selection of Crime Victim Because of Sexual Orientation 

1. Defendants' conduct in devising a plan to lure a gay man to a remote location in order to rob him fell within the scope of the hate crimes statute, even if their motivation might have been limited to their desire to obtain money and drugs. A hate crime is committed when a person intentionally selects a crime victim "in whole or in substantial part because of a belief or perception regarding the race, color, national origin, ancestry, gender, religion, religious practice, age, disability or sexual orientation" of that person (Penal Law § 485.05 [1] [a]). The grand jury evidence demonstrated that defendants deliberately set out to commit a violent crime against a man whom they intentionally selected because of his sexual orientation. The statute did not require the People to prove that the crime was motivated by bias, prejudice, or hatred toward the victim. Rather, the statute only required proof of the intentional selection of the victim based on a protected characteristic.

Constitutional Law - Validity of Statute - Hate Crimes Statute Not Unconstitutionally Vague as Applied - Selection of Crime Victim Because of Sexual Orientation 

2. In the hate crimes prosecution of defendants for intentionally luring a gay man to a remote location in order to rob him, defendants failed to demonstrate beyond a reasonable doubt that the statute was unconstitutionally vague as applied to their conduct. A hate crime is committed when a person intentionally selects a crime victim "in whole or in substantial part because of a belief or perception regarding the race, color, national origin, ancestry, gender, religion, religious practice, age, disability or sexual orientation" of that person (Penal Law § 485.05 [1] [a]). The statute provided clear notice that the intentional selection of a crime victim in whole or in substantial part because of that victim's sexual orientation was prohibited. The term "in whole or in substantial part," as set forth in the hate crimes statute, was not imprecise; rather, it sufficiently informed people of ordinary intellect that if they searched the Internet for the express purpose of finding a gay man to rob, as defendants did, they would be charged with a hate crime. Nor was the statute rendered unconstitutionally vague by the absence of an express requirement that the commission of the crime have been motivated by bias, prejudice or hate.

 

1428 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Primiano


OSC 1192(4-a)


COUNTY COURT OF NEW YORK, SULLIVAN COUNTY


16 Misc. 3d 1023; 843 N.Y.S.2d 799; 2007 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 5029; 2007 NY Slip Op 27297



July 25, 2007, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with driving while ability impaired by combined influence of alcohol and a drug or drugs pursuant to Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1192(4-a). He submitted an order to show cause in which he requested that the charge be dismissed.


OVERVIEW: A blood specimen showed that defendant had a blood alcohol level of .04 and that the drug Trazodone was present. Section 1192(4) defined "drug" as "defined in this chapter," whereas § 1192(4-a), which was newly enacted, did not define "drug" or indicate that the word should follow the definition prescribed in § 1192(4). Under Vehicle and Traffic Law § 114-a, the word "drug" included any substance listed in Public Health Law § 3306. Trazodone was not proscribed in § 3306. The court stated that in enacting Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1192(4-a), the legislature had the opportunity to amend the definition of the word "drug" under Vehicle and Traffic Law § 114-a or to redefine the term "drug" as applied to the new legislation. The legislature had chosen not to address the issue. The court could not presume that the legislature intended to broaden the definition of "drug" under § 114-a. Accordingly, the court held, the term "any drug or drugs" as used in Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1192(4-a) meant any substance listed in Public Health Law § 3306 as required under Vehicle and Traffic Law § 114-a. Accordingly, the charges would be dismissed.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the charges against defendant.


HEADNOTES

Motor Vehicles -- Operating Vehicle while Under Influence of Alcohol or Drugs -- Operating Vehicle while Ability Impaired by Combined Influence of Alcohol and any Drug 

A felony charge against defendant of driving while his ability was impaired by a combination of alcohol and the drug Trazodone pursuant to the newly enacted Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1192 (4-a) was dismissed, since the drug found in defendant's system was not a proscribed drug under Public Health Law § 3306. The newly enacted statute, effective November 1, 2006, proscribes operating a motor vehicle "while the person's ability to operate such a motor vehicle is impaired by the combined influence of drugs or of alcohol and any drug or drugs," but does not otherwise define the word "drug." However, since the term "drug" as used throughout the Vehicle and Traffic Law pertains to any substance listed in Public Health Law §3306 (Vehicle and Traffic Law § 114-a), that statutory definition of the word "drug" applies to a charge under Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1192 (4-a), especially in view of the failure of the Legislature to redefine the term "drug" in enacting the new statute.

1431 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Fairchild v. Riva Jewelry Mfg.


101169/2006


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


2007 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4912; 101 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 51; 238 N.Y.L.J. 5



June 28, 2007, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff former employee filed an employment discrimination action against defendants, a former employer and a former supervisor, alleging that he was terminated as a result of his sexual orientation, in violation of Executive Law § 296 and Administrative Code of the City of NY §§ 8-107 and 8-502. The employee filed a motion to compel responses to three interrogatories and the supervisor cross-moved for a protective order under CPLR 3103(a).


OVERVIEW: The employee alleged that he was an exemplary employee and that the supervisor encouraged, fostered, and maintained a work environment of overt sexual discrimination. The day before the employee was terminated, the supervisor questioned him about a lesbian magazine on his desk. The employee stated that he obtained the magazine for his lesbian daughter and also admitted that he was a homosexual. The employee was allegedly terminated without a legitimate business reason and as a direct consequence of the supervisor having learned he was a homosexual. In the employee's interrogatories, he asked questions about the supervisor's religious beliefs, alleging that such questions were relevant to demonstrating the supervisor's bias and intent to discriminate. The supervisor maintained that he did not place his religion at issue and that the employee was terminated for poor work performance. The court concluded that the responses to the interrogatories were being explored for the limited purpose of leading to further evidence to establish that the supervisor's proffered reasons for terminating the employee were a "pretext" and thus, the supervisor was required to answer the questions.


OUTCOME: The employee's motion to compel was granted. The supervisor's cross-motion for a protective order was denied.

 

1432 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Liotti v. Rice


2336/2007


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NASSAU COUNTY


2007 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4904; 238 N.Y.L.J. 4



June 26, 2007, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent county officials filed a cross-motion to dismiss petitioner taxpayer/attorney's petition to compel them to, inter alia, comply with a county administrative code and to stop hiring non-resident assistant district attorneys (ADAs); the officials claimed that the taxpayer/attorney lacked standing to bring the action.


OVERVIEW: The attorney/taxpayer claimed that he was an aggrieved taxpayer and property owner and that his tax dollars were being misappropriated to pay non-resident ADAs. He further argued that his clients had been indicted and convicted by non-resident ADAs. The court found that although State Finance Law § 123-b eliminated the need to demonstrate an injury in fact, it could not be used as a pretense to use the expenditure of money to challenge a government decision. The attorney/taxpayer's status as an attorney for criminal defendants did not confer standing upon him. The attorney/taxpayer did not demonstrate that his claimed injury was any different from that common to all taxpayers and citizens. Therefore, he lacked standing under § 123-b and General Municipal Law § 51 to bring the claim against the county officials.


OUTCOME: The cross-motion to dismiss the petition was granted.

Later proceeding at Suffolk Asphalt Supply, Inc. v. Board of Trustees of the Vil. of Westhampton Beach, 51 Misc. 3d 303, 25 N.Y.S.3d 809, 2016 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 47 (Jan. 11, 2016)

 

1436 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Town of Macedon v. Hsarman


57314


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, WAYNE COUNTY


17 Misc. 3d 417; 844 N.Y.S.2d 825; 2007 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 5986; 2007 NY Slip Op 27348



June 8, 2007, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant company filed a motion for an order granting summary judgment on its affirmative defenses and counterclaims, and to, inter alia, permanently enjoin plaintiff town from seeking to remove or alter its advertising signs without compensation pursuant to Highway Law § 88.


OVERVIEW: In 2000, the company received correspondence from the town requesting its cooperation in arranging to have its billboards removed. The company notified the town that it believed that the town could only demand removal of the signs if adequate compensation were paid. The company heard nothing more from the town after the town filed suit in June 2005, seeking a mandatory injunction requiring the company to remove the billboards and the imposition of a civil penalty for a violation of the town code. The court found, inter alia, that the signs at issue were located within 660 feet of a federal aid highway. Therefore, Highway Law § 88 was applicable. That section, by its unambiguous language, provided for just compensation, not amortization. Therefore, the town was required to pay the company just compensation before it could require the company to remove its advertising signs. The company showed good cause to seal an affidavit and an attached appraisal report.


OUTCOME: The motion for summary judgment was granted, and the application to place an affidavit and an attached appraisal report under seal was granted.


HEADNOTES

Highways--Advertising Signs--Taking of Billboards--Payment of Just Compensation for Billboards Covered by Federal Highway Beautification Act

1. Plaintiff Town could not rely on its police power under a town ordinance to require defendant to remove lawfully erected outdoor advertising billboards covered by the Federal Highway Beautification Act of 1965 without paying defendant just compensation. Highway Law § 88 (7) requires a municipality to pay full compensation in accordance with the provisions of article 5 of the Eminent Domain Procedure Law for the removal of billboards covered by the Highway Beautification Act. Consequently, the ordinance's provision for a 10-year amortization period, and not payment of compensation, for billboards covered by Highway Law § 88 was insufficient. The amortization schedules set forth in General Municipal Law § 74-c for the removal of outdoor advertising signs located in areas other than industrial or manufacturing zones are expressly not applicable where, as here, compensation is provided pursuant to Highway Law § 88.

Records--Sealing of Records--Proprietary Business Information

2. In an action in which defendant succeeded in preventing plaintiff Town from requiring it to remove its lawfully erected outdoor advertising billboards without paying just compensation (see Highway Law § 88 [7]), defendant demonstrated sufficient good cause to seal the court file (see 22 NYCRR 216.1) with respect to an affidavit and attached appraisal report that contained proprietary business information of minimal interest to the public.

1440 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Alteri


4744


COUNTY COURT OF NEW YORK, ESSEX COUNTY


16 Misc. 3d 167; 835 N.Y.S.2d 869; 2007 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2852; 2007 NY Slip Op 27160



April 26, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Affirmed by People v Alteri, 47 AD3d 1070, 850 NYS2d 258, 2008 NY App Div LEXIS 285 (3d Dept 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed an omnibus motion seeking, inter alia, to suppress all evidence seized or discovered from execution of the search warrant, and upon such suppression dismissal of the indictment.


OVERVIEW: After defendant, a town dispatcher, was arrested and charged with misdemeanor crimes of endangering the welfare of a child, third-degree sexual abuse, and first-degree unlawfully dealing with a child, the two town justices disqualified themselves pursuant to CPL 170.15(3), and the action was removed to another local criminal court. One month later, the police chief applied in writing to one of the disqualified town justices for a search warrant of defendant's residence. A videotape found during the search, depicting defendant's former wife and a third party in the marital home recorded by means of surveillance equipment without the consent of either party. That evidence gave rise to a single-count indictment charging defendant with the crime of eavesdropping under Penal Law § 250.05. The court found that town justices' disqualification rendered them without jurisdiction to issue the warrant. Because the search warrant was invalid, pursuant to CPL 690.05, 690.35(2), U.S. Const. amend. IV, and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 12, the evidence seized from its execution, including the videotape underlying the eavesdropping charge against defendant, had to be suppressed.


OUTCOME: The motion to suppress was granted, and the eavesdropping charge was dismissed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Search Warrant -- Validity of Search Warrant Issued by Disqualified Town Justice

Seizure of evidence from defendant's residence pursuant to a search warrant issued by a town justice who had been disqualified from presiding over defendant's pending misdemeanor charges violated defendant's constitutional right against unreasonable searches and seizures, and the evidence was suppressed. The authority of a local criminal court to issue a search warrant is regulated by statute and is limited to matters of which the court has jurisdiction (see CPL 690.05, 690.35 [2]). The disqualification of a judge is jurisdictional. Here, because the town justice had properly disqualified himself based on his close working relationship with defendant, he was without jurisdiction to issue the warrant.

1442 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Braun Bros. Brushes


021122/06


JUSTICE COURT OF NEW YORK, VILLAGE OF VALLEY STREAM, NASSAU COUNTY


15 Misc. 3d 1030; 838 N.Y.S.2d 408; 2007 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2452; 2007 NY Slip Op 27146; 237 N.Y.L.J. 91



April 11, 2007, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a corporation, was charged with violating a village code by not having a certificate of occupancy and by not having a permit for its brush business. It filed a motion to dismiss.


OVERVIEW: The court rejected defendant's argument that CPL 150.40(2), which allowed an appearance ticket for the violation of a zoning law to be served under any manner authorized by CPLR 308, violated due process under both the federal and state constitutions. The key to the commencement of any criminal action was the filing of an appropriate accusatory instrument. Once it was established that a sufficient instrument was filed, the type of service was of little consequence. Next, defendant had raised a sufficient defense of a prior nonconforming use of a substantial nature. It had presented affidavits showing that its property had been used for a brush business since the 1940s. The last serious construction project on the property was concluded in mid-1951, before a 1952 zoning code went into effect. A permit had been filed at that time. Construction under a validly issued permit where the property was a nonconforming use would validate such a use. Furthermore, it was contradictory for the village to seek to find defendant guilty for not having a certificate of occupancy when in 2003 it issued defendant a mercantile permit for the very same purpose and use.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the summons and information.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Appearance Ticket -- Violation of Local Zoning Ordinance -- Substituted Service Not Violative of Due Process 

1. In defendant's criminal prosecution for violating a local zoning ordinance by operating a brush business without a business certificate of occupancy or business permit, the service of the appearance ticket by substituted service pursuant to CPLR 308 as authorized by CPL 150.40 (2) was not violative of due process. CPL 150.40 (2), which expanded the means to serve process for violations of local zoning ordinances beyond personal delivery in order to make it easier and less time consuming to serve the violators with appearance tickets, satisfies due process requirements. Jurisdiction in a criminal action is acquired by the filing of a legally sufficient accusatory instrument with the local criminal court. The appearance ticket is merely a letter of invitation. Once it is established that a sufficient accusatory instrument has been filed, the type of service or the means in which the defendant arrives in court is of little consequence.

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Nonconforming Use as Defense 

2. An accusatory instrument charging defendant with violating a local zoning ordinance by operating a brush business without a business certificate of occupancy or business permit was dismissed where defendant demonstrated by substantial evidence that a prior nonconforming use existed on the property prior to the enactment of the appropriate zoning code. Furthermore, the fact that the locality issued defendant a mercantile permit in 2003 to continue and conduct its commercial business on the property operated as a complete defense. Under the local ordinance, registering a business with a mercantile permit ensures that all businesses are in suitable zoning areas and meet fire protection and structure standards.

 

1443 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Mendelson


2006NA 00602


DISTRICT COURT OF NEW YORK, FIRST DISTRICT, NASSAU COUNTY


15 Misc. 3d 925; 834 N.Y.S.2d 445; 2007 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1973; 2007 NY Slip Op 27137; 237 N.Y.L.J. 71



April 4, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at People v. Mendelson, 2009 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 558 (N.Y. Dist. Ct., Mar. 17, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with a violation of § 168-16 of the Code of the Town of Oyster Bay, New York, alleging that in a park owned and maintained by the Town of Oyster Bay, New York, she was distributing leaflets on behalf of "Jews for Jesus" without a permit. Defendant moved to dismiss the action, or in the alternative, to dismiss the information as defective pursuant to CPL 170.35(l).


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged the constitutionality of the ordinance which she was accused to have violated by her distributing religious literature and engaging citizens in conversation about religion in a town park without a permit. The court found that the ordinance acted as a prior restraint on the exercise of First Amendment Rights. Moreover, the ordinance imposed an absolute bar to various forms of speech and did not contain the reasonable time, place, and manner restrictions to provide safeguards for First Amendment liberties. The ordinance also contained no guidelines, standards, or criteria upon which a permit was to be granted or denied. Therefore, the ordinance vested in the town board unfettered discretion in determining whether to grant or deny special permission to engage in a host of constitutionally protected activities. Finally, the ordinance was unconstitutionally applied to defendant when a town official, authorized to entertain her application for a permit, refused to consider her application. However, the allegations in the superceding information that was filed were facially sufficient under CPL 100.40 and 100.15.


OUTCOME: The motion to dismiss the action on the grounds that the ordinance was unconstitutional on its face and as applied to defendant was granted. Furthermore, the motion to dismiss the information as defective was denied.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional Law -- Validity of Ordinance -- First Amendment Rights -- Permit Requirement for Distribution of Literature in Public Park

A town ordinance that prohibited the distribution of literature and other constitutionally protected activities, including speech, in any public park without first obtaining the "special permission of the Town Board," was unconstitutional on its face and as applied to defendant, who was charged with distributing religious literature and walking through a town park and speaking with people about their religious beliefs without a permit. The permit requirement was an invalid prior restraint on the exercise of First Amendment rights. The ordinance did not contain the reasonable time, place, and manner restrictions necessary to safeguard First Amendment activities. Furthermore, the ordinance contained no guidelines, standards, or criteria upon which a permit would be granted or denied and, thus, unlawfully vested in the Town Board unfettered discretion in determining whether to grant or deny "special permission" to engage in constitutionally protected activities.

 

1444 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Ciaprazi v. Goord


4623-06


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, ALBANY COUNTY


2007 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3293; 237 N.Y.L.J. 83



April 2, 2007, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate brought a CPLR art. 78 proceeding against respondent commissioner challenging the commissioner's denial of his application to have his confinement transferred to Romania.


OVERVIEW: The commissioner argued that Corrections Law §§ 5(4), 71 conferred on the commissioner or his designee sole authority to authorize prisoner transfer requests within the guidelines in the statute and that Correction Law § 5(4) prohibited the commissioner from considering for transfer any prisoner serving a term of life imprisonment. The court found that the plain language of Correction Law § 5(4) addressed only the conversion of an indeterminate sentence to a determinate sentence where necessary to make such person eligible for transfer either to federal custody or to foreign countries. And, although the statute withheld authority to convert a sentence from a maximum of life imprisonment to something less, the language did not include a prohibition against transferring individuals serving a maximum term of life imprisonment. Correction Law § 5(4) clearly envisioned that there would be situations in which sentence conversion was not necessary. Corrections Law § 71 did not restrict eligibility for transfer based on the sentence imposed. The interpretation of the statute used to deny transfer was arbitrary and capricious and based on an error of law.


OUTCOME: The determination that the inmate was not eligible for transfer because he was serving a sentence with a maximum term of life imprisonment was set aside. The matter was remanded to the commissioner for consideration of the application.

People v. Wilkins


93-30A


COUNTY COURT OF NEW YORK, YATES COUNTY


15 Misc. 3d 862; 832 N.Y.S.2d 428; 2007 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 996; 2007 NY Slip Op 27120



March 22, 2007, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to vacate his judgments of conviction entered more than 12 years earlier following a jury trial after he was found guilty of attempted murder in the first degree, criminal possession of a weapon in the second degree, two counts of obstructing governmental administration, and one count of resisting arrest, and those convictions were affirmed on appeal.


OVERVIEW: Following a jury trial, defendant was convicted of attempted murder in the first degree, criminal possession of a weapon in the second degree, two counts of obstructing governmental administration, and one count of resisting arrest. Defendant appealed and raised as appellate issues: insufficient evidence for attempted murder as accomplice, insufficient evidence of intention to use a weapon unlawfully against another, failure to instruct the jury on the definition of a firearm as related to the charge of Criminal Possession of a Weapon in the Second Degree, and harsh and excessive sentence. The appellate court then denied his appeal. More than 12 years after his convictions were entered, defendant filed a motion to vacate his judgments of conviction and alleged that his trial counsel should have asserted that some of the statutes used to convict him were unconstitutional. The trial court denied his motion because it found that defendant could have raised his claim on appeal and did not do so. However, the trial court also found that it could not find any authority to support his contention and his attorney otherwise provided meaningful representation.


OUTCOME: The trial court denied defendant's motion to vacate his judgments of conviction.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Vacatur of Judgment of Conviction -- Ineffective Assistance of Counsel -- Failure to Raise Ground on Prior Appeal

Defendant's motion to vacate his conviction, based on the ground of ineffective assistance of counsel in that his trial counsel failed to argue the constitutionality of several statutes and failed to object to certain jury instructions, was denied since defendant unjustifiably failed to raise the ground of ineffective assistance of counsel upon a prior appeal actually perfected by him (see CPL 440.10 [2] [c]). Because defendant's argument for vacatur did not involve trial strategy concerning his defense, and there were no additional facts that needed to appear on the record for appellate review of the issue, defendant could have pursued the ineffective assistance of counsel issue on his prior appeal. In any event, defendant's trial counsel's conduct did not rise to the level of ineffective assistance of counsel; no authority addressing the statutes defendant alleged as being unconstitutional was cited or found.
