Kuwaiti Eng'g Group v. Consortium of Int'l Consultants


600033/05


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


2007 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2446; 237 N.Y.L.J. 66



March 15, 2007, Decided


NOTICE:

THE LEXIS PAGINATION OF THIS DOCUMENT IS SUBJECT TO CHANGE PENDING THE RELEASE OF THE FINAL PUBLISHED VERSION.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed in part and modified in part by Kuwaiti Eng'g Group v. Consortium of Intl. Consultants, LLC, 2008 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3719 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, Apr. 29, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, a Kuwaiti company, alleged that defendant, a French company, breached and tortiously interfered with a master technical services agreement (MTSA) and agreements between defendant consortium and plaintiff when it refused to use plaintiff as the consortium's agent and appointed another agent for a project in Kuwait. The French company filed a motion to dismiss based on lack of personal jurisdiction and forum non conveniens.


OVERVIEW: The French company and non-party companies created the consortium to bid on contracts to administer environmental monitoring and assessment programs in Kuwait. In the MTSA, the members of the consortium agreed to use plaintiff's services to obtain local labor for environmental studies that were to be performed in Kuwait. Separate agreements between plaintiff and the consortium also provided that plaintiff would provide local labor to the consortium's members. Following the French company's refusal to use plaintiff as its local agent and its appointment of another sponsor, plaintiff commenced the instant action. The court determined that it could exercise long-arm jurisdiction over the French company pursuant to CPLR 302(a)(1) because the French company went to New York several times to negotiate the terms of the MTSA and the negotiations, which included e-mails the French company sent to New York, substantially advanced the formation of the MTSA. The court concluded that dismissal was warranted pursuant to CPLR 327(a) on the ground of forum non conveniens because the parties' dispute had no substantial nexus to New York, and Kuwait and France provided alternative forums.


OUTCOME: The court granted the French company's motion to dismiss the complaint.

 

1451 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Godfrey v. Spano


16894/06


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, WESTCHESTER COUNTY


15 Misc. 3d 809; 836 N.Y.S.2d 813; 2007 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 853; 2007 NY Slip Op 27105; 237 N.Y.L.J. 52



March 12, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed by, in part Godfrey v. Spano, 2008 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10226 (N.Y. App. Div. 2d Dep't, Dec. 30, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayers filed suit against defendant county executive seeking a preliminary injunction and a declaratory judgment with respect to an executive order. Intervenor same-sex couple's motion to intervene as defendants was granted. Defendants moved to dismiss the complaint for failure to state a claim.


OVERVIEW: The executive order required county agencies to recognize same-sex marriages where validly contracted out-of-state. The couple was legally married in Canada and resided in the county. The trial court held that New York recognized out-of-state marriages of heterosexuals that would have been invalid if made in New York if the marriage was valid where contracted. Since there was no legislation or appellate court ruling that declared same-sex marriages out-of-state void in New York, though valid in Canada, there was no positive law to interdict recognition of the marriage. Cases in courts of coordinate jurisdiction were not binding. The appellate court had not changed the law with respect to comity regarding recognition of out-of-state or foreign country marriages valid where contracted. Comity was to be applied in accordance with the attitudes of the community. There had been a change in the county as to the rights of same-sex persons. Thus, the General Municipal Law § 51 claim failed as no illegality was shown. The N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 2(c) and Municipal Home Rule Law § 10(1)(i) also claim failed as the executive order was not legislation, but merely the implementation of policy.


OUTCOME: The motion for a preliminary injunction was denied.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Marriage -- Same Sex Marriage -- Validity of Local Executive Order Requiring County Agencies to Recognize Out-of-State Same-Sex Marriages

Defendant Westchester County Executive's executive order requiring county agencies to recognize same-sex marriages where validly contracted out of state "for the purposes of extending and administering all rights and benefits belonging to these couples, to the maximum extent allowed by law," constituted a lawful exercise of the County Executive's power in the absence of legislation or an appellate court ruling prohibiting the recognition of same-sex marriages from other jurisdictions. The executive order constituted a lawful policy-implementing directive in accordance with the current and evolving state of law on recognition of out-of-state same-sex marriages as a matter of comity.

 

1452 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


McKinney v. Commissioner of N.Y. State Dep't of Health


6034/2007


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, BRONX COUNTY


15 Misc. 3d 743; 836 N.Y.S.2d 794; 2007 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 820; 2007 NY Slip Op 27097; 237 N.Y.L.J. 65



March 8, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Injunction denied by McKinney v Comm'r of N.Y. State Dep't of Health, 2007 NY App Div LEXIS 5364 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, Apr. 26, 2007)
Affirmed by McKinney v Commissioner of the N.Y. State Dept. of Health, 41 AD3d 252, 840 NYS2d 6, 2007 N.Y. App Div LEXIS 7562, 2007 NY Slip Op 5360 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, June 19, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a patient and a non-profit corporation, sought a declaration that L 2005, ch. 63 was unconstitutional and made a motion for an injunction enjoining defendant, state department of health commissioner, from implementing recommendations regarding the closure of a certain healthcare facility. The commissioner, along with defendants, the State and the state department of health, made a cross-motion for summary judgment under CPLR 3211(a).


OVERVIEW: The enabling legislation at issue created a commission to develop recommendations for reconfiguring the state's general hospital and nursing home bed supply to align such supply with regional needs. The commission recommended that a certain healthcare facility, which served the patient and senior citizens served by the corporation, be closed. The court determined that the healthcare facility was not required to be joined as a party under CPLR 1001(a) as the facility chose not to intervene and the only cause of action was the constitutionality of the enabling legislation. Plaintiffs could not assert taxpayer standing but had common law standing because they would be adversely affected by an administrative agency's decision. However, plaintiffs failed to state a cause of action because the enabling legislation did not violate N.Y. Const. art. III, § 1 with respect to the non-delegation of legislative powers as the basic policy decision had properly been made by the legislature, which could defer to a commission with specialized knowledge, and sufficient guidelines and standards had been provided for the commission to follow when making its recommendations.


OUTCOME: The court denied plaintiffs' motion for injunctive relief but granted defendants' cross-motion for summary judgment dismissing the complaint.


HEADNOTES

Parties -- Necessary Parties

1. In an action by plaintiffs, a patient at a health care facility facing closure pursuant to the recommendation of the Commission on Health Care Facilities and a nonprofit organization that provided services to senior citizens who used the facility, challenging the enabling legislation that created the Commission and empowered it to make potentially binding "recommendations relating to facilities to be closed and facilities to be resized, consolidated, converted or restructured" throughout the state (see L 2005, ch 63, part E, § 31), the facility was not a necessary party. To the extent that the facility might claim that the enabling legislation was unconstitutional, the facility's and plaintiffs' interests were interwined and coincided. Further, the facility could have avoided any prejudice by seeking intervention. Finally, plaintiffs' only cause of action involved an issue of law.

Parties -- Standing -- Constitutional Challenge to Statute Creating Commission on Health Care Facilities

2. Plaintiffs, a patient at a health care facility facing closure pursuant to the recommendation of the Commission on Health Care Facilities and a nonprofit organization that provided services to senior citizens who used the facility, lacked taxpayer standing under State Finance Law § 123-b to challenge the constitutionality of the enabling legislation that created the Commission (see L 2005, ch 63, part E, § 31). Although state funds will be expended in implementing the Commission's final report, the purpose of the enabling legislation was not the expenditure of state funds. However, the constitutional issue raised by plaintiffs necessitated a ruling on the merits, notwithstanding that plaintiffs' injury did not fall within the zone of interests sought to be protected by the enabling legislation for standing purposes.

Constitutional Law -- Separation of Powers -- Recommendations of Commission on Health Care Facilities Regarding Hospital and Nursing Home Closures

3. Plaintiffs, a patient at a health care facility facing closure pursuant to the recommendation of the Commission on Health Care Facilities and a nonprofit organization that provided services to senior citizens who used the facility, failed to demonstrate the unconstitutionality of the enabling legislation that created the Commission and empowered it to make potentially binding "recommendations relating to facilities to be closed and facilities to be resized, consolidated, converted or restructured" throughout the state (see L 2005, ch 63, part E, § 31). The enabling statute did not violate the separation of powers doctrine (NY Const, art III, § 1) by unconstitutionally delegating to the Commission the power to redirect the State's critical health care resources. In view of the complexity and difficulty of the issues involved with streamlining the State's health care system, there was no constitutional violation of the Legislature's deference to a Commission with specialized knowledge so long as adequate standards or guidelines were provided. The general guidelines set forth in the enabling statute authorizing the Commission to utilize an economic/cost-benefit analysis, and the enumerated criteria that were to be used by the Commission in arriving at its recommendations satisfied constitutional requirements. The Legislature was not required to promulgate specific guidelines as to how competing interests and costs were to be weighed.

Decision reached on appeal by People v. Basbus, 68 A.D.3d 441, 889 N.Y.S.2d 578, 2009 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8782 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, Dec. 3, 2009)

1455 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Violi v. New York State Dept. of Motor Vehs.


377/07


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, WESTCHESTER COUNTY


15 Misc. 3d 1044; 838 N.Y.S.2d 354; 2007 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2502; 2007 NY Slip Op 27151



February 16, 2007, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent state department of motor vehicles (DMV) made a motion to dismiss a CPLR art. 78 proceeding pursuant to CPLR 7804(f) and CPLR 3211(a)(7) for failure to state a cause of action. Petitioner licensee sought an order enjoining the DMV from taking an action to suspend or revoke his driver's license as a result of the licensee's alleged refusal to submit to a chemical test.


OVERVIEW: During the licensee's arraignment on charges of driving while intoxicated based on his refusal to submit to a chemical test after a traffic stop, a notice of temporary suspension of his driver's license was issued under Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1194(2)(b)(3). At the revocation hearing, the licensee's counsel made a motion to dismiss on the basis that the refusal report was left blank with respect to the facts to be set forth by the police officer establishing the reasonable grounds for making the arrest. The motion to dismiss was denied, and the licensee filed an administrative appeal with the DMV. A stay of the suspension of the license was granted; that appeal was still pending. The court determined that a viable claim for a writ of prohibition had been stated because the missing information in the refusal report violated the mandatory requirement of § 1194(2)(b)(2) and, thus, a jurisdictional defect existed. The licensee was not entitled to preliminary injunctive relief because he failed to show irreparable injury as while he asserted that he needed his license to practice law, he did not present any evidence that he had no other form of transportation available to him.


OUTCOME: The court denied the DMV's motion to dismiss the petition and the licensee's motion for a preliminary injunction.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Proceeding against Body or Officer -- Prohibition -- Suspension of Driver's License Due to Failure to Submit to Chemical Test -- Defective Refusal Report 

Petitioner, whose driver's license was temporarily suspended at his arraignment on charges of driving while intoxicated based on his alleged refusal to submit to a chemical test after a traffic stop, stated a viable claim pursuant to CPLR 7803 (2) to prohibit respondent Department of Motor Vehicles from taking any action to suspend or revoke his license. Respondent lacked jurisdiction over the chemical test refusal hearing because the arresting officer's refusal report was defective for failing to set forth the facts establishing the reasonable grounds for making the arrest both with regard to the traffic infraction supporting the stop and the indicia of petitioner's intoxication. The statutory requirements regarding the contents of the refusal report (Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1194 [2] [b] [2]) are considered mandatory in nature in order to satisfy due process concerns. A writ of prohibition was therefore available to petitioner based on allegations that respondent would be acting in excess of the jurisdiction conferred on it by statute. However, petitioner failed to demonstrate irreparable injury in the event he were to lose his license so as to be granted preliminary injunctive relief pending a final determination of this proceeding.

 

1456 of 1817 DOCUMENTS



St. Joseph Hosp. of Cheektowaga v. Novello


11568/06


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, ERIE COUNTY


15 Misc. 3d 333; 828 N.Y.S.2d 877; 2007 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 254; 2007 NY Slip Op 27035



February 2, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Affirmed in part and modified in part by, Vacated by, in part St. Joseph Hosp. of Cheektowaga v Novello, 43 AD3d 139, 840 NYS2d 263, 2007 NY App Div LEXIS 8546 (N.Y. App. Div. 4th Dep't, July 18, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a hospital and a corporate health system, moved for summary judgment on all causes of action, and defendants, the Commissioner of the New York State Commission On Healthcare Facilities in the 21st Century, the Governor of the State of New York, and the State of New York, cross-moved for summary judgment. Plaintiffs commenced the declaratory judgment action seeking to prevent the recommended closing of the hospital.


OVERVIEW: The Commissioner, acting through the Commission, which was created to review the State's health care capacity and resources, recommended closing the hospital. Plaintiffs asserted that the Enabling Act, L 2005, ch. 63, § 31, that created the Commission unconstitutionally delegated the Commissioner's duties under Public Health Law § 2806(6); therefore, it violated the Presentment Clause and Separation of Powers Clause of the New York State Constitution. The court held that while the Commission was charged under the Enabling Act with a comprehensive review of all of the hospitals and nursing homes in the State, the actual power given to the Commission was no greater than the power already vested in the Commissioner. The delegation of power under the Enabling Act was neither so controversial nor so broad to conclude that the legislature would not have been willing to make the delegation without a legislative veto. The court did hold that plaintiffs had a property interest in the hospital's operating license but that no violation of plaintiffs' substantive due process rights occurred since the actions permitted by the Enabling Act were within the boundaries of the State's police power.


OUTCOME: The court denied plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment and granted defendants' motion for summary judgment. The court, therefore, dismissed the complaint in all respects.



HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Hospital Closings Recommended by State Commission -- Severability of Legislative Veto Provision

1. The statute creating the Temporary State Commission on Health Care Facilities, which was charged with review of the State's health care delivery system infrastructure and authorized to recommend hospital and nursing home closings and reorganizations, and requiring the Health Commissioner to implement the Commission's recommendations unless those recommendations were rejected by the Legislature (L 2005, ch 63, part E, § 31) is not unconstitutional under the Presentment Clause of the State Constitution (NY Const, art IV, § 7) for failing to provide for the enactment of legislation with presentment to the Governor. Even if the legislative veto is unconstitutional, that provision of the enabling statute may be severed from the other substantive provisions of the statute in view of the statute's comprehensive severance clause. Furthermore, the absence of the legislative veto would not result in an unconstitutional delegation of legislative powers, since the actual power given to the Commission without legislative oversight was no greater than the broad power already vested in the Health Commissioner (see Public Health Law § 2806 [6]). Moreover, since the legislative veto was not exercised, plaintiffs lacked standing to challenge the constitutionality of the legislative veto in any event.

Constitutional Law -- Due Process of Law -- Hospital Closings Recommended by State Commission 

2. The statute creating the Temporary State Commission on Health Care Facilities, which was charged with review of the State's health care delivery system infrastructure and authorized to recommend hospital and nursing home closings and reorganizations, and requiring the Health Commissioner to implement the Commission's recommendations unless those recommendations were rejected by the Legislature (L 2005, ch 63, part E, § 31) did not violate plaintiff health care providers' substantive or procedural due process rights. While plaintiffs have a protected property interest in their operating certificates (see Public Health Law § 2806 [6]), any termination of that property interest would be rationally related to a legitimate state interest to promote stability and efficiency in the health care delivery system infrastructure, and would thus not violate substantive due process. Furthermore, plaintiffs were afforded procedural due process by having received notice of the deliberations of the Commission and an opportunity to submit written material. A more formal hearing process was not constitutionally required for the Commission to fulfill its legislative mandate. In addition, the absence of a specific provision in the enabling statute providing for judicial review of the Commission's recommendations did not render the statute unconstitutional as violative of due process.

Constitutional Law -- Freedom of Religion -- Hospital Closings Recommended by State Commission

3. Plaintiff, a religiously affiliated hospital, was not entitled to injunctive relief preventing the Commissioner of Health from implementing the recom mendations of the Temporary State Commission on Health Care Facilities regarding hospital and nursing home closings and reorganizations as required by law (see L 2005, ch 63, part E, § 31) on the ground that the closing of the hospital would violate its state or federal constitutional free exercise of religion rights. The enabling statute was enacted in furtherance of the legitimate state interest to promote stability and efficiency in the health care delivery system infrastructure. Plaintiff failed to demonstrate how the closing of its hospital would deprive it of the ability to perform any religious practice or otherwise violate its religious tenets.

Constitutional Law -- Contract Clause -- Hospital Closings Recommended by State Commission 

4. Plaintiff health care providers were not entitled to injunctive relief preventing the Commissioner of Health from implementing the recommendations of the Temporary State Commission on Health Care Facilities regarding hospital and nursing home closings and reorganizations in accordance with law (see L 2005, ch 63, part E, § 31) on the ground that the closing of their facilities would unconstitutionally impair their contractual relationships with suppliers, vendors and employees. Any impairment of plaintiffs' contract rights was necessary to effectuate the purpose of the enabling statute to promote stability and efficiency in the State's health care delivery system infrastructure.

 

1457 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. White v. Warden, Rikers Is. Correctional Facility


75071-06


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, BRONX COUNTY


15 Misc. 3d 360; 833 N.Y.S.2d 363; 2007 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 275; 2007 NY Slip Op 27038; 237 N.Y.L.J. 36



January 26, 2007, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner moved to vacate a warrant charging him with violating the conditions of his post-release supervision. The People opposed the motion.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner claimed that because post-release supervision was not imposed by the judge who sentenced him, but instead by the New York State Department of Correctional Services, the conditions of his post-release supervision were void. Although Penal Law § 70.45 provided that the sentence imposed upon defendant's probation violation also included a term of post-release supervision, the trial court did not announce that it was imposing such a term, and the commitment sheet did not contain a notation that such a term was included in the petitioner's sentence. The appellate court held that because the sentence pronounced by the trial court both orally and in the commitment order made no mention of a period of post-release supervision, and because the sentence was not corrected in a direct proceeding, the sentence announced by the trial court was irrebuttably presumed to have said what it was meant to say, and did not include a period of post-release supervision. The appellate court further held that defendant did not waive his claim, because petitioner did not know of the post-release supervision until the Department of Correctional Services informed him of the condition of release.


OUTCOME: The petition was sustained, and the parole warrant was vacated.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Sentence -- Postrelease Supervision -- Invalidity of Administratively Imposed Period of Postrelease Supervision

Petitioner's writ of habeas corpus (CPLR 7002 [a]) was granted vacating a parole warrant charging him with violating the conditions of his statutorily-mandated postrelease supervision (Penal Law § 70.45) where the then maximum three-year period of postrelease supervision for his class D violent felony offense was invalidly imposed by the Department of Correctional Services and not by the sentencing court. Since, under the sentencing statute in effect at the time of petitioner's crime, the sentencing court had the discretion to impose a shorter period of mandatory postrelease supervision of not less than 1 1/2 years on petitioner as a first time violent felony offender, the maximum period of postrelease supervision cannot be deemed to have been "judicially imposed" by the automatic operation of law. Furthermore, petitioner did not waive or fail to preserve the constitutional claim that the administratively imposed period of postrelease supervision was invalid. Even if defendant, by his guilty plea, waived the right to appeal, that waiver did not encompass the sentencing claim raised in this proceeding. Moreover, since petitioner was not made aware of the period of postrelease supervision any time before or at sentencing, there was no basis to conclude that he was capable of preserving the claim in the context of the criminal proceeding.

 

1458 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Alexis


4391/2006


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


14 Misc. 3d 978; 828 N.Y.S.2d 793; 2007 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 66; 2007 NY Slip Op 27009



January 12, 2007, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants moved, inter alia, for dismissal of each indictment or a reduction of the charges on the ground of legal insufficiency of the evidence; defendants claimed that the "marital exception" element to third-degree sexual abuse in Penal Law § 130.55 was constitutional.


OVERVIEW: Two separate grand juries were presented with evidence that defendants committed a series of sexual offenses against their wives. In instructing the grand juries, the assistant district attorneys omitted the language regarding marriage and/or did not proffer a definition of "sexual contact," thereby omitting any reference to the element that the parties could not be married to each other. The appellate court found that the only possible rationale for the continued constitutionality of the marital exception for third-degree sexual abuse was that a person had an absolute right to conjugal relations with his or her spouse based on an assumption that consent to such relations was implicit in marriage. However, given the established case law finding similar statutes unconstitutional, because the conclusion was inescapable, and because the invalidity of the act was apparent on its face, the court held that the marital exception to PL § 130.55 was unconstitutional. The evidence adduced before each grand jury was legally sufficient to establish the finding of each and every count of both indictments. The grand juries were correctly charged with respect to the applicable law.


OUTCOME: The motions to dismiss were denied, and the statute was declared unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Sexual Abuse -- Marital Exemption 

The marital exemption element to sexual abuse in the third degree under Penal Law § 130.55, pursuant to which a married spouse cannot be convicted of subjecting the other spouse to sexual contact without consent, is unconstitutional. Sexual contact includes "any touching of the sexual or other intimate parts of a person not married to the actor for the purpose of gratifying sexual desire of either party" (Penal Law § 130.00 [3]). Spouses have no right to commit crimes in the name of conjugal visits or familial relations.

People v. DiPippo


39/1996


COUNTY COURT OF NEW YORK, PUTNAM COUNTY


2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4129; 237 N.Y.L.J. 6



December 29, 2006, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted by a jury of first degree rape and second degree murder. Defendant filed an application, pursuant to CPL 440.10, to vacate the judgment of conviction entered against him and moved for an order granting Deoxyribonucleic Acid (DNA) testing pursuant to CPL 440.30 and for other and further relief. The People responded with an affirmation in opposition.


OVERVIEW: Recantation of a prosecution witness did not constitute "newly discovered evidence" because the affidavit impeached and contradicted evidence given at trial. The prosecution witness affidavit contradicted the testimony of eyewitness, who testified that she was present in the van when she saw defendant and co-defendant rape the victim, and the testimony of the eyewitness was corroborated by forensic examination of the victim, physical evidence recovered from the van, and defendant's post-arrest admissions. Also, the court found that defendant's ineffective assistance of trial counsel claims were either raised in previous motions, appeals, or could have been raised by defendant via his previous CPL 440.10 applications. Further, defendant's claim of ineffective assistance of appellate counsel was properly brought in an application for a writ of error coram nobis and was not the proper subject of a CPL 440.10 application. Defendant's application for DNA testing was granted on consent of the People to the limited extent that said analysis could be conducted should defendant show that testable biological material existed.


OUTCOME: Defendant's application to vacate his trial conviction on the grounds of newly discovered evidence and ineffective assistance of trial and appellate counsel were denied. Defendant's application for DNA testing was granted to the limited extent that said analysis could be conducted should defendant show that testable biological material existed.

1466 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Adinolfi


06-0758


COUNTY COURT OF NEW YORK, WESTCHESTER COUNTY


14 Misc. 3d 348; 823 N.Y.S.2d 662; 2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3184; 2006 NY Slip Op 26445



November 3, 2006, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, the sole shareholder of a corporation that contracted with a school district to provide "fill" for sports fields, was charged with violating ECL 71-2703(2)(c)(ii). He was accused of dumping solid waste at a school without a permit to operate a solid waste management facility there. He filed a motion to dismiss the indictment and a motion for discovery and inspection.


OVERVIEW: Defendant had previously been convicted of violating § 71-2703(2)(a). The court rejected defendant's argument that to be charged with a felony, he had to have been previously convicted of a misdemeanor, not a violation. A prior violation of any subparagraph of § 71-2703(2) could serve as a predicate conviction; defendant's interpretation would render parts of the statute meaningless. There was sufficient evidence that defendant individually committed the crime and thus could be held liable under Penal Law § 20.25 for acts performed in the name of his corporation. Defendant had personally contracted with the school district and with the company that supplied the fill; he had personally seen to the fill's unloading and placement; and he personally had a prior conviction. ECL 71-2703(2)(c)(ii) was not unconstitutionally vague. Finally, there was no reason to dismiss in the interests of justice under CPL 210.40(1). The evidence showed that defendant's actions had turned the area into an unlicensed landfill that had required a cleanup. This was a serious offense with serious repercussions, and such a dismissal would be inappropriate, particularly in light of the previous conviction.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion to dismiss the indictment. It granted defendant's motion for discovery and inspection to the extent of the information supplied in the People's responding papers and in the bill of particulars and consented-to discovery. Defendant could move for additional discovery upon a specific showing of necessity for such material.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Unlawful Disposal of Hazardous Waste -- Criminal Liability of Sole Shareholder for Corporate Conduct -- Prior Conviction for Violation Sufficient to Elevate Charge to Felony 

1. Defendant could lawfully be prosecuted under ECL 71-2703 (2) (c) (ii) for illegally disposing of solid waste as a felony based upon allegations that he intentionally dumped several hundred cubic yards of construction and demolition (C & D) debris as "fill" at a public school sports field without a permit pursuant to a contract between his closely held corporation and the school district that required the corporation to supply only approved "fill" for the field. Defendant, as the sole shareholder, officer and director of his corporation, was properly subject to criminal liability in his individual capacity for the corporation's conduct (see Penal Law § 20.25). Defendant personally contracted with a solid waste management facility for delivery of the "fill," was present when most of the "fill" was delivered and directly supervised its placement and unloading. Furthermore, defendant's prior conviction for a violation involving the illegal dumping of solid waste was a sufficient predicate conviction to elevate the charge to a felony. The statute does not require that the prior conviction have been for a misdemeanor. Moreover, defendant failed to apply for a "beneficial use determination" from the Department of Environmental Conservation that might have allowed the C & D debris to be used as "fill" (see 6 NYCRR 360-1.15 [b] [11]).

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Vagueness -- Hazardous Waste 

2. ECL 71-2703 (2) (c) (ii), which prescribes criminal sanctions for unlawfully disposing of solid waste, delineates specific, easily understood conduct, and is thus not unconstitutionally vague.

 

1467 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Kudelko v. Dalessio


300005/05


CIVIL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, RICHMOND COUNTY


14 Misc. 3d 650; 829 N.Y.S.2d 839; 2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3650; 2006 NY Slip Op 26488



November 1, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at Kudelko v. Dalessio, 2008 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 6474 (N.Y. App. Term, Oct. 30, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff consumer filed an action for fraud, forgery, and other claims against defendants, occupants of her property, a checking account holder, two other individuals, and two companies, alleging that they committed identity theft against her which impaired her credit. The checking account holder filed a motion for summary judgment dismissing the complaint. The action had been discontinued against the companies and one of the individuals.


OVERVIEW: Defendants allegedly opened credit card accounts in the consumer's name without her knowledge. The court found that any claims under the identity theft provisions of the Fair Credit Reporting Act (Act), General Business Law art. 25, were time-barred by General Business Law § 380-n of the Act. Factual issues as to actual damages were established by evidence of harm to the consumer's credit scores. She could seek punitive damages for her common-law fraud claim because the harm done by identity theft had serious public repercussions. Her claim for conspiracy to commit fraud was dismissed because it was not a separate cause of action. A claim that the checking account holder negligently allowed checks to be written to pay bills resulting from the identity theft was dismissed because he owed the consumer no duty to supervise and control his checkbook. Prima facie tort was unavailable because defendants were not motivated by the intent to cause economic harm. The other non-fraud claims were dismissed because they were part of the fraud claim. CPLR 3010(d)(1) did not preclude the consumer from calling an economic expert, but she was directed to disclose all of her experts within 30 days.


OUTCOME: The court granted the holder's motion to dismiss all of the consumer's claims except for her claim in common-law fraud. The consumer was directed to disclose the names of all experts she intended to call as witnesses and to provide the holder with their reports and curriculum vitae within 30 days. The holder was given 30 days of receiving the names of experts to schedule depositions, and all discovery was to be completed within 90 days.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Consumer Protection -- Identity Theft -- Application of Identity Theft Statute -- Common-Law Fraud -- Punitive Damages 

Plaintiff failed to state a statutory claim for identity theft against defendants under General Business Law § 380-s where the complaint alleged that defendants began their identity theft scheme by opening credit card accounts in plaintiff's name in May 2002, prior to the November 1, 2002 effective date of the statute, notwithstanding that plaintiff did not learn of the fraudulent accounts until January 2003. A statute that creates a new right of action that did not previously exist generally has only a prospective application. Consequently, the identity theft statute did not apply to defendants' conduct that occurred prior to November 1, 2002. However, plaintiff did state a claim for common-law fraud and put forth sufficient evidence to raise a triable issue of fact as to whether she incurred actual damages because of the negative impact to her "credit rating" resulting from the identity theft scheme. Furthermore, plaintiff was allowed to seek recovery of punitive damages since defendants' actions, if proven, involved a high degree of moral culpability that caused harm not only to plaintiff but to the general public as well.

 

1468 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Egon J. Salmon, Inc. v. Tamarin


SCR 70006/06


CIVIL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, RICHMOND COUNTY


14 Misc. 3d 338; 829 N.Y.S.2d 338; 829 N.Y.S.2d 832; 2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3164; 2006 NY Slip Op 26443



October 20, 2006, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Claimant corporation brought a small claims action against defendant, who was 78 years old, alleging that defendant had failed to pay real estate commissions. At defendant's request, the court transferred the action to the day Small Claims Part under NY City Civ Ct Act § 1815 based on defendant's age. Claimant filed an order to show cause in which it sought to have the case restored to the night Small Claims Part.


OVERVIEW: Under § 1815, senior citizens, disabled persons, and night workers could bring an action in day court. The court stated that § 1815 did not give such a privilege to a defendant. The statute, however, was unconstitutional under the equal protection provision of N.Y. Const. art. I, § 11. The purpose of § 1815 was to protect specific groups of persons, not claimants; thus, because a person's age or disability was the important consideration, the fact that the right did not attach to defendants made § 1815 unconstitutional. Moreover, § 1815 required the clerk of court to tell all claimants who appeared to qualify that they could have their case heard in day court. This procedure was also questionable, as a clerk could not be expected to guess a claimant's age or to detect a nonvisible disability. Next, the court declined claimant's application to restore the case to the night calendar. Claimant, as a corporation, could not assert that it was a hardship for it to appear during the day. Furthermore, although claimant argued that other members of defendant's law firm could appear at night on his behalf, claimant had not brought suit against the firm but against defendant individually.


OUTCOME: The court declined claimant's order to show cause. It held NY City Civ Ct Act § 1815 to be unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Courts -- Small Claims -- Right of Senior Citizen Defendant to Have Action Transferred to Day Court -- CCA 1815 Unconstitutional as Written 

Defendant, a senior citizen more than 65 years old, was entitled to have plaintiff's small claims action to recover real estate commissions transferred to day court, notwithstanding the fact that a defendant in a small claims action has no statutory right to request such a transfer. CCA 1815, which grants senior citizens and persons with disabilities the right as claimants to have their cases heard during the day, is unconstitutional as written. The right to have a case heard in day Small Claims Part must be provided to defendants and not just claimants in order to carry out the statute's intent to protect the rights of persons over age 65 and those with disabilities. The Legislature should establish a procedure for a party to assert this right, whether by motion or answer or some other method, and should also establish a procedure for notifying the public of the existence of this right.

1471 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Policano


2643/2006


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3375; 236 N.Y.L.J. 96



October 19, 2006, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After a habeas petition was granted, a federal appellate court affirmed the federal district court, but certified three questions to the state court as to the state of the law relating to depraved indifference murder at the time of defendant's conviction. Thus, ultimate disposition of defendant's habeas petition was still pending. In the meantime, the People charged defendant with criminal possession of a weapon. Defendant moved to dismiss.


OVERVIEW: The state trial court had not submitted the weapons counts to the jury in defendant's original trial. Nonetheless, the People argued that the federal district court had nullified the outcome of defendant's trial, thereby allowing the instant case to come within the CPL 40.30(4) exception to CPL 40.30(1)(b). The indictment did not violate constitutional double jeopardy protections where defendant failed to object to the state trial court's decision not to submit the weapons counts to the jury. Thus, he had impliedly consented to their dismissal and could not argue that his right to have his trial completed by a particular tribunal was violated. Moreover, the failure to submit the counts to the jury was not a determination that the evidence was legally insufficient. However, the indictment did violate defendant's statutory double jeopardy protections because the federal court, by ordering a conditional release, had not implicitly authorized a reprosecution of the weapons counts. The weapons counts were not before the federal judge who had granted the habeas petition. Those counts had already been dismissed. Thus, the exemption of CPL 40.30(4) did not apply.


OUTCOME: The motion to dismiss the indictment was granted.

1474 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Greens at Half Hollow, LLC v. Town of Huntington


13247/05


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, SUFFOLK COUNTY


15 Misc. 3d 415; 831 N.Y.S.2d 649; 2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4196; 2006 NY Slip Op 26541; 236 N.Y.L.J. 66



September 18, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Summary judgment granted, in part, summary judgment denied, in part by Greens at Half Hollow LLC v. Town of Huntington, 2008 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 7893 (N.Y. Sup. Ct., Mar. 5, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a limited liability company and a court system, sought a judgment declaring defendant town's local law codified in the town code to be unconstitutional; the local law purported to authorize the town to operate an administrative tribunal called the Zoning Violations Bureau to adjudicate all town zoning and land use violations.


OVERVIEW: The town enacted a local law, Local Law 26-2002, which was codified in Huntington, N.Y., Town Code § 198-125(B), and which authorized the town to operate an administrative tribunal called the Zoning Violations Bureau to hear and adjudicate all town zoning and land use violations. The limited liability company and court system sought a declaratory judgment invalidating the local law as unconstitutional. The trial court first found that the limited liability company and the court system had standing to object because the limited liability company was subject to the law and the court system had an interest in insuring that the local law did not usurp its authority. The trial court then found that N.Y. Const. art. I, § 16 established the district courts and specifically defined the state legislature's power to regulate the court system. It further found that the town's attempt to create the Zoning Violations Bureau violated N.Y. Mun. Home Rule Law § 11(1)(e) by adopting a local law affecting the courts that would supersede a state statute, and violated N.Y. Town Law § 135, which preserved the state legislature's authority and court jurisdiction over all violations of local law.


OUTCOME: The trial court declared and found that the town's local law, as codified in its town code and which enacted and established the Zoning Violations Bureau, was unconstitutional as a matter of law, and that its creation and adjudication of matters were void ab initio.


HEADNOTES

Parties -- Standing -- Constitutionality of Local Law Creating Administrative Tribunal to Adjudicate Zoning Violations

1. Plaintiff, an owner of real property within defendant Town, had standing to challenge the constitutionality of the Town's enactment of a local law that created an alternative administrative tribunal to hear and adjudicate all zoning and land use violations. Plaintiff was clearly subject to the constraints of the local law and was in jeopardy of further adjudications through the newly created Zoning Violations Bureau. Furthermore, intervenor Unified Court System also had standing and an interest in ensuring that a local law did not usurp the exclusive authority of the courts to hear and determine cases or impede the power of the courts as a coequal branch of government.

Local Laws -- Preemption by State -- Constitutionality of Local Law Creating Administrative Tribunal to Adjudicate Zoning Violations

2. A local law enacted by defendant Town that created an alternative administrative tribunal to hear and adjudicate all zoning and land use violations was declared unconstitutional as impermissibly usurping the judicial function without express legislative authorization. The State Legislature preempted the field regarding the creation and operation of local adjudicatory boards. The Legislature empowered the Suffolk County District Court with the exclusive authority and jurisdiction to hear and adjudicate all land use violations within the Town (see NY Const, art VI, § 6; Town Law §§ 135, 268 [1]; Municipal Home Rule Law § 11 [1]; UDCA 203). The local law unconstitutionally transferred certain judicial proceedings and adjudications from the District Court to the newly established Zoning Violations Bureau. Moreover, the local law created an inherent conflict of interest by providing for the appointment of an "independent" hearing officer by the Town Attorney who would also be prosecuting the zoning law violations.

People v. Figueroa


12706/89

September 15, 2006, Decided

 [*1207A]  [**361] Crimes--Murder--Depraved Indifference Murder. Courts--Retroactive Application of Judicial Decision.

 

1476 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Barrett


2006NY020867


CRIMINAL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


13 Misc. 3d 929; 821 N.Y.S.2d 416; 2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2437; 2006 NY Slip Op 26363



September 11, 2006, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Three of defendant cyclists were charged with disorderly conduct by obstructing vehicular or pedestrian traffic and parading without a permit; a fourth defendant cyclist was charged only with disorderly conduct. Each cyclist moved for dismissal of all charges as facially insufficient, and the three charged with parading without a permit challenged that law as unconstitutional on its face.


OVERVIEW: The complaint alleged that the cyclists were riding in a group of four. The cyclists argued that the accusatory instrument failed to set forth facts establishing that the cyclists participated in a procession, parade, or race for which a permit was required and that a permit was not obtained. The court held that the activity in which the cyclists were alleged to be engaging did not come within the language of New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 10.110. The court concluded that to interpret § 10.110 to include an event which was not specifically described in the statute would render it unconstitutionally vague. The court went on to find § 10.110 was unconstitutional because it required even small groups to obtain permits, without justification, and applied strict liability. Next, the court noted that the officer's conclusory allegations that the cyclists did not have a permit was inadmissible hearsay that did not satisfy the standards of CPL 100.15(3). Finally, the court concluded that the charges were sufficient to sustain the disorderly conduct charges against one cyclist, as they alleged that the one cyclist obstructed traffic by failing to obey a traffic signal.


OUTCOME: The charges of parading without a permit were dismissed. The disorderly conduct charges were dismissed as to all but the cyclist who ran a red light.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Parading without Permit

In the absence of any allegation that defendants participated in any organized or formal procession, parade or race for which a permit was required (see 38 RCNY 19-02 [a]), they could not lawfully be prosecuted for violating New York City's Parading Law (Administrative Code of City of NY § 10-110) based upon their having slowly ridden their bicycles four abreast at night through the Theatre District. Interpreting the statute broadly to apply to an unorganized group of bicyclists would render the statute unconstitutionally vague, since individuals would not be given fair notice of the type of conduct that was prohibited. Moreover, interpreting the permit scheme to apply to a small group of friends bicycling together would render the statute facially unconstitutional as overbroad on First Amendment grounds, since the statute would not be narrowly tailored to address the City's legitimate interest in regulating parades and other large gatherings which may disrupt traffic and create the potential for disorder. Furthermore, if the statute did apply to defendants' conduct, it would be deemed unconstitutional for improperly imposing strict liability on all participants in an event that required a permit, regardless of whether they knew that the event lacked a permit.

Crimes -- Disorderly Conduct -- Riding Bicycles Four Abreast through City Streets -- Obstructing Traffic by Slowly Riding Bicycle through Red Light at Intersection

2. In the absence of any allegation that defendants intended to obstruct traffic or otherwise create a public disturbance by their conduct, they could not lawfully be prosecuted for disorderly conduct (Penal Law § 240.20 [5]) merely for having slowly ridden their bicycles four abreast at night through the Theatre District in Manhattan. At best, the complaints alleged only a temporary inconvenience to an unspecified number of cars that were required to switch lanes. However, the disorderly conduct charge was sustained against a bicyclist who allegedly rode her bicycle at a slow rate of speed through a red light at an intersection along with a large group of other cyclists, thereby preventing vehicles and pedestrians from passing and moving forward in the street. Moreover, the allegation that the bicyclist committed a traffic infraction by failing to obey a traffic signal provided a basis for inferring that she intended to cause public inconvenience or recklessly created a risk of it.

 

1477 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Graham v. Dunkley


6123/2006


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, QUEENS COUNTY


13 Misc. 3d 790; 827 N.Y.S.2d 513; 2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2375; 2006 NY Slip Op 26358



September 11, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reversed by, in part, Motion granted by, in part Graham v. Dunkley, 2008 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 593 (N.Y. App. Div. 2d Dep't, Feb. 1, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant lessor moved, pursuant to CPLR § 3211(a)(7), to dismiss plaintiff's automobile personal injury complaint for failure to state a cause of action; the lessor claimed that the Transportation Equity Act of 2005, 49 USC.S. § 30106, expressly preempted Vehicle and Traffic Law § 388, which imposed vicarious liability upon owners of motor vehicles for negligent acts of permissive users of vehicles.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff was involved in an automobile accident with a rental car driven by defendant driver. Plaintiff sued both the driver and the lessor for her injuries under Vehicle and Traffic Law § 388. The court found that 49 USC.S. § 30106 (2005) did not confer a right or immunity that was essential to plaintiff's cause of action. Vehicle and Traffic Law § 388 defined the scope of vicarious liability as part of the substantive law of torts, and it had nothing to do with "commerce." Therefore, because § 388 was a legislative act within the New York State Legislature's inherent authority under US Const. amend. X, 49 USC.S. § 30106 was an unconstitutional exercise of congressional authority under the US Const. art. I, § 8 and US Const. art. VI. Consequently, plaintiff sufficiently stated a cause of action under Vehicle and Traffic Law § 388.


OUTCOME: The motion was denied.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Courts -- Jurisdiction -- Supreme Court -- Power to Pass on Constitutionality of Federal Statute 

1. New York State Supreme Court, as a court of original jurisdiction (see Judiciary Law § 140-b; NY Const, art VI, § 7) had jurisdiction, in a personal injury action against the lessor of a motor vehicle under New York's vicarious liability statute (Vehicle and Traffic Law § 388), to determine the constitutionality of the federal Safe, Accountable, Flexible, Efficient Transportation Equity Act: A Legacy for Users (49 USC § 30106), which preempts all state vicarious liability statutes to the extent that they hold owners in the business of renting or leasing vehicles vicariously liable for the negligence of drivers, except when there is negligence or criminal wrongdoing on the part of the owner. The power to pass upon the constitutionality of the federal statute under the Commerce Clause (US Const, art I, § 8 [3]) and the 10th Amendment to the United States Constitution did not fall within the exclusive jurisdiction of the federal courts in the absence of any federal law that specifically withdrew from the state courts the power to do so.

Motor Vehicles -- Imputing Driver's Negligence to Owner -- Constitutionality of Federal Statute Abrogating New York's Vicarious Liability Statute 

2. A tort claim under New York's vicarious liability statute (Vehicle and Traffic Law § 388) against the lessor of a motor vehicle involved in an automobile accident was not barred by the federal Safe, Accountable, Flexible, Efficient Transportation Equity Act: A Legacy for Users (49 USC § 30106), which expressly preempts all state vicarious liability statutes to the extent that they hold owners in the business of renting or leasing vehicles vicariously liable for the negligence of drivers, except when there is negligence or criminal wrongdoing on the part of the owner. Congress exceeded its constitutional authority under the Commerce Clause (US Const, art I, § 8 [3]) in abrogating New York's long-standing substantive tort law. The vicarious liability statute merely defines the scope of vicarious liability attributable to motor vehicle owners as an integral part of New York's substantive tort doctrine of imputed liability. The vicarious liability law does not have a "substantial effect" on interstate commerce that would justify congressional action in the substantive area of New York's civil tort law. Legislating in that area falls within the scope of powers traditionally reserved to the states under the 10th Amendment to the United States Constitution.

 

1478 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Gangemi v. City of New York


25972/04


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


13 Misc. 3d 1112; 827 N.Y.S.2d 498; 2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2683; 2006 NY Slip Op 26392



September 9, 2006, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, the City of New York, filed a motion to dismiss the complaint or obtain summary judgment in its favor dismissing the complaint. Plaintiffs, a husband and wife, brought suit against the City and co-defendants, the property owners and a restaurant, to recover damages for personal injuries suffered allegedly as a result of defendants' negligence in allowing a dangerous sidewalk condition to exist. Cross-claims were also filed.


OVERVIEW: The wife had allegedly sustained injuries when she slipped and fell on a sidewalk in front of the property owner's premises. The wife alleged that the accident was caused by a cracked, broken, raised, or uneven sidewalk condition. In support of its motion for summary judgment, the City maintained that, pursuant to New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 7-210 (2003), liability for injuries arising from the sidewalk defect claimed herein shifted from the City to the owners of the abutting real property. The court agreed that § 7-210(b) applied since the real property at issue was not a residential property. The court disagreed with the property owners' contention that only defects were covered since even before the enactment of the local law, property owners had a statutory obligation to maintain sidewalks abutting their properties. The court also found no taking of the property, since no physical invasion or ouster was claimed or demonstrated by the property owners, and the City made a prima facie showing that § 7-210 and its related enactments bore a reasonable connection to the public's health, safety, and welfare.


OUTCOME: The court granted the City's motion and dismissed the complaint and cross-claims.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal Corporations -- Sidewalks -- Liability of Abutting Landowners Regardless of Prior Notice of Defective Condition 

1. Defendant City of New York could not be held liable in a personal injury action based upon plaintiff's slip and fall on an uneven sidewalk in front of the codefendants' commercial premises that took place just four days after the effective date of the City's Sidewalk Law, which shifted the City's liability for injuries arising from sidewalk defects to the abutting property owners, other than small owner-occupied homes, regardless of whether the City had prior written notice of the defective condition (see Administrative Code of City of NY § 7-210 [c]). It was not inequitable to impose liability upon codefendants for the defective sidewalk condition which was in existence prior to the effective date of the Sidewalk Law, since the law imposes no additional maintenance duty upon abutting landowners than existed under prior law (see Administrative Code §§ 16-123, 19-152).

Constitutional Law -- Taking of Property -- Validity of New York City's Sidewalk Law 

2. New York City's Sidewalk Law, which shifted the City's liability for injuries arising from sidewalk defects to the abutting property owners, other than small owner-occupied homes, regardless of whether the City had prior written notice of the defective condition (see Administrative Code of City of NY § 7-210 [c]), does not result in an unconstitutional de facto regulatory "taking" of private property for public use without just compensation by requiring abutting landowners to incur the cost of repairing a city-owned sidewalk and to pay potential damages for injuries sustained by third parties or the cost of procuring insurance to protect against such risks. The codefendant abutting commercial landowners in a personal injury action based upon plaintiff's slip and fall on an uneven sidewalk in front of their commercial premises failed to demonstrate that the operation of the Sidewalk Law "completely" deprived them of "all economically beneficial" use of their property or interfered with a distinct investment-backed expectation so as to constitute a regulatory "taking." Furthermore, the law's liability insurance requirement does not constitute an unreasonable exercise of municipal power. There is no constitutional or statutory prohibition preventing a municipality from transferring liability for sidewalk maintenance to abutting landowners (see NY Const, art IX, § 2 [c] [ii] [6]; Municipal Home Rule Law § 10 [1] [ii] [a] [6]).

Constitutional Law -- Due Process of Law -- New York City's Sidewalk Law 

3. New York City's Sidewalk Law, which shifted the City's liability for injuries arising from sidewalk defects to the abutting property owners, other than small owner-occupied homes, regardless of whether the City had prior written notice of the defective condition (see Administrative Code of City of NY § 7-210 [c]), did not violate the due process rights of the defendant commercial property owners who had been sued for injuries plaintiff sustained when she slipped and fell in front of their premises just four days after the Sidewalk Law became effective. Defendants were not entitled to notice and an opportunity to be heard prior to the adoption of the Sidewalk Law. Due process requires "notice and hearing" in quasi-judicial or adjudicatory settings, and not with respect to the adoption of general legislation.

Constitutional Law -- Equal Protection of Laws -- New York City's Sidewalk Law 

4. New York City's Sidewalk Law, which shifted the City's liability for injuries arising from sidewalk defects to the abutting property owners, other than small owner-occupied homes, regardless of whether the City had prior written notice of the defective condition (see Administrative Code of City of NY § 7-210 [c]), did not violate the equal protection rights of defendant abutting commercial landowners, who were sued by plaintiff after she slipped and fell on an uneven sidewalk in front of their premises. Defendants failed to demonstrate that the Sidewalk Law treats them any differently than similarly situated owners of commercial property and multiple dwellings. Furthermore, the exception in the law for one-, two- and three-family homes has a rational basis in view of the legislative findings that small property owners who reside on the premises were less likely than commercial property owners to have the financial resources to bear the costs of sidewalk maintenance and repairs and that two thirds of sidewalk defect-related tort cases filed against the City involved sidewalks abutting commercial properties and multiple dwellings.

 

1479 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Chaves


06010092


JUSTICE COURT OF NEW YORK, TOWN OF OSSINING, WESTCHESTER COUNTY


13 Misc. 3d 782; 827 N.Y.S.2d 480; 2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2361; 2006 NY Slip Op 26356



September 5, 2006, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged in the trial court (New York) with two counts of aggravated harassment under Penal Law § 240.30(1). He moved to dismiss both accusatory instruments.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that his speedy trial rights were violated. The trial court held that defendant did not controvert the claim that the delay as to the second accusation was caused by adjournments requested by him. The complaint was properly converted within the speedy trial time limit. The first accusatory instrument was not insufficient on its face. The message was intended for the victim. The allegations allowed the jury to determine whether defendant acted with intent to harass the victim when he made a threatening call to her home. The statute was not unconstitutional as applied to defendant. Defendant's phone calls were not made in protest against governmental acts, included threats of an obscene sexual attack, and were addressed to the victim at her home. The victim's right of privacy clearly trumped the right of defendant to express his intention to sexually attack the victim. The calls were not protected under the First and Fourteenth Amendments or the New York Constitution. The alleged threatening conduct was not protected speech. Finally, the statute was not unconstitutionally overbroad, although prejudicial misapplication of the statute to protected speech persisted.


OUTCOME: Both counts of aggravated harassment were sustained. Defendant's motion was denied in all respects.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Aggravated Harassment -- Threatening Phone Call Answered by Third Party 

1. Defendant, complainant's former tenant, could lawfully be prosecuted for aggravated harassment in the second degree (Penal Law § 240.30 [1]) for having made threatening phone calls to complainant's house, including a call that, unbeknownst to defendant, was picked up by a third party. The statute does not require that the threatening communication have been made directly to the person intended to be alarmed or annoyed. Rather, the statute includes threats to third parties reasonably intended to be passed on to the victim.

Crimes -- Aggravated Harassment -- Constitutionality of Statute -- Threatening Phone Calls 

2. Defendant, complainant's former tenant, could lawfully be prosecuted for aggravated harassment in the second degree (Penal Law § 240.30 [1]) for having made threatening phone calls to complainant's house, including the threat of inflicting upon her an obscene sexual attack. Defendant's conduct was not constitutionally protected speech and expression. Rather, complainant's right of privacy outweighed defendant's right to express, in a call to complainant's home, his intention to sexually attack her. Moreover, the statute was not unconstitutional on its face when applied to defendant's threatening telephone calls. Defendant could not rely on the theoretical overbreadth of the statute to challenge his prosecution. A jury could reasonably find that defendant not only intentionally invaded complainant's right of privacy in her home, but also that his verbal utterances were vile, continuing and so threatening as to cause complainant to reasonably fear for her safety.

Senior Care Servs., Inc. v. New York State Dept. of Health


5826-04


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, ALBANY COUNTY


21 Misc. 3d 1117(A); 873 N.Y.S.2d 237; 2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 9237; 2006 NY Slip Op 52647(U)



August 25, 2006, Decided


NOTICE: THIS OPINION IS UNCORRECTED AND WILL NOT BE PUBLISHED IN THE PRINTED OFFICIAL REPORTS.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed by Matter of Senior Care Servs., Inc. v New York State Dept. of Health, 46 AD3d 962, 847 NYS2d 264, 2007 NY App Div LEXIS 12411 (N.Y. App. Div. 3d Dep't, 2007)


HEADNOTES

 [*1117A]  [**237] Social Services--Disqualification or Termination as Medicaid Provider.

 

1481 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Martin F.


NN 04511-05


FAMILY COURT OF NEW YORK, MONROE COUNTY


13 Misc. 3d 659; 820 N.Y.S.2d 759; 2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2257; 2006 NY Slip Op 26339



August 23, 2006, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a neglect matter involving respondent mother's two minor children, a county department of human services submitted a permanency hearing report indicating that an anti-psychotic drug had been prescribed for the three-year-old child over the mother's objection. The permanency planning hearing was advanced for a prompt determination of the extent of the department's authority to override the mother's objection in administering the medication.


OVERVIEW: The child's behavior was normal when she was placed in foster care, but five months later, she showed behavior problems. The department's foster care clinic medical director prescribed depakote sprinkles based on a consultant physician's suggestion. The department advised the mother of the decision. She objected, but a department caseworker signed a consent form, and the medication was given. During the hearing, the court issued a preliminary order vacating the department's decision. At the close of the hearing, it confirmed the preliminary order. Neither N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 383-b nor N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 18, § 507.1 authorized the department to administer mental health medications over a parent's objections. The mother's fundamental liberty interests were violated. Mental health medication could not be prescribed to a child in foster care over a parent's objection unless a court found, after a de novo hearing with counsel, that the proposed treatment was narrowly tailored to give substantive effect to the child's liberty interests. The burden was on the department to establish, by clear and convincing evidence, that a proposed medication met these criteria.


OUTCOME: The court confirmed its preliminary order that had vacated the department's decision to medicate the child. It directed that the department was not to administer any anti-psychotic drug to the child on a non-emergency basis over the objection of the mother without a further order of a court of competent jurisdiction.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Social Services -- Foster Care -- Administration of Antipsychotic Drug to Child over Parent's Objection -- Due Process 

The administrative consent procedure utilized by petitioner social services agency in approving the administration of an antipsychotic drug to a three-year-old child in foster care to treat the child's behavioral and emotional problems over the objections of respondent mother was constitutionally invalid to the extent that respondent was merely provided with notice of a decision already made and was not afforded a real opportunity to participate in the decision-making process. Under the circumstances, respondent's due process rights and the fundamental liberty interests of both parent and child were ignored. The decision to medicate the child was based on hearsay and limited information and was made without any complete professional evaluation of an existing mental health issue. Social Services Law § 383-b, which broadly authorizes a social services agency to consent to immediately necessary medical, dental, health and hospital treatment for an abused or neglected child in foster care when no parent is available to consent, does not authorize the nonemergency use of psychotropic drugs on children over parental objection. Rather, if the parent of a child in foster care opposes the administration of mental health medicine, the medicine cannot lawfully be prescribed unless the agency establishes by clear and convincing evidence that the proposed treatment is narrowly tailored to give substantive effect to the child patient's liberty interest.

1483 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Stern v. Bluestone


111895/05


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2495; 236 N.Y.L.J. 48



August 18, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Later proceeding at Stern v. Bluestone, 2007 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4803 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, Apr. 17, 2007)
Affirmed by Stern v. Bluestone, 47 A.D.3d 576, 850 N.Y.S.2d 90, 2008 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 583 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, Jan. 31, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff recipient filed suit in the trial court (New York) against defendant sender under the Telephone Consumer Protection Act of 1991 (TCPA), 47 U.S.C.S. § 227 (2006), seeking damages and injunctive relief for unsolicited advertisements sent by the sender to the recipient's fax machine. The recipient moved for partial summary judgment, injunctive relief, and dismissal and summary judgment as to the sender's affirmative defenses.


OVERVIEW: The recipient received 14 faxes from the sender. The trial court held that the faxes were advertisements as they had the purpose and effect of influencing recipients to procure the sender's services. That the sender sent the faxes on behalf of a for-profit law office confirmed their commercial nature. The faxes were sent without permission. A summary judgment had been entered against the sender for a TCPA violation. The sender willfully and knowingly violated the TCPA. The TCPA was constitutional. The sender's claim that the TCPA only applied to interstate calls was rejected. The recipient's request for an injunction barring the sender from sending further faxes was rejected as the recipient did not show that the sender was likely to continue sending faxes. Given the TCPA's lack of an express limitations period and the uncertainty in selecting an analogous state statute of limitations, the four-year statute of limitations in 28 U.S.C.S. § 1658 applied to TCPA actions. The sender's unclean hands defense was meritless as the recipient did not engage in unconscionable conduct by publishing his fax number in an attorney directory. The sender's laches defense was also meritless.


OUTCOME: Partial summary judgment was granted to the recipient and against the sender as to liability on the damages claim. The damages were to be determined at trial. Summary judgment was denied with respect to the recipient's request for an injunction, and that claim was dismissed. The sender's constitutionality, statute of limitations, unclean hands, and laches affirmative defenses were dismissed.

 

1484 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Griswold


06-05883


CITY COURT OF NEW YORK, ROCHESTER


13 Misc. 3d 560; 821 N.Y.S.2d 394; 2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2215; 2006 NY Slip Op 26327



August 17, 2006, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with violating Rochester, N.Y., City Code § 44-4, the Aggressive Panhandling Act. He moved to dismiss.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested for violating the Aggressive Panhandling Act, Rochester, N.Y., City Code § 44-4, by holding a sign asking for help on a public sidewalk. The Act, as applied, unconstitutionally burdened his right to expression. It was not proved that barring his conduct did not burden substantially more speech than necessary to achieve the city's legitimate substantial interests. Neither his sign nor his conduct embraced unsafe or unwanted behaviors the city could legitimately prohibit, nor were they intimidating, harassing, threatening, or potentially dangerous to pedestrian or vehicular traffic flow. He silently held a sign saying, "Homeless. Hungry. Please Help." The government did not show its legitimate interests would be achieved less effectively if it did not bar his conduct. No causal link was shown between this and the intimidation of passers-by or the disruption of traffic flow. As applied, Rochester, N.Y., City Code § 44-4(H) violated the First Amendment and N.Y. Const. art. 1, § 8 as it was not narrowly tailored to meet the city's legitimate and substantial objectives of preventing unsafe and threatening speech and maintaining traffic flow.


OUTCOME: Defendant's motion to dismiss was granted.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional Law -- Freedom of Speech -- "Aggressive Panhandling" Ordinance 

A municipal "aggressive panhandling" ordinance, which prohibits any person "on a sidewalk or alongside a roadway" from soliciting "from any occupant of a motor vehicle that is on a street or other public place," was unconstitutional as applied to defendant to the extent that the ordinance criminalized defendant's behavior in standing passively and silently on the sidewalk facing traffic and holding a handwritten sign that solicited help. There was no evidence that defendant approached or initiated any conversations with any pedestrians or motorists or impeded any pedestrian or vehicular traffic. Under these circumstances, the ordinance as applied unconstitutionally infringed defendant's First Amendment right to freedom of speech. The ordinance as applied was not narrowly tailored to serve the municipality's legitimate and substantial governmental interests in preventing unsafe and threatening speech and maintaining traffic flow. Neither defendant's conduct nor his sign was, or could be perceived to be, intimidating, harassing, threatening, or potentially dangerous to the flow of pedestrian or vehicular traffic.

1488 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of McDonnell v. Lancaster


115677/05


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


13 Misc. 3d 959; 822 N.Y.S.2d 227; 2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2548; 2006 NY Slip Op 26371; 25 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 883; 236 N.Y.L.J. 38



July 31, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Petition denied by, Dismissed by Matter of McDonnell v. Lancaster, 17 Misc. 3d 1101A, 2007 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 6429 (2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner terminated employee commenced a proceeding pursuant to CPLR art. 78 requesting a judgment annulling and vacating a determination by respondent city building commissioner which terminated the employment of the terminated employee as a plumbing inspector. The terminated employee also sought related relief. The city building commissioner claimed that the termination was proper.


OVERVIEW: The terminated employee was provisionally appointed as a plumbing inspector for the city department of buildings. While he was still a provisional employee, his employment was terminated. He claimed that his termination violated Civil Service Law § 75-b because he had reported to his supervisor that he had been offered a bribe while on the job, which he rejected. He added that he received the telephone number for the Department of Investigations (DOI) from the supervisor and reported the bribe incident to the DOI on the same day as he made the report to the supervisor. The city building commissioner in an answer claimed that the terminated employee, as a provisional employee, could be terminated for any reason and that his inadequate job performance constituted a good faith basis for his termination. The trial court found that in the CPLR art. 78 proceeding before it, the trial court had to determine whether the Civil Service Law § 75-b(2) requirement, that of allowing a reasonable time between the report to the supervisor and the report to the DOI, had been met and, if so, whether the city building commissioner had shown a good faith basis for his termination.


OUTCOME: The trial court ruled that the terminated employee's petition be granted to the extent of directing that a hearing be held on his claim and the city building commissioner's response that his termination was proper.


HEADNOTES

Civil Service -- Termination of Employment -- Applicability of "Whistleblower" Statute 

1. A provisionally appointed employee is entitled to assert a claim under the "whistleblower" statute (Civil Service Law § 75-b). The statute broadly extends "whistleblower" protection to "any person holding a position by appointment or employment in the service of a public employer."

Civil Service -- Termination of Employment -- Compliance with Reporting Requirement of "Whistleblower" Statute 

2. Petitioner, a provisionally appointed plumbing inspector for the New York City Department of Buildings whose employment was terminated after he complained to his supervisor about his coworkers' poor job performance and a bribery attempt, was entitled to a hearing pursuant to CPLR 7804 (h) to determine whether his discharge violated the "whistleblower" statute (Civil Service Law § 75-b) only with respect to the bribery attempt. Although both instances of alleged misconduct reported by petitioner fell within the scope of the "whistleblower" statute as conduct "regarding a violation of a law, rule or regulation which violation creates and presents a substantial and specific danger to the public health or safety," petitioner's failure to report the alleged misconduct of his coworkers to a government body in compliance with the statute's reporting requirement required dismissal of that claim. Reporting to both an appointing authority, in this case petitioner's supervisor, and a governmental body is necessary to make a retaliatory discharge claim under the statute. Questions of fact existed as to whether petitioner satisfied the statute's reporting requirement with respect to the bribery attempt.

People v. Osorio
