1490 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Cintron


7862/87


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, BRONX COUNTY


13 Misc. 3d 833; 827 N.Y.S.2d 445; 2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2035; 2006 NY Slip Op 26301



July 25, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Later proceeding at People v. Cintron, 2006 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 14785 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, Dec. 7, 2006)
Later proceeding at, Sub nomine at People v. Glover, 2006 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 15149 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, Dec. 14, 2006)
Later proceeding at, Sub nomine at People v. Jackson, 2007 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 651 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, Jan. 23, 2007)
Affirmed by People v. Cintron, 2007 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12741 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, Dec. 18, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In cases joined together for decision, five petitioners filed applications seeking to be relieved of the obligation to register as sex offenders pursuant to the New York Sex Offender Registration Act (SORA), N.Y. Correct. Law § 168-a et seq. The People opposed the petitioners' applications for exemption from the SORA.


OVERVIEW: Each petitioner was convicted of either unlawful imprisonment, kidnapping, or both. None of the petitioners were accused of sexual conduct with the victims. These offenses were sex offenses under N.Y. Correct. Law § 168-a(2)(a)(i). Petitioners argued that the New York Sex Offender Registration Act (SORA), N.Y. Correct. Law § 168-a et seq., violated equal protection and due process as applied to them because they had not been accused of sexual conduct. The constitutionality of the SORA was considered under rational basis scrutiny. The trial court determined that the SORA bore a rational relationship to a legitimate government objective. Kidnapping, as defined under N.Y. Penal Law § 135.20, and unlawful imprisonment of a child, as defined under N.Y. Penal Law § 135.10, were often precursor offenses to sex offenses against children. It was lawful for the legislature to impose enhanced criminal sanctions for those who created a risk of violence. Thus, it had the power to make defendants who created a risk of sexual abuse subject to registration requirements that were only civil in nature.


OUTCOME: The applications were denied.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Sex Offenders -- Application of Sex Offender Registration Act to Nonparent Convicted of Kidnapping or Unlawful Imprisonment of Minor

The Sex Offender Registration Act (SORA), which subjects a nonparent convicted of kidnapping or unlawful imprisonment of a minor to registration as a sex offender regardless of any evidence of sexual contact or motivation (see Correction Law § 168-a [2] [a] [i]), was not unconstitutional as applied to petitioners. Nor did SORA afford petitioners, who did not have sexual contact with their minor victims but were convicted of kidnapping and/or unlawful imprisonment, the right to an evidentiary hearing to determine whether they should be subject to registration. The purpose of SORA is to conform New York law to the requirements of the Jacob Wetterling Crimes Against Children and Sexually Violent Offender Registration Program (42 USC § 14071, as amended by Megan's Law, Pub L 104-145, 110 US Stat 1345 [JWA]). JWA requires every state to adopt a registration program for sexual predators, sexually violent offenders, and persons convicted of a criminal offense against a minor, and mandates that kidnapping and unlawful imprisonment of a minor by a nonparent be included in any state registration program for federal funding purposes (see 42 USC § 14071 [a] [3] [A] [i], [ii]). Including those offenses as sex offenses subject to registration and notification bears a rational relationship to the legitimate governmental objective of protecting children from sexual abuse in view of the recognized link between those crimes and the risk of sexual abuse.

1492 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Etheridge


2005-318


COUNTY COURT OF NEW YORK, ORANGE COUNTY


13 Misc. 3d 543; 820 N.Y.S.2d 738; 2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2196; 2006 NY Slip Op 26325



July 13, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Judgment entered by People v Etheridge, 12 Misc 3d 1196A, 824 NYS2d 769, 2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2193 (N.Y. County Ct., Aug. 11, 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After defendant pled guilty to third degree rape, he was placed on probation. A violation of probation petition alleged he violated conditions barring him from possessing materials depicting nudity or sexual activity. He moved to dismiss the petition.


OVERVIEW: Defendant allegedly had material showing nudity and sexual activity. He said a probation condition barring possession of such material was overbroad and its enforcement violated his First Amendment rights and denied substantive due process. The condition was not unconstitutional. His claim that the condition was overbroad was limited by the fact he consented to it, so it did not apply to anyone else. His First Amendment rights were limited by the fact he was a sex offender on probation after a felony conviction. The condition was fundamentally rehabilitative. If its prohibition against viewing nudity was overbroad, its prohibition against possessing depictions of sexual conduct was constitutional, directly related to his crime, and related to his rehabilitation. The condition properly limited his First Amendment rights. The images he allegedly had were not in a gray area in which he unsuspectingly found himself. He did not show he was denied substantive due process. Enforcing probation conditions after a claim of non-compliance required court intervention, after which defendant received constitutional and statutory protections.


OUTCOME: Defendant's application to dismiss the petition alleging he violated a condition of probation on the basis that it was overbroad was denied.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Sentence -- Probation -- Special Condition Prohibiting Possession of Materials Depicting Nudity or Sexual Conduct 

The special condition of defendant's probation prohibiting him from possessing materials which depict nudity or actual or simulated sexual conduct of any kind was not unconstitutionally overbroad or violative of his First Amendment and substantive due process rights. Defendant, who pleaded guilty to third degree rape of an underage student at the school where he worked, consented on the record and in writing to that specific condition when he was placed on probation. His First Amendment rights were circumscribed by the fact that he was a sex offender placed on probation after a felony conviction and was the only person affected by the probation condition. Moreover, there was a legitimate reason for the condition. The probation department attached it as a means of ensuring that defendant followed his course of treatment, and its inclusion was fundamentally rehabilitative.

 

1493 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Baker


06-002


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, ESSEX COUNTY


14 Misc. 3d 629; 826 N.Y.S.2d 550; 2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3777; 2006 NY Slip Op 26507



July 13, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at People v. Baker, 2008 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3771 (N.Y. App. Div. 3d Dep't, May 1, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the count in an indictment charging him with second-degree vehicular manslaughter; defendant claimed that Penal Law § 125.12(1) was unconstitutional or otherwise invalid.


OVERVIEW: Specifically, defendant claimed that § 125.12 violated his due process rights, was vague, and created an impermissible mandatory presumption against him. He claimed that the language of "in a manner that causes the death" was unconstitutionally vague, leaving a jury to speculate as to what needed to be proven by the People as part of its prima facie case. The court found that the objected-to language in § 125.12 clearly required defendant's intoxication to be causally connected to a cause of death, which had to be proven by the People beyond a reasonable doubt. It could not be said that § 125.12 was incapable of any valid application. Consequently, defendant failed to meet his burden of establishing that § 125.12 was unconstitutionally vague.


OUTCOME: The motion to dismiss was denied in all respects.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Vehicular Manslaughter -- Statutory Presumption Regarding Intoxication

Penal Law § 125.12 (1), which, in a prosecution for vehicular manslaughter in the second degree, creates a rebuttable presumption that the element of the crime that the defendant caused the death of another person as the result of operating a motor vehicle in an intoxicated condition is established upon proof that the defendant operated the vehicle while unlawfully intoxicated in violation of Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1192 (2)-(4), is not unconstitutional on its face. The statutory presumption is permissive, not mandatory, and it cannot be said that based upon common experience no rational connection exists between the manner in which a motor vehicle is operated and its driver's blood alcohol content. Moreover, permissive presumptions in criminal statutes may not be attacked on their face. Furthermore, the statutory language "in a manner that causes the death" is not unconstitutionally vague. The objected-to language clearly requires a defendant's intoxication to be causally connected to the cause of death, which must be proven by the People beyond a reasonable doubt, and the statute is not incapable of any valid application.

 

1494 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


County of Nassau v. Velasquez


004441/06


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NASSAU COUNTY


13 Misc. 3d 315; 819 N.Y.S.2d 460; 2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1905; 2006 NY Slip Op 26288



July 13, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reversed by, Summary judgment denied by County of Nassau v. Velasquez, 2007 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11082 (N.Y. App. Div. 2d Dep't, Oct. 30, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff county filed a complaint seeking a civil forfeiture of defendant owner's automobile after her daughter pled guilty to driving it while impaired. The owner filed a motion to dismiss asserting that the owner had no reason to know the daughter would drive the vehicle while under the influence of alcohol. The owner's motion was converted to a summary judgment motion and the county filed a summary judgment cross-motion.


OVERVIEW: The owner's daughter was arrested for driving the owner's vehicle while intoxicated. Later, the daughter pled guilty to driving while impaired. The county filed a forfeiture action in which the county sought to seize the owner's vehicle. The owner filed a motion to dismiss, which included an affidavit stating that the owner had no reason to know that the daughter would drive the vehicle under the influence of alcohol. That affirmative defense which the owner filed was known as the "innocent owner" defense and was contained in Nassau County, N.Y., Administrative Code § 8-7.0(g)(4)(f). The trial court ordered that her motion be converted to a motion for summary judgment. The county filed a summary judgment cross-motion asserting that her statement of lack of knowledge was insufficient to raise a proper affirmative defense under the administrative provision. The trial court granted the owner's summary judgment motion upon finding that her simple statement and the lack of rebuttal of it by the county was sufficient to show she was an "innocent owner" entitled to summary judgment pursuant to the administrative provision.


OUTCOME: The trial court granted the owner's summary judgment motion, dismissed the forfeiture complaint, and denied the county's summary judgment cross motion.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Forfeitures and Penalties -- Forfeiture of Proceeds or Instrumentality of Crime -- Innocent Owner Defense

The County's action pursuant to Nassau County Administrative Code § 8-7.0 (g) seeking civil forfeiture of defendant's automobile, which was seized incident to the arrest of defendant's daughter for driving while intoxicated, was dismissed upon defendant's demonstration that she did not know and had no reason to know about the illegal use. Nassau County Administrative Code § 8-7.0 (g) (4) (f) provides an affirmative defense to a forfeiture action where the owner other than the person arrested did not know or had no reason to know that there was a reasonable likelihood that the vehicle would be used illegally. The noncriminal defendant owner bears the burden of pleading and proving that he or she was ignorant of the illegal use. Here, defendant showed by documentary and affidavit proof that she was the owner and had no reason to know the daughter would use the vehicle illegally. This proof was sufficient to shift the burden to the County to demonstrate that there was a reason for defendant to have a belief, which it failed to do.

 

1495 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Wilson v. Dziedzic


2005-2163


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, BROOME COUNTY


13 Misc. 3d 242; 818 N.Y.S.2d 750; 2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1779; 2006 NY Slip Op 26267



July 3, 2006, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owners commenced a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding against defendant town assessors to challenge the propriety of the method used by the assessors to calculate the owners' property assessments. The assessors moved for dismissal of the petition.


OVERVIEW: The owners, who owned recently built or improved homes in the town, claimed that the assessors acted illegally or improperly by using a method of assessment that encompassed two entirely different procedures for determining a property's assessed value, thus creating a disparity between the assessed values of two groups of otherwise similar properties. The court found that a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding was an appropriate vehicle for obtaining judicial review because the owners' challenge was to the constitutionality of the method employed in the assessment involving several properties. Further, the owners were not deemed to have elected their remedies by challenging their individual assessments on the grounds permitted in a Small Claims Assessment Review proceeding under N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law art. 7. Furthermore, the owners proceeding was not untimely. Finally, the town's assessment method was unconstitutional because the owners' newly-reassessed properties were assessed at a greater proportion of actual market value. Therefore, the owners bore a greater proportion of the tax burden than the owners of other, comparable properties that had not been recently built or improved.


OUTCOME: The assessors' motion was denied, and the owners' petition was granted. The assessors were directed to recompute the owners' assessments for the tax year.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Taxation -- Assessment -- Discriminatory Assessment of Recently Improved Properties

1. Respondent Town Assessor acted in an unconstitutionally discriminatory manner by assessing petitioners' newly built or recently improved properties using a method based on actual, current market value while assessing comparable homes that have not been recently built or improved using a more favorable method based on 1965 values, resulting in the owners of newly reassessed properties bearing a greater proportion of the tax burden. While reassessment of properties that have been improved is not itself improper and the process used to value petitioners' properties was not otherwise inherently flawed or improper, the use of one procedure based on current market value to assess only newly improved properties resulted in discriminatory treatment that served no legitimate governmental interest. Moreover, the fact that petitioners had pending claims challenging the propriety of their individual assessments did not preclude them from challenging the method used to reach those assessments in a CPLR article 78 proceeding.

Affirmed in part and modified in part by Kessler v. Hevesi, 45 A.D.3d 474, 846 N.Y.S.2d 56, 2007 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12069 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, Nov. 27, 2007)

1498 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Kaminsky v. Assessor of the Town of Ossining


16809/03


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, WESTCHESTER COUNTY


12 Misc. 3d 1169(A); 820 N.Y.S.2d 843; 2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1488; 2006 NY Slip Op 51120(U)

1500 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. DeJesus


2004NY090562


CRIMINAL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


12 Misc. 3d 913; 819 N.Y.S.2d 442; 2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1247; 2006 NY Slip Op 26202; 235 N.Y.L.J. 104



May 20, 2006, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pleaded guilty to the criminal possession of a controlled substance in the seventh degree, in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 220.03. He subsequently filed a motion under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 440.10 to vacate the judgment of conviction, claiming that he was not advised of the immigration consequences of his guilty plea and that N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 200.50(7) deprived him of his constitutional right to due process.


OVERVIEW: In 1996, the Immigration and Nationality Act was amended to almost mandate deportation of an alien convicted of a crime that related to a controlled substance, as seen in 8 U.S.C.S. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i) and 8 U.S.C.S. § 1228(b)(5) (the amendment). Defendant argued that as deportation was automatic, it was a direct consequence of a conviction and that the trial court should have advised him of that fact. In considering his motion to vacate, the court, after discussing the holdings of other jurisdictions on the issue, adopted the findings of the federal courts and held that under New York law, deportation remained a collateral consequence of a plea despite the amendment and that the failure to warn a defendant that his plea subjected him to deportation was not a ground on which to vacate a conviction. N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 440.10 provided in part that a failure to advise a defendant of deportation did not affect a plea's voluntariness or a conviction's validity. The court also ruled that as deportation was a collateral consequence of a plea, there was no constitutional due process right to be informed of the consequence of deportation, and § 440.10 did not deny any due process right.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to vacate his conviction.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Plea of Guilty -- Notice of Deportation Consequences 

The trial court's failure to advise defendant, an alien who pleaded guilty to criminal possession of a controlled substance in the seventh degree in violation of Penal Law § 220.03, that he was subject to deportation under the 1996 amendment to the Immigration and Nationality Act did not violate defendant's constitutional due process rights or require vacatur of defendant's conviction. A court must inform a defendant only of the direct consequences of a conviction. Mandatory deportation is not a direct consequence, but rather a collateral consequence, as it is imposed by an independent agency and not the court. Moreover, although CPL 220.50 (7) provides that a court must advise certain defendants that a plea of guilty may result in deportation, the statute also provides that the failure to so advise a defendant does not affect the voluntariness of the plea or the validity of the conviction.

1503 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. M.R.


2006NY001986


CRIMINAL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


12 Misc. 3d 671; 814 N.Y.S.2d 517; 2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 894; 2006 NY Slip Op 26154; 235 N.Y.L.J. 98



April 20, 2006, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] DISMISSED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant moved to dismiss for facial insufficiency a misdemeanor information charging him with unlawful assembly and disorderly conduct after gathering with others in a public park.


OVERVIEW: The information alleged that defendant and a group of other men were jumping up and down, jostling each other, and chanting "DDP." The trial court held that there was no allegation that the conduct was violent or that anyone else was present. The group's purpose was found to be to engage in tumultuous conduct. It could not be inferred that the group's purpose was to engage in violent conduct or to cause public alarm. Further, there was no allegation that defendant intended to act to cause public alarm. The allegation that "DDP" was a gang chant did not mean that it was an incitement to imminent lawless action or that it contained fighting words. The First and Fourteenth Amendments did not permit the exercise of the right to assembly to be made criminal simply because it was annoying. Thus, the unlawful assembly charge failed. The disorderly conduct charge also failed. Although defendant was in a public park, there were no allegations that defendant's conduct annoyed or interfered with anyone. Further, there was no allegation that defendant persisted in the conduct after warnings, or that his conduct created a risk of disorder. Finally, defendant's intent could not be determined.


OUTCOME: Defendant's motion to dismiss both counts of the accusatory instrument was granted.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Unlawful Assembly -- Sufficiency of Information

1 A misdemeanor information that charged defendant with unlawful assembly in violation of Penal Law § 240.10 and alleged that he was observed in a public park with about 30 other people jumping up and down, pushing against each other, and loudly chanting, including chanting the letters "DDP," a known gang chant, was facially insufficient. There were no facts stated in the information from which it could have been inferred that the group's purpose was to engage in violent conduct, since they were neither acting violently, nor making statements which incited violence. There were also no facts from which it could be inferred that their purpose was to cause public alarm, because there were no allegations that any members of the public were present. Moreover, the group's chanting of the phrase "DDP" did not bring the alleged conduct within the statute, as the information did not allege that the words "DDP" were an "incitement to imminent lawless action" or "fighting words" which may properly be regulated.

Crimes -- Disorderly Conduct -- Sufficiency of Information

2. A misdemeanor information that charged defendant with disorderly conduct in violation of Penal Law § 240.20 (1) and alleged that he was observed in a public park with about 30 other people jumping up and down, pushing against each other, and loudly chanting, including chanting the letters "DDP," a known gang chant, was facially insufficient. Although the information alleged that defendant acted in a concededly public place, there were no allegations that anyone was present other than defendant, his companions, and the arresting officer, and therefore there was no allegation that defendant annoyed, disturbed, or interfered with any member of the public. Moreover, the information failed to allege that defendant's conduct actually annoyed anyone, that defendant persisted in the conduct after warnings by the police or others, or that his conduct created even a risk of disorder.

1505 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Festa v. New York City Dept. of Consumer Affairs


108724/2005


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


12 Misc. 3d 466; 820 N.Y.S.2d 452; 2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 682; 2006 NY Slip Op 26125; 235 N.Y.L.J. 67



April 3, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Affirmed in part and modified in part by Festa v New York City Dept. of Consumer Affairs, 37 AD3d 343, 830 NYS2d 133, 2007 NY App Div LEXIS 2019 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, Feb. 22, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, several dancers and a dance club (dancers), sought a preliminary injunction to prevent defendants, the New York City Planning Commission, the New York City Department of Buildings, and the New York City Department of Consumer Affairs (City), from enforcing New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 20-360 (the cabaret law) and the New York City Zoning Resolution. The City cross-moved for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The dancers argued that social dancing was expression protected under the New York Constitution and that the cabaret law and the Resolution infringed upon their right of expression. The trial court held that the cabaret law was constitutional, on its face and as applied to the dancers, as social dancing was not recognized as expressive communication either by the First Amendment or N.Y. Const. art. I, § 8. The cabaret law was reasonably related to public safety and welfare, and, assuming that the dancers had a due process interest that the cabaret law restricted, the dancers failed to show that the cabaret law was so arbitrary that it violated substantive due process. The Resolution, as applied, was not arbitrary and capricious and did not deprive the dancers of due process of law under N.Y. Const. art. I, § 6. Social dancing was not constitutionally protected as expressive conduct, and the Resolution was a reasonable exercise of the City's police power and bore a substantial relationship to the health, safety, and welfare of New Yorkers. Finally, as it was debatable whether dancing should have been restricted to Use Group 12, the City's judgment was not arbitrary or irrational.


OUTCOME: The dancers' motion for a preliminary injunction was denied. The City's cross-motion was granted, and the complaint was dismissed.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Freedom of Speech -- Regulation of Participatory Social Dancing -- Validity of Cabaret Licensing Law and Related Zoning Provisions

New York City's Cabaret Law (Administrative Code of City of NY § 20-360) and related zoning provisions are not unconstitutional to the extent that they regulate the circumstances under which participatory social dancing is permitted in eating and drinking establishments and the location of those establishments throughout New York City. Participatory recreational/social dancing as an activity does not rise to the level of protected expressive conduct under the State Constitution (NY Const, art I, § 8). Plaintiffs failed to rebut the presumption of the constitutionality of the City's Cabaret Law. A licensing requirement for eating and drinking establishments that offer participatory social dancing is not arbitrary. The Cabaret Law is reasonably related to public safety and welfare. The City has a legitimate public interest in regulating bars, clubs, and restaurants and the circumstances under which they may offer patrons the opportunity to dance. The fact that an establishment may conform to building and fire codes does not assure that its space is adequate or appropriate for social dancing. Furthermore, application of the zoning provisions that limit participatory social dancing to certain commercial districts is not arbitrary and capricious. The zoning restrictions constitute a reasonable exercise of the police power to control noise and congestion and bear a substantial relationship to the health, safety and general welfare of the public.

1507 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Barton


05/0017


COUNTY COURT OF NEW YORK, MONROE COUNTY


12 Misc. 3d 322; 816 N.Y.S.2d 853; 2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 586; 2006 NY Slip Op 26109



March 10, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal granted by People v Barton, 6 NY3d 892, 850 NE2d 674, 817 NYS2d 627, 2006 NY LEXIS 1890 (2006)
Motion granted by People v Barton, 7 NY3d 855, 857 NE2d 1125, 824 NYS2d 594, 2006 NY LEXIS 3240 (2006)
Motion granted by People v Barton, 7 NY3d 858, 857 NE2d 1128, 824 NYS2d 596, 2006 NY LEXIS 3299 (2006)
Affirmed by People v Barton, 8 NY3d 70, 861 NE2d 75, 828 NYS2d 260, 2006 NY LEXIS 3709 (N.Y., Dec. 19, 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was ticketed for aggressive panhandling in violation of Rochester, N.Y., Municipal Code § 44-4(H). Before trial in a lower court in New York, defendant moved to dismiss the accusatory instrument. The lower court granted defendant's motion and dismissed the charges pending against defendant. The People of the State of New York appealed.


OVERVIEW: Rochester, N.Y., Municipal Code § 44-4(H) forbid the approaching of vehicles on the street by panhandlers for a contribution. Defendant, who allegedly walked into traffic on an exit ramp to a city street and solicited funds from passing motorists, successfully argued in the court below that § 44-4(H) was unconstitutional. On appeal, the court found that the governmental interest at issue as to § 44-4(H), prohibiting interference with the free flow of vehicular traffic and promoting traffic safety, was a compelling interest and directly promoted by the regulation. Further, while some incidental burden existed on First Amendment freedoms, the governmental interest at issue was unrelated to the limited ban on free expression. Any incidental restriction on alleged First Amendment freedoms was no greater than was essential to the furtherance of that interest. Therefore, the ordinance, as written, was content neutral. Additionally, § 44-4(H), as written, was sufficiently narrowly tailored to pass constitutional muster. Moreover, the ordinance clearly left open ample alternative channels of communication. Finally, the ordinance was not overly broad. Thus, the ordinance was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed, and the case was remanded for further proceedings.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Municipal Ordinance Prohibiting "Aggressive" Panhandling Directed at Occupants of Motor Vehicles 

A municipal ordinance that prohibits various forms of "aggressive" panhandling by persons approaching cars stopped in traffic is not unconstitutionally overbroad. Although the otherwise content-neutral ordinance implicates some constitutionally protected expressive activity, it was sufficiently narrowly tailored to address legitimate public safety and traffic concerns while at the same time protecting the First Amendment rights of persons engaged in the nonaggressive solicitation of funds or the mere communication of thoughts and ideas. The municipality acted within its authority to limit words or acts of solicitation directed at the occupants of motor vehicles, an activity that presents a threat to vehicular and pedestrian safety.

1509 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Coleman


6715/04


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


11 Misc. 3d 1019; 812 N.Y.S.2d 857; 2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 425; 2006 NY Slip Op 26084; 235 N.Y.L.J. 58



March 8, 2006, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court sentenced defendant to a one-year conditional discharge for a conviction of criminal contempt in the second degree after defendant entered a plea of guilty to the charge. As a condition of the conditional discharge and the order of protection issued, defendant agreed to a provision prohibiting him from entering New York City, New York, for three years unless he made an application to the court.


OVERVIEW: Defendant entered a plea of guilty to criminal contempt in the first degree and criminal contempt in the second degree. Defendant was a Pennsylvania resident and expressed to the court that he did not want to be placed on probation in New York. The court agreed to dismiss the felony count of criminal contempt in the first degree and to release defendant on a conditional discharge if defendant completed a batterer intervention program. In that event, the court would give defendant a conditional discharge along with an order of protection as defendant agreed to stay out of New York City, where the victim lived. After defendant completed a batterer intervention program, the court released defendant on the conditional discharge. The court found that the imposed condition did not unlawfully impinge on defendant's constitutional right to travel. The restriction on travel was valid under N.Y. Penal Law § 65.10 as it ensured that defendant would be law-abiding and assisted defendant in his rehabilitation while taking into account defendant's concerns and also provided for the victim's safety under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 530.13. Additionally, defendant received the benefit of the bargain.


OUTCOME: The court determined that the travel restriction imposed on defendant as a special condition of the conditional discharge and order of protection was lawful.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Sentence -- Conditional Discharge -- Restricted Travel

In sentencing defendant, a Pennsylvania resident, to a one-year conditional discharge upon a criminal contempt conviction, the court did not deny defendant his constitutional right to travel by prohibiting him from entering New York City for three years unless the court lifted the restriction upon defendant's request due to an emergency. The fundamental right to travel is not absolute. Defendant, as a convicted criminal, did not possess an absolute right to travel. Under Penal Law § 65.10 (1), a court may impose fundamentally rehabilitative conditions on a conditional discharge sentence reasonably necessary to ensure that defendant will lead a law-abiding life or to assist him to do so. The proscription on travel helped defendant adhere to the order of protection and lead a law-abiding life. It was fundamentally rehabilitative because the area defendant was not allowed to enter was reasonably related to where the offense occurred. Defendant was entitled to request relief from the restriction in an emergency. Further, CPL 530.13 permits a court to place additional conditions on a defendant served with a protective order which reasonably relate to protection of a victim. Here, the travel restrictions, as a specific condition of the protective order, ensured no contact. Finally, defendant agreed to the restriction on travel in exchange for a nonfelony conviction and nonprobationary sentence.

Dalva v Pataki

1511 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hendrix


3668/03


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


12 Misc. 3d 447; 820 N.Y.S.2d 411; 2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 595; 2006 NY Slip Op 26111; 34 Media L. Rep. 1548



February 28, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at People v. Hendrix, 2009 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5023 (N.Y. App. Div. 2d Dep't, June 16, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In two criminal prosecutions, the defendant in each case issued a subpoena to private entities, each of which moved to quash the subpoenas.


OVERVIEW: One defendant sought outtakes from a documentary, and another defendant sought materials from an interview with a complainant in his case. Both sought these for possible impeachment material. The subpoenaed entities were not government agents and were covered by the N.Y. Civ. Rights Law § 79-h privilege. Defendants had no constitutional right to the material. Neither defendant said they would seek to introduce evidence from the subpoenaed documents. They only wanted to see if they contained inconsistent witness statements. There was no constitutional or common law right to discovery, which was entirely governed by statute, and they did not show a compulsory process right to compel a non-governmental agency to turn over prior witness statements, nor did they show a confrontation right to the subpoenaed material. N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law art. 240 did not allow discovery from non-governmental agents. Witness statements possessed by civilian non-party individuals were not constitutionally required to be turned over to defendants. They did not show the material was possessed by the government or was exculpatory, so quashing the subpoenas was not a Brady violation.


OUTCOME: The motions to quash the subpoenas were granted, and the subpoenas were quashed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Disclosure -- Materials in Custody of News Media 

Defendants were not entitled to subpoena outtakes of a police reality television program and other materials compiled as part of a documentary program in order to engage in a "fishing expedition" to determine if the subpoenaed materials contained inconsistent statements of prosecution witnesses for the potential use as impeachment material. The subpoenaed materials were privileged from disclosure under Civil Rights Law § 79-h. Application of the statute to quash the subpoenas did not violate any of defendants' constitutional rights. Defendants had no constitutional right to discover materials in the hands of nongovernmental nonparty civilian witnesses. In addition, there is no due process right to prior witness statements in the custody of the news media, nor had defendants established any Sixth Amendment compulsory process right to compel a nongovernmental agency to turn over prior witness statements. Furthermore, defendants failed to establish a confrontation right to the subpoenaed materials, and the quashing of the subpoenas did not constitute a Brady violation.

Ulster County Dep't of Soc. Servs. v. Tracy Lynn V.


NN-3458-05


FAMILY COURT OF NEW YORK, ULSTER COUNTY


2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4111; 235 N.Y.L.J. 38



February 27, 2006 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner county social services department (department) filed a petition against respondent mother, alleging that she had neglected her three children by the infliction of excessive corporal punishment. The mother moved to dismiss certain allegations on the basis of res judicata or collateral estoppel.


OVERVIEW: The current neglect petition was based on incidents occurring November 17, 2005, May 2, 2005, April 20, 2004, and January 27, 2004. The two 2004 incidents had already been the subject of a petition against the mother, which concluded with an Order of Adjournment in Contemplation of Dismissal (ACD) providing that the case was adjourned on specified terms and conditions, with a view to ultimate dismissal in the interests of justice. The department filed a petition alleging violation of the ACD, but an order of dismissal of the petition was signed and the dismissal was without prejudice. The court determined that the allegations regarding the two 2004 incidents were subject to dismissal because (1) the prior petition was dismissed in the interests of justice under Family Ct Act § 1039(f) and could not be restored since the adjournment period expired and the time to restore the matter to the calendar had passed, and (2) the mother made an admission in the prosecution of the prior petition, but the department was not allowed to revisit the allegations in the prior petition. However, regarding the incident of May 2, 2005, the dismissal without prejudice had no preclusive effect.


OUTCOME: The court granted in part and denied in part the motion to dismiss. The court dismissed the allegations based on incidents occurring on April 20, 2004, and January 27, 2004.

1517 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Bezjak


2005NY011706


CRIMINAL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


11 Misc. 3d 424; 812 N.Y.S.2d 829; 2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 33; 2006 NY Slip Op 26006



January 9, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at Matter of Bezjak v. New York City Police Department, 2008 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 9431 (N.Y. Sup. Ct., July 17, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants moved to dismiss the charges against them for parading without a permit and disorderly conduct, violations of New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 10-110(c) and N.Y. Penal Law § 240.20(6); defendants claimed, inter alia, that the ordinance was facially insufficient and unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were arrested by the police for participating in a bicycle procession known as a Critical Mass. The court held that the city's parade permitting scheme, New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 10-110 and New York City, N.Y., R. tit. 38, § 19-01 et seq., was overbroad and it was unconstitutional on its face. Although the city's parade permit scheme was a content-neutral time, place, and manner regulation of the use of city streets, it gave no guidance as to the minimum number of persons that constituted a parade or procession requiring a permit. It vested unbridled discretion in a government official over whether to permit or deny expressive activity. Another constitutional infirmity of the city's permit scheme was the absence of a requirement of mental culpability. Although defendants could not be found guilty of violating N.Y. Penal Law § 240.20(6), because they were not ordered to disburse, they were participants in a group of at least 50 bicyclists that rode their bikes in a manner that obstructed the flow of vehicular traffic. Consequently, they were guilty of violating § 240.20(5).


OUTCOME: The counts charging a violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 240.20(6) and New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 10-110 were dismissed; defendants were found guilty of violating N.Y. Penal Law § 240.20(5).


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Courts -- Law of the Case -- Constitutionality of Municipal Parade Permit Scheme 

1. In defendants' criminal prosecution for parading without a permit in violation of New York City's parade permit scheme (Administrative Code of City of NY § 10-110; 38 RCNY 19-02), the trial court was not bound under the law of the case doctrine by the ruling of the motion court on the ordinance's constitutionality. The motion court, in denying defendants' motion to dismiss the charges as constitutionally defective, found that the City's parade permit scheme was constitutional as an appropriate content-neutral regulation of the time, place and manner of all uses of the city streets by individuals moving along the roadway as a group on foot, bicycles, or in vehicles. However, the existence of exceptional circumstances warranted reconsideration of the constitutional issues raised by defendants. The trial court heard the testimony of numerous witnesses that highlighted deficiencies in the parade permit scheme. Furthermore, the motion court did not address the specific issues of whether the statute was overbroad for failing to adequately define what constitutes a parade or procession and providing no guidance as to what minimum number of participants may implicate the statute. Nor did the motion court address the potentially chilling effect upon freedom of expression inherent in a statute that purports to impose strict criminal liability upon persons who may join a procession without being aware that a required permit had not been issued.

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Parading without Permit 

2. Charges against defendants for parading without a permit in violation of New York City's parade permit scheme (Administrative Code of City of NY § 10-110; 38 RCNY 19-02) stemming from their participation in a monthly "Critical Mass" spontaneous bike event involving over 50 bicyclists were dismissed as constitutionally defective. The otherwise content-neutral parade permit scheme, which requires a permit for "any march, motorcade, caravan, promenade, foot or bicycle race, or similar event of any kind upon any street or roadway," is not narrowly tailored to meet the City's significant interest in keeping the streets open and available for movement, and is thus unconstitutionally overbroad on its face. The permit scheme gives no guidance as to the minimum number of persons that constitutes a parade or procession and otherwise vests the Police Commissioner with unfettered discretion to determine when any particular event may be found to fall within the amorphous definition of a parade or procession. Furthermore, a mental culpability requirement (see Penal Law § 15.15 [2]) must be read into the parade permit scheme in order to avoid the potentially chilling effect upon freedom of expression inherent in a statute that purports to impose strict criminal liability upon persons who may join a procession without being aware that a required permit had not been issued.

Jennings v. N.Y. City Council


111597/05


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


10 Misc. 3d 1073(A); 814 N.Y.S.2d 890; 2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 72; 2006 NY Slip Op 50052(U); 235 N.Y.L.J. 15

1519 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Kimberley's A Day Spa, Ltd. v. Hevesi


5128-05


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, ALBANY COUNTY


11 Misc. 3d 954; 810 N.Y.S.2d 616; 2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 374; 2006 NY Slip Op 26075



January 5, 2006, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner business challenged the determination by respondent New York State Comptroller that the unclaimed amount of an expired gift certificate was abandoned property after five years despite the earlier expiration of the certificate, and that the business was required to report as abandoned property and pay over the entire unclaimed amount of the expired gift certificate notwithstanding any claim of profit from the sale of the certificate.


OVERVIEW: The business argued that in making the determinations the Comptroller improperly interpreted N.Y. Aband. Prop. Law § 1315 so as to disregard the business's legally binding gift certificate contract. The business also argued that the statute was interpreted in an unconstitutional manner in that the determination deprived the business of property without just compensation and without due process of law, and that N.Y. Aband. Prop. Law § 1315 was unconstitutional on its face. The court ruled that the only interpretation that provided consistent meaning to both N.Y. Aband. Prop. Law § 1315 and N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 396-i was the conclusion reached by the Comptroller that although gift certificates could expire, they were nonetheless deemed abandoned property after five years. The interpretation that the unredeemed value of gift certificates escheat to the State after five years even if the certificate had expired was consistent with the language of N.Y. Aband. Prop. Law § 1315. Also, the business's claim that N.Y. Aband. Prop. Law § 1315 was unconstitutional, or was interpreted in an unconstitutional manner by the Comptroller, was without merit.


OUTCOME: The Comptroller was awarded judgment declaring that the unclaimed amount of an expired gift certificate was deemed abandoned property after five years despite the earlier expiration of the certificate, and the business was required to report as abandoned property and pay over the entire unclaimed amount of the expired gift certificate notwithstanding any claim of profit from the sale of the certificate.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Property -- Abandoned Property -- Expired Gift Certificates -- Constitutionality of Abandoned Property Law § 1315 

Abandoned Property Law § 1315, which provides that any unclaimed amount representing unredeemed gift certificates sold after December 31, 1983 and remaining unclaimed by the owner for five years shall be deemed abandoned property and paid to the State Comptroller, is not unconstitutional on its face or as applied to petitioner with respect to any of its unredeemed gift certificates that expired prior to the end of the five-year period. As Abandoned Property Law § 1315 was in effect at the time petitioner was incorporated, it did not deprive petitioner of constitutionally protected rights to the benefit of its gift certificate contracts because those rights were subject to section 1315. Nor was petitioner deprived of private property without just compensation and without due process of law, as petitioner did not have a property interest in the unredeemed gift certificates because the statute declared that the value of those certificates escheated to the State.

 

1520 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Namer


2005NY047941


CRIMINAL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


11 Misc. 3d 409; 812 N.Y.S.2d 824; 2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 15; 2006 NY Slip Op 26001; 235 N.Y.L.J. 8



January 4, 2006, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, who had been charged with violations of N.Y. Penal Law § 240.20(5), disorderly conduct-obstruction of pedestrian/vehicular traffic, and New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 10-110, parading without a permit, sought reconsideration of the holding by the court in People v. Charity James. Defendants had participated in a "Critical Mass" bicycle event in Manhattan.


OVERVIEW: Defendants sought reconsideration of the Charity James decision, which upheld the constitutionality of New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 10-110, in light of a recent decision by the United States Court of Appeals for the Sixth Circuit. The court initially noted that § 10-110 had to be read in conjunction with New York City, N.Y., R. tit. 38, ch. 19 as part of the parade permitting scheme. In examining the Michigan ordinance at issue in the Sixth Circuit case, the court found that that ordinance required speakers to apply for a permit 30 days in advance but New York City, N.Y., R. tit. 38, § 19-04(c)(iv) and (v) permitted applications to be filed within 36 hours or less in exigent circumstances. A provision in the Michigan ordinance was overly broad in its definition of a special event while New York City, N.Y., R. tit. 38, § 19-02(a) included specific examples of the type of events requiring a permit. The Michigan ordinance also differed because it contained a strict liability provision as opposed to the New York ordinance, which was only a violation and not a crime. Based on those critical distinctions, the court declined to reconsider its prior holding in Charity James.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendants' request to reconsider its holding in a prior case.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Parading without Permit 

Defendants were lawfully charged with the violation of parading without a permit in violation of New York City's parade permit ordinance (Administrative Code of City of NY § 10-110) stemming from their participation in a monthly "Critical Mass" spontaneous bike event in Manhattan. The ordinance, which requires a permit on at least 36 hours' prior notice for "any march, motorcade, caravan, promenade, foot or bicycle race, or similar event of any kind upon any street or roadway" (see 38 RCNY 19-02 [a]), is not unconstitutional on its face. The content-neutral ordinance, by including specific examples of the types of events requiring a permit, alleviates vagueness problems and provides sufficient protection against small groups of innocuous pedestrians or cyclists being targeted. The parade permit scheme was intended to encompass large events such as "Critical Mass" bike rides, whether such events are spontaneous or organized. Requiring permits for marches or parades proceeding on public rights-of-way constitutes a legitimate exercise of governmental authority.

 

1521 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Butler


13019C-2005


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, BRONX COUNTY


11 Misc. 3d 547; 812 N.Y.S.2d 275; 2005 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2928; 2005 NY Slip Op 25550; 235 N.Y.L.J. 13



December 22, 2005, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion for a jury trial in his criminal action, wherein he was charged with two Class B misdemeanors of attempted assault in the third degree, attempted criminal mischief in the fourth degree, as well as harassment in the second degree. His case had been transferred from the New York City Criminal Court, Bronx County, to the newly-formed court wherein the jury request was filed.


OVERVIEW: A new criminal division of the court was established, pursuant to N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 22, § 142.2(b), and a number of cases, including the instant one, were transferred. The case was to be adjudicated pursuant to the same substantive and procedural law as it would have been in the originating court, pursuant to N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 22, § 142.3. The original charges against defendant, which included two Class A misdemeanors, were reduced to Class B misdemeanors, as well as a harassment violation. Upon the People's announcement of their readiness for trial, defendant filed a motion for a jury trial. The court found that the newly formed court was a "superior court" and as such, there was no jury trial right under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 340.40(2). Further, the transfer order was authorized by N.Y. Const. art. VI, § 30 because no rights of defendant were affected. Defendant was not entitled to a jury trial under U.S. Const. amend. VI, XIV, and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 2 only provided for jury trials for "serious" offenses. Also, there was no statutory authority under N.Y. Civ. Rights Law art. 2, § 12 and no equal protection or home rule violation.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion for a jury trial.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Right to Jury Trial -- Misdemeanor Case Transferred to Criminal Division of Supreme Court, Bronx County

1. Defendant, whose class B misdemeanor prosecution was transferred from the New York City Criminal Court, Bronx County, to the newly formed Criminal Division of the Supreme Court in Bronx County, was not entitled to a trial by jury. Supreme Court is a superior court, and CPL 340.40 (2), which requires that misdemeanor defendants tried in non-New York City local criminal courts be accorded jury trials, does not apply by its terms to the Supreme Court, Criminal Division, and cannot be a predicate for entitlement to a jury trial. Although the CPL is silent as to whether a defendant who is charged with a misdemeanor and prosecuted by way of a misdemeanor information in Supreme Court is to be tried by a jury or the court, a rule of the Chief Administrative Judge provides that every case transferred to the Criminal Division is to be adjudicated "subject to the same substantive and procedural law as would have applied to it had it not been transferred." (22 NYCRR 142.3.) Had defendant, who was charged with two misdemeanors for which the authorized term of imprisonment was not more than six months, not been transferred to the Criminal Division, he would have been tried in a bench trial in the New York City Criminal Court pursuant to CPL 340.40 (2).

Crimes -- Right to Jury Trial -- Misdemeanor Case Transferred to Criminal Division of Supreme Court, Bronx County

2. Defendant, whose class B misdemeanor prosecution was transferred from the New York City Criminal Court, Bronx County, to the newly formed Criminal Division of the Supreme Court in Bronx County, where it was to be adjudicated in a bench trial pursuant to 22 NYCRR 142.3 and CPL 340.40 (2), did not have a right to a jury trial under either the United States or New York Constitution (US Const 6th Amend; NY Const, art I, § 2). He was charged with two misdemeanors that carried a maximum penalty of three months' incarceration, and a defendant charged with offenses for which the maximum authorized term of imprisonment does not exceed six months is not entitled to a jury trial. The added possible penalty of a maximum $ 500 fine was not so grave as to elevate the offenses from the "petty" category to the "serious" category.

Crimes -- Right to Jury Trial -- Misdemeanor Case Transferred to Criminal Division of Supreme Court, Bronx County

3. The failure to provide defendant with the right to a jury trial after his class B misdemeanor prosecution was transferred from the New York City Criminal Court, Bronx County, to the newly formed Criminal Division of the Supreme Court in Bronx County, where it was to be adjudicated in a bench trial pursuant to 22 NYCRR 142.3 and CPL 340.40 (2), did not violate his right to equal protection under either the United States or New York Constitution (US Const 14th Amend; NY Const, art I, § 11). Statistics showing that a higher percentage of minorities reside in New York City than in other areas of the state did not prove that the denial of jury trials to defendants in New York City charged with petty crimes while affording jury trials outside of New York City for such crimes was discriminatory. Rather, bench trials were required for defendants in New York City charged with petty crimes in an effort to accommodate the unusually heavy demands of the New York City criminal justice system.

1524 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Green v. DeMarco


2005/09951


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, MONROE COUNTY


11 Misc. 3d 451; 812 N.Y.S.2d 772; 2005 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2776; 2005 NY Slip Op 25528



December 12, 2005, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff prosecutor sought declaratory and injunctive relief, collaterally attacking a ruling by defendant criminal court judge dismissing drunk driving charges after excluding breathalyzer test results. The judge had held that establishing a foundation for the admissibility of such results by certifications of calibration and reference solution testing violated the Confrontation Clause, despite their characterization as business records.


OVERVIEW: State regulations required that breathalyzer machines be properly maintained and tested, and that reference solutions used in blood alcohol testing be double-checked with each use of the machines. These records of reliability had traditionally been admitted as business records under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 4518 to establish a foundation for the admission of breathalyzer results in drunk driving prosecutions. The judge had dismissed such a prosecution after holding the breathalyzer results inadmissible based on a further holding that the certifications were not admissible as business records because they had been prepared with an eye to use in future criminal trials and were therefore impermissibly testimonial. The court declared that admission of the documents for foundational purposes did not violate the Confrontation Clause. Breathalyzer testing did not always result in prosecutions. It often exonerated drivers. Therefore, the documents certifying their proper functioning were not prepared solely to aid in criminal prosecution, and were entirely admissible as business records.


OUTCOME: The court denied the request for injunctive relief. In a subsequent ruling, it entered summary judgment in favor of the prosecutor on his request for declaratory relief.


HEADNOTES

Declaratory Judgments -- When Remedy Appropriate -- Collateral Challenge by District Attorney to Ruling of Local Criminal Court Precluding Use of Certain Business Records in Prosecutions for Driving while Intoxicated 

1. Declaratory relief was available to the District Attorney to collaterally attack the ruling of a local criminal court that precluded the use of government-generated business records to establish the requisite foundation for the admission of breathalyzer test results in prosecutions for driving while intoxicated (Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1192 [2]) as being violative of the Confrontation Clause of the US Constitution. A declaratory judgment action was appropriate because the challenged ruling was likely to recur with similar results, and, if incorrect, could significantly obstruct the administration of justice. Moreover, no factual issues were required to be determined, and the action would not delay any pending criminal trial. Furthermore, no other remedy was available to the District Attorney, since an appeal from a trial order of dismissal resulting from the challenged ruling (see CPL 450.20 [2]) would violate the constitutional protection against double jeopardy.

Crimes -- Right of Confrontation -- Admissibility of Breath Test Foundational Documents 

2. Government-generated documents pertaining to the servicing of a breath testing device and the State Police certification of the reference solution used to calibrate the device constitute nontestimonial business records that may be used to establish the requisite foundation for the admission of breathalyzer test results in a prosecution for driving while intoxicated (Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1192 [2]) without violating the Confrontation Clause of the US Constitution. The subject documents primarily serve legitimate business purposes and do not implicate core concerns of the Confrontation Clause, even if the documents have an incidental litigation purpose. Moreover, the records are neutral in character, relating only to the operation of the breath test instrument and the reference solution used to calibrate it. Furthermore, the admission of the documents would not establish any of the elements of driving while intoxicated.

 

1525 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Rake v. Spall


F03131-00/04A


FAMILY COURT OF NEW YORK, MONROE COUNTY


11 Misc. 3d 364; 806 N.Y.S.2d 843; 2005 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2778; 2005 NY Slip Op 25530



December 7, 2005, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent support obligor moved to vacate orders of the court directing his incarceration for failure to comply with orders that he make payments to petitioner obligee. He argued that the court had lacked personal jurisdiction over him when the orders were entered, because he had not been personally served with the summons, petition, or notice of proceedings.


OVERVIEW: The obligor failed to appear at a proceeding regarding his noncompliance with an order of support, so the court ordered his incarceration. The obligor argued that there was no personal jurisdiction empowering the court to order his incarceration, because he had not been personally served with the enforcement summons and complaint. The court held that service on the obligor's mother, with whom he lived, concededly a person of suitable age and discretion, was the equivalent of personal service on the obligor within the meaning of N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 427 and 453. The additional act of mailing the documents to the address where the obligor lived was simply an extra safeguard. It was clear that due process had been more than satisfied, and that the obligor had actual notice of all the proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court denied the obligor's motion in its entirety.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Parent and Child -- Support -- Notice Required for Entry of Default Order of Incarceration 

Respondent was lawfully incarcerated based upon his default in appearing for a child support order violation proceeding. Delivery of the summons and petition with default warnings to respondent's mother at his residence, together with mailing, satisfied statutory notice requirements for the entry of a default order of incarceration (see Family Ct Act §§ 427, 453 [c]; see also CPLR 308 [2]). The method of service gave Family Court personal jurisdiction over respondent and afforded respondent sufficient due process. Proof of actual notice was not constitutionally required before incarceration could be ordered.

Albany Preparatory Charter School v. City of Albany


2285-05


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, ALBANY COUNTY


10 Misc. 3d 870; 805 N.Y.S.2d 818; 2005 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2678; 2005 NY Slip Op 25505



November 30, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
Affirmed in part and modified in part by, Remitted by Albany Preparatory Charter School v City of Albany, 31 AD3d 870, 818 NYS2d 651, 2006 NY App Div LEXIS 8618 (N.Y. App. Div. 3d Dep't, July 6, 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent, the Albany Board of Zoning Appeals (Board) denied a request filed by petitioner applicant for a use variance, area variance, and parking lot permit to construct and operate a charter school. The applicant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Respondents, all city entities and the mayor (city), moved to dismiss the applicant's appeal, claiming that its petition failed to state a cause of action and that a necessary party had not been joined. The court first found that Albany, N.Y., City Zoning Ordinance §§ 375-71 and 375-73, which did not list a school as an allowable principal, accessory or special permit use in the C-1 or C-2 districts, denied educational institutions the opportunity to apply for special use permits and were therefore unconstitutional. Second, Albany, N.Y., City Zoning Ordinance §§ 375-26(B)(1), 375-26(B)(2)(a) and 375-185 were also unconstitutional as applied to the instant application insofar as they required the issuance of a variance for the applicant's proposed educational use of the property and condition the issuance of such variance upon a showing of need or hardship. Further, the city failed to show a significant impact on traffic congestion, property values or municipal services, and had not overcome the presumption that a public school had a beneficial effect on the community.


OUTCOME: The determination by the Board was annulled and the Board was directed to issue a special permit for the use of the property as a school, subject only to reasonable conditions and safeguards, within 60 days of entry of this decision and judgment.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Special Use Permit -- Unconstitutionality of Ordinances Excluding Educational Uses in Commercial District 

1. City of Albany Zoning Ordinance §§ 375-71 and 375-73, which do not list a school as an allowable principal, accessory or special permit use in C-1 (neighborhood commercial district) or C-2 (highway commercial district) districts, are unauthorized and therefore unconstitutional. The ordinances deny educational institutions, which are presumed to have a beneficial effect on a community, the opportunity to apply for special use permits.

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning - Variance -- Unconstitutionality of Ordinances as Applied to Variance Application for Educational Use 

2. City of Albany Zoning Ordinance § 375-26 (B) (1) and (2) (a) and § 375-185 are unconstitutional as applied to petitioner's application for a use variance, area variance and parking lot permit to construct and operate a charter school in a commercial district, insofar as they require the issuance of a variance for petitioner's proposed use of the property and condition the issuance of the variance upon a showing of need or hardship. There is no legal basis to require a variance for an educational use of the property.

Parties -- Necessary Parties -- Notice to Attorney General -- Declaration of Unconstitutionality of Zoning Ordinances 

3. A proceeding in which the court declared municipal zoning ordinances unconstitutional as applied to petitioner's application for a variance for an educational use was not dismissed for failure to join the Attorney General as a necessary party (CPLR 1012 [b]). The declaration did not involve the general constitutionality of a state statute.

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Variance for Educational Use 

4. A determination of the Board of Zoning Appeals denying petitioners' application for variances and a parking lot permit to construct and operate a charter school on commercial property that previously had been used as an elementary school for over 70 years was arbitrary and capricious and not supported by competent evidence in the record. The property is located in a neighborhood comprised of diverse land uses, one block from a major thoroughfare lined with commercial establishments. The zoning ordinance restrictions of building height, front and rear yard setbacks, maximum lot coverage and parking were reasonably addressed in the proposal. Educational institutions are presumed to have a beneficial effect on a community, and zoning boards must make every effort to accommodate these uses. Respondents failed to overcome the presumption in that they presented no evidence of a significant impact on traffic congestion, property values or municipal services.

 

1528 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Bostic


2004SU31912


DISTRICT COURT OF NEW YORK, FIRST DISTRICT, SUFFOLK COUNTY


10 Misc. 3d 775; 807 N.Y.S.2d 280; 2005 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2697; 2005 NY Slip Op 25510



November 30, 2005, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to a plea agreement, a "stay away" permanent order of protection was entered against defendant, preventing him from having any contact with the victim. At the time of sentencing, defense counsel requested that the order of protection contain the specific addresses from which defendant was to stay away.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that in light of the fact that defendant and the victim were strangers, the failure to include the specific address information would violate defendant's due process rights. The court found that the plain language of the uniform form order of protection found in N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 530.13(1)(a) satisfied the Bright test, and thus did not violate defendant's due process rights. Under the statute, defendant would not violate the order unless he was aware that a particular location was barred to him. Including a victim's address information in a "stay away" order would thus add no permanent due process protection for defendant while confusing the function of the order, to protect a particular person, not the current or subsequent location of the person. Defendant's due process rights would be sufficiently protected and the intent of the statute would be better served if it was incumbent upon a defendant who inadvertently entered a location and recognized a protected party to leave that location. The court additionally found that a "stay away" order which did not include address information would not be unconstitutionally vague.


OUTCOME: Defendant's application to include the victim's home, school, business, or place of employment addresses in a "stay away" order of protection was denied.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Order of Protection -- Validity of "Stay Away" Order of Protection

A nonfamily "stay away" permanent order of protection issued pursuant to CPL 530.13 in connection with defendant's negotiated plea to a disorderly conduct charge did not violate defendant's due process rights by failing to include the specific addresses of the protected party from which defendant was to stay away. The function of an order of protection is to protect a particular person, not the current or subsequent location of that person's home, school, business or place of employment. The order herein provided clear notice to defendant of the conduct that was prohibited, to stay away from the protected party wherever that party might be, and contained objective standards and guidelines to govern law enforcement. Inclusion of specific addresses in an order of protection would add no due process protection and would not account for the possibility that the protected party could move.

 

1529 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Lesczynski v. Bloomberg


102751/2005


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


2005 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3532; 234 N.Y.L.J. 101



November 25, 2005 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer filed a declaratory judgment suit against defendants, the City of New York (New York) a city board, and two city officials, seeking a judicial declaration that the New York City Campaign Finance Act (NYCCFA), Administrative Code of the City of NY § 3-701 et seq., was unconstitutional under U.S. Const. amend. I and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 8. Defendants moved to dismiss the complaint pursuant to CPLR 3211(a)(7).


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer was the chairperson of an alternative political party. He claimed that the NYCCFA impermissibly required taxpayers to fund political speech, in violation of their constitutional right to freedom of speech. Defendants asserted, among other claims, that the resident lacked standing to prosecute the suit. The court agreed. It was not clear what the nature of the speech that the taxpayer was objecting to was. He failed to show an "injury in fact" that he personally suffered, arising from the NYCCFA and its enforcement. The tax consequences arising from the NYCCRA was an injury suffered by all taxpayers. The taxpayer did not show that he had any special rights or interests in the matter. The compulsory funding of political speech did not fall within the zone of interests protected by the NYCCFA, which was designed to reduce political corruption and to provide voters with a greater selection of candidates. The taxpayer did not claim standing under General Municipal Law § 51, which allowed taxpayers to seek injunctive relief in certain situations. That statute did not apply because the taxpayer did not allege any illegal acts of fraud, corruption, or bad faith.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendants' dismissal motion. It ordered the court clerk to enter a judgment dismissing complaint in its entirety, with costs and disbursements to defendants.

People v. Fisher


04-1556


CITY COURT OF NEW YORK, ROCHESTER

1531 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Ortiz


3658-04


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, BRONX COUNTY


11 Misc. 3d 192; 811 N.Y.S.2d 547; 2005 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2742; 2005 NY Slip Op 25520; 234 N.Y.L.J. 68



October 6, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at People v. Ortiz, 2006 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8080 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, June 13, 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury returned a verdict finding defendant guilty of burglary in the third degree, N.Y. Penal Law § 140.20. The People served a persistent felony information upon the court and moved to have the defendant sentenced as a persistent felony offender pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 70.10. The court held a hearing to consider defendant's claims that the persistent felony offender was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the persistent felony offender (PFO) statute violated the Apprendi doctrine, which held that other than the fact of a prior conviction, any fact that increased the penalty for a crime beyond the prescribed statutory maximum had to be submitted to a jury, and proved beyond a reasonable doubt. The judge found that a recent New York case held that Apprendi was distinguishable from the PFO statute, because under New York's scheme, it was the mere fact of two or more prior convictions alone which determined eligibility for the higher sentence. The court was required to consider other enumerated factors to determine whether it was of the opinion that a PFO sentence was warranted, N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 400.20(9). The court found that this action was only fulfilling the court's traditional role, giving due consideration to agreed-upon factors in determining an appropriate sentence within the permissible statutory range. The court found that it was constrained to find that the defendant's challenge to the PFO statute was without merit.


OUTCOME: The court ordered a hearing on the People's request to sentence defendant as a persistent felony offender.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Sentence -- Persistent Felony Offender -- Constitutionality of Statute 

New York's persistent felony offender statute, which permits the imposition of a sentence of imprisonment authorized for a class A-I felony if the defendant has previously been convicted of two or more felonies (Penal Law § 70.10), is not unconstitutional. The statutory scheme does not impermissibly permit an enhanced sentence based upon a fact which has not previously been submitted to a jury and proved beyond a reasonable doubt. Rather, the prior felony convictions are the sole determinant of whether a defendant is subject to enhanced sentencing as a persistent felony offender. It is only after defendant's eligibility for persistent felony offender treatment is established by reason of the prior convictions that the sentencing court can consider other enumerated factors to determine whether persistent felony treatment is warranted (see CPL 400.20 [9]). Furthermore, any such enhancing factors must be proved beyond a reasonable doubt.

 

1532 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


McGuire v. Gamache


4509/05


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, DUTCHESS COUNTY


2005 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3578; 234 N.Y.L.J. 66



October 4, 2005 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed by Matter of McGuire v. Gamache, 22 A.D.3d 614, 804 N.Y.S.2d 333, 2005 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10923 (N.Y. App. Div. 2d Dep't, 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner aggrieved candidate filed an application seeking to invalidate the nominating petitions filed by respondent opponent for a county legislative representative position. The candidate also asked the court to enjoin respondent, a county board of elections (BOE), from placing the opponent's name on a general election official ballots as a candidate of a senior citizens' political party. Respondents moved to dismiss the suit.


OVERVIEW: Respondents contended that the suit should be dismissed because the candidate had not filed objections with the BOE, which they argued was a prerequisite for filing the suit under Election Law § 6-154(2). The candidate argued that a retracted portion of the nominating petitions, which struck out the statement that the witness was duly qualified to sign the petition, rendered the petitions invalid under Election Law § 6-140. The court held that Election Law § 6-154(2) did not apply because the nominating petitions were being challenged on their face and the candidate did not challenge the manner in which the opponent had obtained her candidacy to seek the party's nomination. It found that, pursuant to prior precedent that had held that a similar requirement in Election Law § 6-132 was unconstitutional, Election Law § 6-140's residency requirement was unconstitutional. It was not clear, however, that the statement was redacted from the petitions on that ground. The candidate had shown that the petitions did not facially comply with Election Law § 6-140. Respondents did not show that the petitions, as redacted, substantially complied with the statute.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the affirmative defense of lack of service, granted the candidate's application, and declared null and void the opponent's nominating petitions. The court enjoined and restrained the board of elections from placing the opponent's name on the general election ballot as the candidate for the senior citizens' political party. The parties were notified that they had 30 days in which to take an appeal.

105023/05402363/05
