Rose v. Brown & Williamson Tobacco Corp.


101996/2002


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


10 Misc. 3d 680; 809 N.Y.S.2d 784; 2005 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2392; 2005 NY Slip Op 25459; 234 N.Y.L.J. 81



September 29, 2005, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff injured party and her spouse sued defendant tobacco companies for damages from smoking their cigarettes and for loss of consortium, due to negligent product design. The trial was divided into considering (1) the companies' liability for compensatory damages, (2) the companies' liability for punitive damages, and (3) the amount of punitive damages. The trial court ruled as follows on various motions in limine.


OVERVIEW: Public awareness of smoking danger was barred unless the companies relied on public information to make product design decisions as all seven risk/utility factors, including awareness of a product's danger, did not have to be presented to a jury, and this factor was irrelevant to negligent product design. Primary assumption of the risk did not apply to cigarette smoking. Express assumption of the risk, based on cigarette package warnings, could only be claimed if the injured party was not addicted to smoking when the warnings were required. Federal conflict preemption did not bar negligent product design claims against the companies. The commercial viability of safer cigarettes was irrelevant to whether the safer design was feasible or functional. As alleged torts were directed by the companies' officers, the "clear and convincing" standard was inapplicable to proof of punitive damages. The companies' financial condition was relevant to the punitive damages amount. No such evidence was allowed until the trial phase at which this amount was considered, so such damages were not awarded based on the companies' wealth.


OUTCOME: The injured parties' motions to bar common knowledge evidence and dismiss primary assumption of the risk were granted, their motion to dismiss express assumption of the risk was denied, the companies' motion to dismiss was denied, evidence of a safer product's commercial viability was barred, the companies' motion for additional jury instructions was denied, and their motion to exclude evidence of their financial condition was denied.


HEADNOTES

Products Liability--Defectively Designed Product--Cigarette Manufacturers--Admissibility of Evidence of General Public's Awareness of Dangers of Cigarette Smoking

1. In a products liability action based upon defendants' alleged negligent design of cigarettes that plaintiff smoked over a 46-year period, allegedly resulting in her development of lung cancer, defendants were precluded from introducing at the liability phase of the trial evidence of the general public's awareness of the dangers of cigarette smoking in the absence of evidence showing that they made product design decisions in reasonable reliance upon the information available to the public. Information generally known to the public concerning the condition of a product would be relevant in a strict products liability action in which the condition of the product itself, as it is offered in the marketplace, is the central issue. However, in a negligent product design action in which the manufacturer's knowledge of the condition of the product as designed and the manufacturer's conduct in light of that knowledge are the central issues, public awareness of the dangers of the product would only be relevant and admissible when evidence exists that the manufacturer of the product reasonably relied upon the public awareness when making design decisions concerning the product.

Negligence--Assumption of Risk--Primary and Express Assumption of Risk--Defectively Designed Product--Dangers of Cigarette Smoking

2. Defendant cigarette manufacturers were not entitled to assert the affirmative defense of primary assumption of the risk in a products liability action based upon their alleged negligent design of cigarettes that plaintiff smoked over a 46-year period, allegedly resulting in her development of lung cancer. The doctrine of primary assumption of the risk is limited to participation in recreational or sporting events where there is a commonly understood elevated risk of danger inherent in the activity. The doctrine is premised upon the concept that the participant in the activity is free to choose whether or not to do so. Consequently, the doctrine would not apply to cigarette smoking because of the smoker's claim of addiction. However, defendants could assert the affirmative defense of express assumption of the risk to the extent they could demonstrate that plaintiff was not addicted to smoking cigarettes prior to 1969 when labels warning of the dangers of cigarette smoking became mandatory.

United States--Federal Preemption--Products Liability Action against Cigarette Manufacturers

3. Plaintiff's cause of action against defendant cigarette manufacturers for negligent product design was not barred by the doctrine of federal conflict preemption. Federal statutes regarding the labeling and marketing of tobacco products and the adverse consequences of tobacco use (see 15 USC § 1331 et seq.) do not preclude negligent product design claims from being asserted against cigarette manufacturers under state law.

Products Liability--Defectively Designed Product--Cigarette Manufacturers--Admissibility of Evidence of Commercial Viability of Safer Alternatively Designed Cigarettes

4. In a products liability action based upon defendants' alleged negligent design of cigarettes that plaintiff smoked over a 46-year period, allegedly resulting in her development of lung cancer, defendants were precluded from offering evidence concerning the commercial viability of safer, alternatively designed cigarettes to prove that a safer alternative design to the cigarettes they marketed was not feasible at the time plaintiff used their products. In a negligent product design action, the commercial viability of the proposed alternatively designed product is not relevant to the determination of whether the safer alternative design was feasible. In order to meet the burden of proving the feasibility of a safer alternative design, a plaintiff need only demonstrate the technological feasibility of the "design," not its commercial viability.

Damages--Punitive Damages--Defectively Designed Product--Cigarette Manufacturers--Burden of Proof

5. In a products liability action against defendant cigarette manufacturers for negligent product design, defendants were not entitled to a jury instruction at the punitive damages phase of the trial that plaintiff's entitlement to punitive damages had to be established by "clear and convincing" evidence. While a heightened "clear and convincing" evidentiary standard for punitive damages might apply where liability was based on respondeat superior, the ordinary "preponderance of the evidence" was applicable in this negligent design case where plaintiff was not seeking to hold defendants liable for the tortious acts of their employees. Under New York law, a claim for punitive damages is "inextricably linked" to the underlying cause of action. Consequently, the level of proof necessary to establish punitive damages must be the same as the level required to establish the underlying claim.

Damages--Punitive Damages--Defectively Designed Product--Cigarette Manufacturers--Admissibility of Evidence of Defendants' Financial Condition

6. In a products liability action against defendant cigarette manufacturers for negligent product design, evidence of defendants' financial condition was admissible during the punitive damages phase of the trial. Any due process limitations placed on the size of punitive damages awards to prevent unconstitutionally excessive awards do not foreclose consideration of a defendant's financial status in determining the amount of a punitive damages award. A defendant's wealth is relevant to the amount of the punitive damages award in order to ensure that the award carries the proper punishment and deterrent effect.

1539 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Uhlfelder v. Weinshall


109890/04


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


10 Misc. 3d 151; 810 N.Y.S.2d 275; 2005 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1812; 2005 NY Slip Op 25349; 234 N.Y.L.J. 43



August 24, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Injunction denied by Uhlfelder v. Weinshall, 2006 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1578 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, Jan. 26, 2006)
Later proceeding at Uhlfelder v. Weinshall, 2006 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2769 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, Mar. 9, 2006)
Motion granted by, in part, Motion denied by, in part Uhlfelder v. Weinshall, 2006 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3690 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, Mar. 21, 2006)
Affirmed by Uhlfelder v. Weinshall, 2007 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11141 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, Nov. 8, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a group of newsstand owners and operators, brought suit against defendants, a city, the city's department of transportation, and various other city officials and agencies, seeking a preliminary injunction against defendants from implementing and enforcing New York City, N.Y., Loc. Laws No. 64 (2003), which replaced all existing city newsstands with a single franchisee. Defendants moved for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The franchise created by Local Law 64 caused the newsstands to become owned by a single franchisee, subject to rebuilding and maintenance at the franchisee's expense, with plaintiffs continuing to operate the stands as licensees. Plaintiffs asserted that Local Law 64 was overly broad and vague, would cause 64 of 281 newsstands to close, violated their free speech rights, and constituted a taking under N.Y. Const. art. 1, § 8. The court disagreed and held that Local Law 64 provided reasonable and practical criteria as to location and building that were needed to promote public safety. The court further found that Local Law 64 was content neutral and that the siting requirements, including the American with Disabilities Act requirements, were narrowly drawn, rational, and objective regulations that reasonably balanced plaintiffs' rights to conduct business with a significant public interest. The court did find that plaintiffs sufficiently stated a claim to withstand summary judgment on the issue of whether a taking under N.Y. Const. art. 1, § 8 occurred by implementation of Local Law 64.


OUTCOME: The court denied plaintiffs' motion for a preliminary injunction, granted defendants' motion for summary judgment as to plaintiffs' siting requirements, advertising, fees for new licensee, and equal protection claims, but denied defendants' motion for summary judgment as to plaintiffs' taking claim.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Municipal Regulation of Location and Operation of Sidewalk Newsstands -- Preliminary Injunction 

Plaintiffs were not entitled to preliminary injunctive relief on constitutional grounds preventing the City from implementing a 2003 local law that required replacement of all existing newsstands with new newsstands owned, constructed and maintained by a single franchisee, required that the new newsstands be designed and built in compliance with the Americans With Disabilities Act (ADA) (42 USC § 12131 et seq.), and imposed siting and display space requirements for new licensees (Administrative Code of City of NY § 20-241.1). Plaintiffs failed to demonstrate that the local law violated due process by giving city officials unfettered discretion to issue and revoke licenses based upon an unconstitutionally vague criterion of avoiding "hazardous conditions." The provisions of the local law regarding "hazardous conditions" set forth reasonable and practical criteria consistent with the City's governmental obligation to provide for a safe urban environment. Furthermore, the siting requirements of the local law, including the amount of clear path around a newsstand, the rate of pedestrian flow at the location and compliance with ADA standards, did not impermissibly burden any cognizable First Amendment rights. The siting requirements, taken together with the ADA requirements, were narrowly drawn, rational, objective, content-neutral regulations that reasonably balanced the licensees' rights to conduct their businesses with a significant public interest. In addition, the provision of the local law requiring new licensees to pay the franchisee for the cost of construction of the newsstand did not impermissibly burden any First Amendment rights.

1542 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Michaelides


QN11291/96


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, QUEENS COUNTY


9 Misc. 3d 408; 799 N.Y.S.2d 401; 2005 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1505; 2005 NY Slip Op 25294; 234 N.Y.L.J. 27



July 14, 2005, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant moved for an order pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 440.20(1) setting aside his sentence on the ground that it was illegally imposed or otherwise invalid as a matter of law, or, in the alternative, granting him a resentencing hearing consistent with the current sentencing provisions of the recently enacted Rockefeller Drug Law Reform Act, 2004 N.Y. Laws 738. This matter followed.


OVERVIEW: Under 2004 N.Y. Laws 738, § 23 of the legislation at issue, the retroactive resentencing provisions applied only to those sentenced defendants who were convicted of an A-I drug offense and sentenced to an indeterminate term of imprisonment of at least 15 years to life. Defendant, as a sentenced A-II felony drug offender, was thus not eligible to be resentenced. But, he alleged that there was an unconstitutional disparity in the retroactive application of the new legislation. Hence, the court found that there was a rational basis permitting A-I drug offenders to be resentenced immediately, as: (1) the legislature believed that the long minimum sentences being served by A-I drug offenders were much harsher and more egregious than the sentences being served by A-II drug offenders; (2) allowing A-I felons to be resentenced intended to lessen any disparity in the treatment of defendants sentenced under the old law; and (3) the retroactive sentencing effected approximately four hundred inmates, whereas resentencing all lower level drug offenders could cause an undue burden on the court system. Second, the difference in the severity of the sentences justified the different treatment.


OUTCOME: Defendant's motion was denied.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Sentence -- Rockefeller Drug Law Reform Act -- Constitutionality of Disparity in Retroactive Application of Act 

The disparity in the retroactive application of the Rockefeller Drug Law Reform Act (L 2004, ch 738), which denies retroactive resentencing for those currently serving sentences for class A-II drug felony convictions while allowing retroactive resentencing for those convicted of class A-I drug felonies, is not unconstitutional. The significant differences in the old sentencing structure, requiring a 15-year minimum sentence for A-I offenders as compared to a three-year minimum sentence for A-II offenders, provides a rational basis for the different treatment accorded to A-I and A-II offenders. By permitting immediate retroactive resentencing for A-I and not for A-II offenders, the Legislature intended to allow for the release of those drug offenders serving the harshest and longest minimum sentences. The Legislature also intended to lessen any disparity in the treatment of A-I offenders sentenced under the old law as opposed to the new law, and no significant disparity between the laws exists for A-II offenders. Moreover, the retroactive sentencing provision for A-I offenders affects only approximately 400 inmates, whereas resentencing all lower level drug offenders could cause an undue burden on the court system.

1546 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Wing Dong Moi


1114-87


COUNTY COURT OF NEW YORK, WESTCHESTER COUNTY


8 Misc. 3d 1012(A); 801 N.Y.S.2d 780; 2005 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1401; 2005 NY Slip Op 51068(U)



June 3, 2005, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] THIS OPINION IS UNCORRECTED AND WILL NOT BE PUBLISHED IN THE OFFICIAL REPORTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State sought to require defendant, who had been convicted of kidnapping, N.Y. Penal Law § 135.20, to register as a sex offender under New York's Sex Offender Registration Act (SORA), N.Y. Correct. Law § 168-a(2)(a). Defendant objected, arguing that the crime for which he was convicted had no sexual component.


OVERVIEW: In 1989, defendant pleaded guilty to kidnapping based on his role in forcing two members of a rival gang, males aged 15 and 16, into a car. The victims were taken away by other gang members and killed. Defendant was released from prison in 1991, and in 1996 he was notified that he was required to register as a sex offender based on his kidnapping conviction. He claimed that SORA violated his right to due process and equal protection as guaranteed to him by U.S. Const. amends. V and XIV and N.Y. Const., art I, §§ 6, 11. The court found that SORA did not implicate a fundamental right, thereby disagreeing in part with the decision in Bell, and applied the rational basis test. It found that the rational relation to a legitimate state interest in protecting children from sexual predators, which the statute possessed, evaporated where there was no sexual element to the crime. The court noted that there was a presumption of legitimacy in SORA's requirement that those convicted of certain kidnapping offenses register as sex offenders, but that the presumption could be rebutted where, as here, defendant could establish that there was no rational basis for application of the statute to him.


OUTCOME: The court ruled that defendant would not be subject to the classification and registration requirements of New York's Sex Offender Registration Act.


HEADNOTES

 [*1012A]  [**780] Constitutional Law--Validity of Statute--Application of Sex Offender Registration Act.

 

1547 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Goldman v. Fay


S.C.R. 243/05


CIVIL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, RICHMOND COUNTY


8 Misc. 3d 959; 797 N.Y.S.2d 731; 2005 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1215; 2005 NY Slip Op 25236; 233 N.Y.L.J. 114



June 2, 2005, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Claimant buyer filed a small claims action against defendant seller, alleging that the seller failed to disclose a leak in the premises the buyer purchased from the seller. The seller counterclaimed, alleging that the buyer's suit was frivolous.


OVERVIEW: The buyer argued that the air conditioning pipe had been cut and left to drain into the wall, causing water damage. The trial court held that because N.Y. Real Prop. Law § 461(5) excluded residential condominiums and cooperatives from the New York Property Condition Disclosure Act, N.Y. Real Prop. Law § 460 et seq., N.Y. Real Prop. Law art. 14 violated the Equal Protection Clause. The property was inspected by a structural engineer before the contract was executed. Although the engineer's report was not part of the record, the contract did not contain any amendments indicating that items needed to be repaired. The buyer did not discover the problem until seven months after the sale. There was no independent evidence that the defective condition was caused by the seller or that the condition existed prior to the seller's move. The buyer also did not discover the problem in a walk-through inspection. The buyer did not provide expert testimony as to the cause of the problem. Further, it would violate public policy to allow the buyer to recover as repairs were not made by a licensed home improvement contractor. Finally, the seller failed to show damages to support her counterclaim.


OUTCOME: The buyer's cause of action was dismissed. The seller's counterclaim was dismissed. The New York Property Condition Disclosure Act was declared unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional Law -- Equal Protection of Laws -- Property Condition Disclosure Act 

1. The Property Condition Disclosure Act, which requires the seller in a "residential real estate" transaction to complete a property condition disclosure form prior to the execution of the contract of sale (Real Property Law § 461 [5]), is unconstitutional as being violative of equal protection to the extent that the statute excludes condominium and cooperative transactions from its coverage. There is no rational basis for the statutory distinction between different classes of residential real estate ownership.

Vendor and Purchaser -- Contract for Sale of Condominium -- Liability for Defective Condition in Premises -- No Recovery for Repair Work Performed by Unlicensed Home Improvement Contractor 

2. Claimant in a smalls claim action was not entitled to recover the costs incurred in repairing water damage caused by an allegedly undisclosed leak in the condominium unit purchased from defendant. Claimant had a structural engineer inspect the premises before entering into the contract of sale and did a walk-through inspection before closing title. Claimant failed to produce any independent expert evidence that the defect in the air-conditioning system was caused by defendant or that defendant had either actual or constructive knowledge of the defective condition prior to the closing. In any event, since the repair work was not performed by a licensed home improvement contractor (see General Business Law art 36-A; 6 RCNY 2-221 et seq.), public policy precluded reimbursement of claimant for the repair work.

People v. Stephen B.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


JUSTICE COURT OF NEW YORK, WESTCHESTER COUNTY


2005 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3322; 233 N.Y.L.J. 97



May 20, 2005 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought relief in the form of an order dismissing a misdemeanor complaint charging him with second-degree criminal contempt, a violation of Penal Law § 215.50(3), and vacating a temporary order of protection issued pursuant to CPL §§ 170.30(1)(a)(f), 170.35 (1)(a), (b) on the ground that the accusatory instrument was facially insufficient to support the charge.


OVERVIEW: The defendant and the complainant were husband and wife engaged in a contested divorce proceeding. During the pendency of those proceedings, mutual orders of protection were issued by a family court. The marital residence was divided by the family court, reserving different living areas for each of the parties. Defendant allegedly called the complainant names and removed wires from a computer. The court found no allegation that defendant violated the territorial restrictions of the family court order of protection or physically attacked or threatened the complainant. Furthermore, there was no allegation of a course of conduct, but rather the complaint alleged only one incident. Because all of the conduct complained of occurred within the marital residence, it was private in nature. In addition, the conduct that occurred did not rise to the level of the harassment. Therefore, pursuant to CPL § 170.30(1)(a)(f), 170.35(1)(a), (b), the allegations contained in the accusatory instrument charging defendant with contempt for violating the protective order under Penal Law § 215 50(3) were facially insufficient.


OUTCOME: The allegations contained in the accusatory instrument were dismissed in all respects as facially insufficient.

1553 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Quinones


2584/04


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


2005 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3332; 233 N.Y.L.J. 79



April 26, 2005 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at People v. Quinones, 2007 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12157 (N.Y. App. Div. 2d Dep't, Nov. 27, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of burglary and, in addition, had two prior convictions for felony offenses. The people requested that the court sentence defendant as a persistent felony offender, Penal Law § 70.10. Defendant opposed and argued that the court was precluded from sentencing him as such because the persistent felony offender statute was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: A factual determination regarding the commission of the crime for which defendant had not been convicted, but which served as the basis for an enhanced sentence, had to be determined by jury beyond a reasonable doubt. Pursuant to Penal Law § 70.10, the instant court would not make additional factual determinations regarding the crime for which defendant was convicted. Rather, defendant, by his prior convictions, was eligible to be sentenced within the range of a persistent felony offender. Whether the court would impose such a sentence was an exercise of broad discretion based on traditional sentencing factors. Thus, the New York sentencing statute regarding a persistent felony offender did not violate United States Supreme Court holdings in Apprendi, Ring, and Blakely. Moreover, because the statute did not mandate that the court impose a persistent felony sentence, it did not violate Booker. In Booker, the Supreme Court held that a discretionary component of a sentence need not be determined by a jury beyond a reasonable doubt. Thus, the New York State persistent felony offender statute comported with recently established Supreme Court reasoning and precedent.


OUTCOME: Defendant's motion was denied.

 

1554 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Marrero


64550C-2003


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, BRONX COUNTY


8 Misc. 3d 172; 792 N.Y.S.2d 845; 2005 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 586; 2005 NY Slip Op 25125



March 29, 2005, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendant has failed to make out a prima facie case that her right to equal protection has been violated by transferring her cases to Supreme Court, and her motion to dismiss on this ground denied as well.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, charged by information with various identity theft-related misdemeanors, moved to dismiss the charges after an administrative judge transferred the proceeding from the Criminal Court of the City of New York, arguing that N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 210.05 deprived the supreme court of jurisdiction over misdemeanor prosecutions and that transfer of her cases violated her equal protection guarantees.


OVERVIEW: Section 210.05 required prosecutors to seek indictments before invoking the supreme court's jurisdiction in certain types of prosecutions. Based on both the plain language of the statute and its legislative history, it was clear that this provision was intended to prevent prosecutors from overreaching in their prosecutions, not to limit the general jurisdiction of the supreme court, guaranteed by N.Y. Const. art. VI and N.Y. Jud. Law § 140-b, in any way. The legislation establishing local criminal courts had likewise established only concurrent jurisdiction over certain matters but had not limited supreme court jurisdiction in any way. Therefore, there was no statutory impediment to an administrative order, aimed at relieving court congestion, transferring some misdemeanor prosecutions from the criminal court to the supreme court. Although African American and Hispanic defendants predominated in the county in question, that was not true of all the counties where experimental transfers were being made, so there was no showing of equal protection violations.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion to dismiss on all grounds.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Removal to Superior Court -- Jurisdiction of Supreme Court over Misdemeanor Cases 

There was no statutory or constitutional impediment to the transfer of misdemeanor charges against defendant from the lower criminal court to the newly-created Criminal Division of Supreme Court, Bronx County, pursuant to administrative order (see 22 NYCRR 142.3). Supreme Court has general trial jurisdiction over misdemeanor cases being prosecuted by information (see NY Const, art VI, § 7; CPL 10.20 [1]). CPL 210.05, which provides that the "only methods of prosecuting an offense in a superior court are by indictment . . . or by superior court information," is a procedural statute directed to prosecutors that could not be interpreted to deprive Supreme Court of its historical and constitutional power to preside over misdemeanor cases. Furthermore, the transfer of the misdemeanor charges to Supreme Court did not violate defendant's equal protection rights in the absence of any allegation that the transfer order was done with a "racially discriminatory purpose."

Peerless Abstract Corp. v. Seltzer


04-21613


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, SUFFOLK COUNTY


2005 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3290; 233 N.Y.L.J. 54



March 22, 2005 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at Peerless Abstract Corp. v. Seltzer, 35 A.D.3d 422, 824 N.Y.S.2d 714, 2006 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 14545 (N.Y. App. Div. 2d Dep't, Dec. 5, 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, an abstract company and others, sued defendants, including attorneys and residents, alleging abuse of process for the commencement and continuation of a frivolous lawsuit. Defendants applied for dismissal of the complaint pursuant to CPLR 3211(a)(1), (a)(5), (a)(7), and (g). Plaintiffs applied for summary judgment as to the abuse of process claim and defendants' counterclaim.


OVERVIEW: In an underlying action, defendants sued plaintiffs, alleging that the payment by a county and a town for estate property was unlawful. Plaintiffs provided documentary proof that the abstract company was not involved in the relevant transactions, but defendants refused to withdraw plaintiffs' names. Eventually, defendants withdrew the action and were sanctioned. In the present case, the court determined that dismissal of plaintiffs' complaint was not warranted under CPLR 3211(a)(1) because the decision in the underlying action indicated that there was no basis for naming plaintiffs in the underlying action. Under CPLR 3211(a)(5), plaintiffs' action was not barred by stipulation, because defendants did not submit the agreement to the court. Under CPLR 3211(a)(7), plaintiffs sufficiently set forth an abuse of process cause of action based upon the allegations regarding the frivolous lawsuit. Under CPLR 3211(g), defendants did not demonstrate that the action or counterclaim was an action involving public petition and participation under Civil Rights Law § 76-a(1)(a). Finally, plaintiffs were entitled to summary judgment as to the abuse of process claim based on the frivolous action.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendants' application for dismissal. The court granted plaintiffs' application for summary judgment as to the abuse of process claim against one attorney, but denied the application for summary judgment as to the counterclaim.

 

1556 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Weaver


165-04


COUNTY COURT OF NEW YORK, ULSTER COUNTY


7 Misc. 3d 922; 791 N.Y.S.2d 914; 2005 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 469; 2005 NY Slip Op 25102



March 16, 2005, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendant Weaver may plead to the B felony in count 2 in satisfaction of the A-I felony in Count 1; upon a plea defendant Weaver's sentence will be guided by Penal Law § 70.06 which has indeterminate sentencing from a minimum of 4 1/2 to 9 years to a max of 12 1/2 to 25.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with criminal possession of a controlled substance in the first degree, an A-I felony, and criminal possession of a controlled substance in the third degree, a B felony. The New York State Legislature passed the Drug Law Reform Act (DLRA). A potential plea bargain sought to have the court accept a plea to the B felony in satisfaction of the A-I felony and the minimum mandatory state prison sentence based upon the new DLRA.


OVERVIEW: The People were willing to take the plea with a minimum sentence. The crimes that defendant was charged with were committed prior to the earliest effective date of the DLRA. The general rule was that nonprocedural statutes were not to be applied retroactively absent a manifest legislative intent to that effect. The reduction statute, DLRA § 16, N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 220.10, was a procedural statute and did not specify whether it was intended to be retroactive. The sentencing statute, DLRA § 36, N.Y. Penal Law § 70.70, on the other hand, was a substantive (nonprocedural) statute which specified that it was not retroactive to the January 13, 2005, effective date. The court found that the sentencing statute evinced in N.Y. Penal Law § 70.70 was not retroactive to crimes committed prior to the effective date of January 13, 2005, through the manifest intent of the plain language of DLRA § 41(d-1) that it applied to crimes committed on or after the effective day. It was not the province of the trial judiciary to change the clear and unambiguous language of duly enacted law unless its application would have effected an unconstitutional, illegal, or harmful act.


OUTCOME: Defendant could have pled to the B felony in satisfaction of the A-I felony. Upon a plea, defendant's sentence would have been guided by the prior statute that had indeterminate sentencing from a minimum of 4 1/2 to 9 years to a maximum of 12 1/2 to 25.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Sentence -- New Sentencing Guidelines under Drug Law Reform Act Not Retroactive 

Defendant was not entitled to be sentenced under the more favorable sentencing guidelines of the Drug Law Reform Act (L 2004, ch 738 [DLRA]) for drug offenses contained in Penal Law § 70.70, as his offense was committed prior to the effective date of section 70.70. While a nonprocedural statute reducing the punishment for a particular crime is generally retroactive absent evidence of legislative intent to the contrary, the DLRA clearly states that the new sentencing guidelines "shall apply to crimes committed on or after the effective date" (L 2004, ch 738, § 41 [d-1]).

 

1557 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Feldman


7189/03


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


7 Misc. 3d 794; 791 N.Y.S.2d 361; 2005 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 399; 2005 NY Slip Op 25083



March 3, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition denied by, Dismissed by Feldman v. Marcus, 23 A.D.3d 559, 806 N.Y.S.2d 620, 2005 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13336 (2d Dept 2005)



DISPOSITION: Defendant's motion for the Court to inspect the grand jury minutes granted; Defendants' motions for disclosure of the grand jury minutes to them denied; Defendants' motions to dismiss the indictment denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants filed respective motions to dismiss the indictment against them and for disclosure of the grand jury minutes. One defendant filed a motion to inspect the grand jury minutes. Defendants were charged in a 22-count indictment with various counts and degrees of grand larceny by extortion, attempted grand larceny and extortion, coercion and attempted coercion, and conspiracy to commit those crimes.


OVERVIEW: The charges against defendants arose out of the 2002 primary election campaigns of two judges. Defendants were the chairman and the executive director, respectively, of a county Democratic committee. The allegations were that defendants demanded that the candidates campaign jointly, use a vendor designated by them to produce and distribute campaign literature for at least three joint mailings, and pay a person designated by them to run campaign operations and, if they did not, defendants would cause the Democratic Party to withdraw its endorsement and/or support from the candidates. The court refused to dismiss the indictments, because the evidence was legally sufficient to establish that defendants threatened and conspired to threaten the candidates with conduct that would have damaged their campaigns. The evidence was also sufficient to show that defendants sought or obtained a financial benefit for themselves in the nature of a kickback from the campaign operations person they sought to force the candidates to use. The court also held N.Y. Penal Law §§ 135.60, 155.05 were constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion of one defendant to inspect the grand jury minutes. The court denied defendants' motions for disclosure of the grand jury minutes, and denied defendants' motions to dismiss the indictment.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Larceny -- Extortion -- Coercion -- Threats by Political Party Leaders to Withdraw Party's Endorsements from Judicial Candidates

1. An indictment charging defendants, in their representative capacities as political party leaders, with larceny by extortion and coercion for having allegedly threatened to withdraw their party's previously given endorsements in the primary election from judicial candidates who did not use a designated vendor to produce and distribute campaign literature for joint mailings and to pay another designated person to run joint campaign "operations" was supported by legally sufficient evidence. The grand jury could have reasonably inferred from the timing of defendants' demands shortly before the primary election in the midst of the window of time when nominating signatures could be gathered that defendants exercised their power as party leaders in a coercive and improper manner that would not have materially benefitted the party (see Penal Law § 135.60 [9]; § 155.05 [2] [e] [ix]). Moreover, the grand jury could have reasonably concluded that the party had no reason to demand that the judicial candidates use the designated vendor or suffer the loss of the party's endorsement or support.

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Validity of Extortion and Coercion Statutes as Applied to Threats Made by Political Party Leaders to Judicial Candidates

2. An indictment charging defendants, in their representative capacities as political party leaders, with larceny by extortion and coercion for having allegedly threatened to withdraw their party's previously given endorsements in the primary election from judicial candidates who did not use a designated vendor to produce and distribute campaign literature for joint mailings and to pay another designated person to run joint campaign "operations" was not subject to dismissal on constitutional grounds. The extortion and coercion statutes involved (Penal Law § 135.60 [9]; § 155.05 [2] [e] [ix]) are not violative of either the right to free speech or the right of association. The statutes focus on threats of damage made for no legitimate purpose that fall outside the realm of ordinary social or political conflict. Moreover, application of the statutes to defendants' conduct would not impermissibly interfere with the "internal administration" of their party's organization. Furthermore, the statutes are not impermissibly vague or overbroad as applied to the conduct with which defendants were charged. The threats defendants allegedly made did not embody either legitimate political decision making or a legitimate exercise of party discipline, and were not otherwise constitutionally protected expressions of ideas or advocacy.

Crimes -- Indictment -- Dismissal Based upon Doctrine of Lenity

3. An indictment charging defendants, in their representative capacities as political party leaders, with larceny by extortion and coercion for having allegedly threatened to withdraw their party's previously given endorsements in the primary election from judicial candidates who did not use a designated vendor to produce and distribute campaign literature for joint mailings and to pay another designated person to run joint campaign "operations" was not subject to dismissal based upon the doctrine of lenity. That doctrine is one of "last resort" and should only be invoked with regard to punishment for actions that are not plainly and unmistakably proscribed. Here, the extortion and coercion statutes involved (Penal Law § 135.60 [9]; § 155.05 [2] [e] [ix]) made reasonably clear to defendants at the relevant time that their alleged conduct was criminal.

 

1558 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Investigation No. 04-730


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


COUNTY COURT OF NEW YORK, ONONDAGA COUNTY


8 Misc. 3d 238; 794 N.Y.S.2d 873; 2005 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 785; 2005 NY Slip Op 25150



March 2, 2005, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Movant, a witness, sought to have a grand jury subpoena issued to her quashed, or to be excused from testifying, based on her claim that she was the subject of a person in need of supervision (PINS) proceeding and a witness in a neglect proceeding, and that any testimony she gave might put her liberty at stake.


OVERVIEW: The witness contended that her testimony would presumably deal with the subject matter of the PINS and the neglect proceeding, which had not been resolved. Further, she claimed that if she was found to be a PINS, she faced placement in a non-secured detention facility, which would impair her liberty. The court determined that the application was made pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 2304. The court noted that the witness would not be obligated to execute a waiver of immunity. The court found that the grant of transactional immunity conferred on the witness by N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 190.40 extended to the PINS proceeding. It also concluded that while the witness could be compelled to testify before the grand jury, she was to be accorded the protections under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 50.10. The court reviewed the history and purpose of the immunity, and noted that it was not limited to the prosecution of criminal offenses, but instead, applied to the witness's potential loss of liberty herein. Accordingly, she was not to be subjected to any family court proceeding in which her testimony would result in her loss of liberty.


OUTCOME: The court denied the witness's motion, but ordered that her testimony was entitled to statutory protections of immunity.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Grand Jury -- Transactional Immunity -- Immunity Extending to Person in Need of Supervision Proceeding 

The People had the authority pursuant to CPL 190.40 to compel a juvenile who is both the subject of a person in need of supervision (PINS) proceeding that could result in the loss of her liberty and a witness in a neglect proceeding to testify before the grand jury concerning unresolved Family Court matters. However, if the juvenile testifies before the grand jury without a waiver of immunity, she must be accorded full transactional immunity pursuant to CPL 50.10 that extends to the PINS proceeding. A PINS proceeding implicates a fundamental "liberty interest" in remaining free from detention and supervision restrictions and is thus covered by the immunity protections afforded by CPL 50.10.

Minchew v. City of New York


41573/04


CIVIL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, RICHMOND COUNTY


6 Misc. 3d 1038(A); 800 N.Y.S.2d 350; 2005 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 470; 2005 NY Slip Op 50344(U)

1560 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Seymour v. Holcomb


2004-0458


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, TOMPKINS COUNTY


7 Misc. 3d 530; 790 N.Y.S.2d 858; 2005 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 313; 2005 NY Slip Op 25070



February 23, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
Affirmed by Seymour v. Holcomb, 26 A.D.3d 661, 811 N.Y.S.2d 134, 2006 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1975 (N.Y. App. Div. 3d Dep't, Feb. 16, 2006)



DISPOSITION: Plaintiffs' motions for summary judgment denied; Department of Health's cross-motion for summary judgment dismissing the complaint granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff same-sex couples and nominal defendants, the city and its clerk, moved for summary judgment in the couples' action seeking a declaration that the New York's Domestic Relations Law was unconstitutionally denying the couples equal protection, substantive due process, and free speech. Defendant New York State Department of Health, which was responsible for issuance of marriage certificates, cross-moved for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The couples brought their action after the city clerk told them that under guidelines issued by the Department she could not issue marriage licenses to them. The court held, first, that the clerk and the city had no standing to appear in the action at all, since the clerk's only role in issuing the license was a ministerial one. On the merits, the couples failed to carry their burden to show unconstitutionality. Since sexual orientation was not a suspect classification, the Department only had to show a rational relationship to a legitimate state purpose, and as an appellate court in another division had found, the legislature was within its rights in deciding that promotion of traditional family life and procreation of children was an important purpose of marriage. There was also no violation of substantive due process, because the right to same-sex marriage had not been recognized as a fundamental right. Finally, there was no violation of the couples' free speech guarantees because they retained the right to express their commitment to each other in a ceremonial manner.


OUTCOME: The court granted the Department's cross-motion, and entered summary judgment of dismissal.


HEADNOTES

Parties -- Standing -- Same Sex Marriage -- Standing of Municipality to Challenge Constitutionality of State Marriage Laws

1. In a declaratory judgment action by same-sex couples seeking to obtain marriage licenses from the City Clerk of the City of Ithaca, defendant City lacked standing to interpose cross claims challenging the constitutionality of the state law limiting the issuance of marriage licenses to heterosexual couples. The duty of the City Clerk to issue marriage licenses to qualified applicants (Domestic Relations Law § 15 [2]; § 23) is ministerial and nondiscretionary. The Clerk's actions with respect to license applications and recording of information are governed solely by state law and are not in furtherance of any role as a representative or agency of the City. The City failed to establish that it had a justiciable stake in the outcome of the action or that it was aggrieved or even affected by the Department of Health's interpretation of the State's marriage laws as not authorizing the issuance of marriage licenses to same-sex couples.

Administrative Law -- Administrative Interpretation -- Same Sex Marriage -- Authority of Department of Health to Issue Advisory Letter

2. The City Clerk of the City of Ithaca properly refused to issue marriage licenses to same-sex couples in reliance on an advisory letter from the New York State Department of Health indicating that such licenses were not authorized by state law. The Department acted within its authority in providing guidance to the City Clerk regarding the application of the State's marriage laws. The Department has authority to supervise the registration and recording of all marriages (Domestic Relations Law § 23), and has unlimited authority to issue "forms and instructions, interpretive statements and statements of general policy which in themselves have no legal effect but are merely explanatory" (State Administrative Procedure Act § 102 [2] [b] [iv]). The advisory letter was an accurate explanation of the law and was thus not subject to the rule-making process.

Marriage -- Same Sex Marriage -- Constitutionality of Limiting Issuance of Marriage Licenses to Heterosexual Couples

3. State law does not authorize the issuance of marriage licenses to same-sex couples. The limitation of marriage licenses to heterosexual couples is not violative of the equal protection rights of same-sex couples under the rational basis standard. The sexual orientation-based license classification is rationally related to the legitimate state interest in preserving the historic institution of heterosexual marriage, which in turn uniquely fosters procreation and child-rearing. Similarly, the limitation of marriage licenses to opposite-sex couples is not violative of due process. The civil marriage of same-sex couples is not a fundamental constitutional right entitled to due process protection.

212812

1562 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Metro. Opera Ass'n, Inc. v. Figaro Sys., Inc.


602578/03


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


7 Misc. 3d 503; 794 N.Y.S.2d 611; 2005 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 230; 2005 NY Slip Op 25062; 233 N.Y.L.J. 35



February 16, 2005, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Motions ruled upon.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff opera association sued defendant technical systems company under N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 397 and sought summary judgment as to liability and a permanent injunction to prevent the company from using the association's name, and an inquest to determine the association's damages. The company cross-moved for summary judgment dismissing the complaint and for leave to amend its answer to add affirmative defenses.


OVERVIEW: After the trial court denied the parties' summary judgment motions, counsel agreed the fact question the court found was not an element of N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 397 and only the statute's facial constitutionality, contested by the company, should be considered. The parties could not agree to have a court decide a constitutional question. While the truth or falsity of literature in which the company allegedly used the association's name without consent was no element of § 397, the issue was whether, in analyzing § 397's infringement of the company's commercial speech, this speech was misleading and not entitled to First Amendment protection. A dispute as to whether the literature was misleading had to be tried to decide if the statute was constitutional. If the speech was not protected by the First Amendment, the court could not decide the statute's constitutionality. The company's proposed amendments had no arguable merit. Equitable estoppel did not apply as N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 397 required written consent to use the association's name. The company did not show it no longer used the association's name, so mootness and/or futility were not defenses.


OUTCOME: The parties' motions were previously denied.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Unlawful Use of Name or Other Identification of Nonprofit Organization 

In an action for injunctive relief and damages pursuant to General Business Law § 397 based upon defendant's alleged unauthorized use of plaintiff nonprofit organization's name for defendant's own commercial purposes, the issue of whether the statute was facially unconstitutional under the First Amendment was not reached prior to resolution of the disputed factual issue of whether defendant's commercial speech was misleading, regardless of the stipulation of the parties to have the court decide the constitutional issue. Plaintiff, a world renowned opera company, challenged the claim made in defendant's advertising brochure and on its Web site that defendant's patented simultaneous opera translation system was installed at plaintiff's opera house. Plaintiff alleged that it developed and used its own simultaneous translation system. If it is determined that defendant's brochure and Web site were misleading, defendant's commercial speech would not be protected by the First Amendment, and there would thus be no need to reach the constitutional issue. A court should not reach a constitutional challenge before it is necessary to do so, and the parties cannot consent to bind a court to determine a constitutional issue.

1564 of 1817 DOCUMENTS



Hernandez v. Robles


103434/2004


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


7 Misc. 3d 459; 794 N.Y.S.2d 579; 2005 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 248; 2005 NY Slip Op 25057; 233 N.Y.L.J. 25



February 4, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Transferred by Hernandez v. Robles, 4 N.Y.3d 824, 829 N.E.2d 670, 2005 N.Y. LEXIS 752, 796 N.Y.S.2d 577 (2005)
Motion granted by, in part, Motion denied by, in part Hernandez v. Robles, 2005 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7529 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, June 30, 2005)
Later proceeding at Hernandez v. Robles, 2005 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7905 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, July 14, 2005)
Later proceeding at Hernandez v. Robles, 2005 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8002 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, July 21, 2005)
Later proceeding at Hernandez v. Robles, 2005 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8358 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, Aug. 4, 2005)
Later proceeding at Hernandez v. Robles, 2005 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8554 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, Aug. 4, 2005)
Later proceeding at Hernandez v. Robles, 2005 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8857 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, Aug. 18, 2005)
Later proceeding at Hernandez v. Robles, 2005 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8908 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, Sept. 1, 2005)
Later proceeding at Hernandez v. Robles, 2005 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8907 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, Sept. 1, 2005)
Reversed by, Vacated by, in part, Summary judgment granted, in part, summary judgment denied, in part by Hernandez v. Robles, 26 A.D.3d 98, 805 N.Y.S.2d 354, 2005 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13892 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, Dec. 8, 2005)



DISPOSITION: Defendant was enjoined from denying a marriage license to any couple solely on the ground that the two persons in that couple are of the same sex.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs same-sex couples sued defendant city clerk for a declaration that they were entitled to treatment equal to opposite-sex couples regarding the issuance of marriage licenses and access to civil marriage and sought an injunction requiring the clerk to grant each couple a marriage license. The couples moved for summary judgment, and the clerk moved for summary judgment dismissing the complaint.


OVERVIEW: N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 10 did not expressly prohibit same-sex civil marriages, but the legislature did not intend to authorize them. No binding precedent resolved the constitutionality of prohibiting such marriages. The personal autonomy to decide who one would marry was within the right to privacy. Excluding same-sex couples from civil marriage infringed on that fundamental right and served no compelling state interest. Moral disapproval of same-sex couples was not a legitimate state purpose or rational reason to infringe that right. The state could not deprive this right because other states and the federal government did. Denying the freedom to choose one's spouse to one who wished to marry a person of the same sex denied the fundamental right to marry. Excluding the couples from civil marriage discriminated against them based on sexual orientation, which could not be a basis for denying equal protection. It rationally served no legitimate state purpose. To cure the constitutional defect in the New York Domestic Relations Law, "husband," "wife," "groom" and "bride" were construed to mean "spouse," and all personal pronouns were construed to apply equally to either men or women.


OUTCOME: The couples' summary judgment motion was granted, the clerk's summary judgment motion was denied, and the clerk was permanently enjoined from denying a marriage license to any couple on the ground that the two persons in that couple were of the same sex.



HEADNOTES

Marriage -- Same Sex Marriage -- Constitutionality of Marriage Laws 

New York's marriage laws are unconstitutional under the State Constitution to the extent that they deny marriage licenses and access to civil marriage to same-sex couples such as plaintiffs (see Domestic Relations Law §§ 12, 15). The right to choose one's spouse is fundamental to the State's constitutional right of privacy (see NY Const, art I, § 6). The restriction of civil marriage to only opposite-sex couples violates fundamental due process protections under the strict scrutiny standard. Defendant City of New York failed to demonstrate "a compelling state interest" for the exclusion of same-sex couples from eligibility for civil marriage. Fostering the traditional institution of marriage and avoiding the problems that might arise from a refusal by other jurisdictions to recognize the validity of same-sex marriages did not constitute the requisite "compelling state interest" necessary to sustain the marriage classification. The State's restriction on same-sex marriage also violates equal protection. Sexual orientation cannot be used as a basis for denying any person the equal protection of the law. In order to cure the constitutional defect in the marriage laws, the words "husband," "wife," "groom" and "bride," as they appear in the relevant sections of the Domestic Relations Law, should be construed to mean "spouse," and all relevant personal pronouns should be construed to apply equally to either men or women.

 

1565 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. James


2004NY0655625


CRIMINAL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


7 Misc. 3d 363; 793 N.Y.S.2d 871; 2005 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 81; 2005 NY Slip Op 25027; 233 N.Y.L.J. 25



January 18, 2005, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendant's motion denied in all respects.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant moved to dismiss the charges pending against her of disorderly conduct and parading without a permit.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the information was facially insufficient. The trial court held that the disorderly conduct charge was sufficiently pled. The information alleged that the deponent observed defendant behind a police barricade with over 100 other individuals, that the conduct caused a public inconvenience by obstructing traffic, that defendant intended to cause a public inconvenience, and that the conduct recklessly created a risk of public inconvenience. The information also sufficiently charged a parading without a permit violation. The deponent observed defendant walking with over 100 other individuals on a public street. Neither the deponent nor defendant had a written permit authorizing a parade. The non-hearsay element was met in both instances as deponent observed defendant's conduct and signed the information. Finally, defendant's prior restraint challenge to New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 10-110 failed as, when read in conjunction with New York City, N.Y., R. tit. 38, § 19-04, the ordinance was content neutral and specified the reasons a permit could be denied, required explanations for a denial, and placed time limits on the processing of applications.


OUTCOME: The motion to dismiss was denied.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Information -- Facial Sufficiency -- Disorderly Conduct and Parading without Permit 

1. An accusatory instrument charging defendant with disorderly conduct (Penal Law § 240.20 [5]) and parading without a permit (Administrative Code of City of NY § 10-110) arising out of her alleged participation in unlawful protest demonstrations with a large group of persons was facially sufficient. The factual allegations that defendant caused a public inconvenience by obstructing pedestrian and vehicular traffic and lacked a required permit were based upon the arresting officer's observations of defendant's conduct, and were thus sufficient to establish reasonable cause to believe that defendant committed both offenses.

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Parading without Permit 

2. A content-neutral municipal ordinance that proscribes parading without a permit (Administrative Code of City of NY § 10-110) is not invalid as constituting an impermissible prior restraint on constitutionally protected freedoms of speech and assembly when read in conjunction with the administrative rules governing the permit application process (see 38 RCNY 19-04). The rules comport with constitutional requirements by plainly indicating the permissible grounds for permit denial, requiring explanations for denial and placing time limits on the processing of permit applications. Consequently, defendant could lawfully be prosecuted for parading without a permit based upon her alleged participation in unlawful protest demonstrations during the 2004 Republican National Convention.

 

1566 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Vasquez


4943/97


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


7 Misc. 3d 762; 795 N.Y.S.2d 820; 2005 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 285; 2005 NY Slip Op 25065; 233 N.Y.L.J. 15



January 18, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal granted by People v. Vasquez, 2006 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6739 (1st Dept, May 18, 2006)



DISPOSITION: Defendant's motion denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Long after the Court of Appeals of New York had denied leave to appeal a judgment convicting defendant of first-degree manslaughter as a juvenile offender, defendant moved to vacate the judgment of conviction pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 440.10 on grounds that admission of certain evidence at his trial violated his right to confrontation, as articulated by the United States Supreme Court in Crawford v. Washington.


OVERVIEW: At defendant's trial, the court had scrupulously applied the then-applicable Roberts test and determined that a co-defendant's testimony at her own plea allocution was admissible as a statement against penal interest with sufficient guarantees of trustworthiness to allow the jury to consider it. Under the rule announced years later in Crawford, that evidence would not have been admissible because the co-defendant had not been subject to cross-examination. Defendant argued that even though his conviction was final, he could collaterally attack it as obtained in violation of his constitutional rights. The court determined that although the confrontation right was fundamental, Crawford simply prescribed a new, preferable method of effectuating it, so it was procedural rather than substantive. Procedural rules only applied retroactively where a watershed right was involved and where the failure to apply the new rule seriously diminished the likelihood of an accurate judgment. There was no basis whatsoever for arguing that the admission of the co-defendant's evidence, after the original court's searching inquiry, had affected the accuracy of the outcome, so there was no retroactivity.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion to vacate in its entirety.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Right of Confrontation -- Retroactive Application of Crawford Rule on Collateral Review

Defendant was not entitled to the vacatur of his 1999 manslaughter conviction pursuant to CPL 440.10 on the ground that the introduction of his codefendant's plea allocution on the prosecution's direct case as a declaration against penal interest hearsay exception violated defendant's right of confrontation under the rule enunciated in Crawford v Washington (541 U.S. 36, 124 S. Ct. 1354, 158 L. Ed. 2d 177 [2004]). Defendant's conviction became final on direct review prior to the Crawford decision. The Crawford rule, which altered the manner in which the admissibility of testimonial statements is to be determined when the declarant is unavailable to testify at trial, constitutes a new procedural rule that does not apply retroactively on collateral review to cases already final on direct review. Collateral retroactive application of a new constitutional criminal procedural rule is not required unless following the invalidated former rule would have seriously diminished the likelihood of an accurate conviction. Here, the admission of the codefendant's plea allocution did not result in an impermissibly large risk that defendant was wrongfully convicted.

 

1567 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Barrow


2004BX039063


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, BRONX COUNTY


6 Misc. 3d 945; 791 N.Y.S.2d 906; 2005 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 38; 2005 NY Slip Op 25009; 233 N.Y.L.J. 9



January 5, 2005, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Case determined to be improperly before the Supreme Court; returned to the assigning court for proper disposition.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to dismiss, pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 170.30(i)(f), alleging a jurisdictional or legal impediment to his potential conviction in the supreme court on misdemeanor charges arising from a criminal court information.


OVERVIEW: The complaint charging defendant with misdemeanors was deemed an information at arraignment, transferred to the criminal court, and transferred to the newly-created criminal division of the supreme court pursuant to a rule of the chief administrator, which was promulgated by the administrative judge. The court concluded that pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law §§ 10.20, 10.30, and 210.05, the case was improperly before the supreme court and should have been returned to the assigning court for disposition. The court held that while the administrative judge had the general power to restructure the county court system, it could not deviate from the legislature's clear intent in delineating the roles of the criminal and supreme courts. The court held that it could not construe N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law §§ 10.20, 10.30, and 210.05 as placing unconstitutional limitations on its jurisdiction; that they were arguably not jurisdictional at all but merely regulatory of the manner of prosecuting misdemeanors; and that they were part of a legitimate legislative plan to reconcile the operation of the criminal court with the supreme court's constitutionally-mandated jurisdiction.


OUTCOME: The court ruled that the case was improperly before it and should have been returned to the assigning court for disposition.


HEADNOTES

Courts -- Transfer to Other Court -- Trial Jurisdiction of Supreme Court over Criminal Action Transferred from Lower Criminal Court 

Supreme Court lacked trial jurisdiction over misdemeanor charges that had been transferred from the local criminal court to the newly-created Criminal Division of Bronx County Supreme Court pursuant to the court rule promulgated on September 21, 2004 (22 NYCRR part 142). Pursuant to that rule, after November 5, 2004 all felony and misdemeanor cases in the Criminal Court of Bronx County were transferred to the Criminal Division of Bronx County Supreme Court. However, notwithstanding that rule, CPL 10.20, 10.30 and 210.05 bar Supreme Court from trying an offense that has not been presented to it in the form of an indictment or superior court information. Those statutes do not place unconstitutional limitations on the jurisdiction of the Supreme Court. Rather, the statutory scheme merely regulates the manner of prosecuting misdemeanor and petty offenses before the Supreme Court and should be construed as being part of a legitimate legislative plan to delineate the functions of the constitutionally created New York City Criminal Court and to reconcile its operation with the constitutionally mandated jurisdiction of the Supreme Court.

 

1568 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Henriques


2594/97


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


7 Misc. 3d 453; 794 N.Y.S.2d 569; 2005 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 17; 2005 NY Slip Op 25005; 233 N.Y.L.J. 12



January 5, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal dismissed by People v. Henriques, 2006 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 14567 (N.Y. App. Div. 2d Dep't, Dec. 5, 2006)



DISPOSITION: Defendant's motion to vacate his probationary sentence denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant moved to vacate his probationary sentence pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 440.20; defendant claimed that his jail time credit exceeded his probation sentence.


OVERVIEW: After defendant was convicted of depraved indifference murder, he pleaded guilty to an unrelated narcotics charge. When the murder conviction was reversed, defendant was convicted of criminally negligent homicide on retrial and sentenced to five years probation. The court held that jail time served on the reversed conviction could not be applied to the probationary sentence based upon N.Y. Penal Law § 65.15(1). Since the probation sentence was legal, the court lacked authority under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 440.20 and N.Y. Penal Law § 70.30 to alter it. In any event, defendant might not be entitled to the full seven-year credit since three of those years were served under the narcotics sentence.


OUTCOME: The motion was denied.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Sentence -- Probation -- Jail Time Credit under Reversed Conviction 

Jail time defendant served on a reversed depraved indifference murder conviction could not, pursuant to Penal Law § 65.15 (1), be applied to the five-year probationary sentence imposed upon his subsequent conviction of criminally negligent homicide. Consequently, the probationary sentence was legal and not subject to vacatur under CPL 440.20. The failure to provide defendant with any jail time credit against his probationary sentence did not render the statutory scheme unconstitutional as applied in view of the substantive differences between a probationary sentence and a sentence of imprisonment. The primary purpose of a probation sentence is to restore offenders to society and assist them to function productively, while the function of a sentence of incarceration is to punish and remove offenders from society. Since defendant needed counseling and supervision, a probationary sentence was necessary for his future well-being.
