Matter of M./B. Children


B-27601-05/02


FAMILY COURT OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


7 Misc. 3d 272; 792 N.Y.S.2d 785; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2979; 2004 NY Slip Op 24554; 233 N.Y.L.J. 7



December 22, 2004, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent unwed father moved to dismiss a petition filed by petitioner child services agency seeking to terminate the parental rights of the parents, with notice to the father under N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 384-c and N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 111(1)(d).


OVERVIEW: The father argued that § 111(1)(d) was unconstitutional as applied to him. The trial court held that while the father's name was not on any of the birth certificates, he had obtained orders of filiation for the four eldest children. The father was involved with the four older children's daily care, even before he obtained custody. The children were in the father's sole custody for 11 months. When custody was transferred to the grandmother, the father was granted visitation rights. He had telephone contact with his children while he was in prison and visited them after his release. To impose the requirements in § 111(1)(d) to the father would constitute the denial of equal protection based on sex and marital status. Section 111(1)(d) was unconstitutional as applied to the father and his four older children. The father's relationship with a fifth child was not so substantial as to create parental rights. The father might lose his parental rights if the agency proved that he had abandoned or permanently neglected the children, but the burden was on the agency to prove a basis for terminating the father's rights. The father did not have a burden to establish his right to consent.


OUTCOME: The father's motion to dismiss the petition was granted as to the four older children. The motion was denied as to the fifth child.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Adoption -- Consent -- Rights of Unwed Father -- Constitutionality of Domestic Relations Law § 111 (1) (d) as Applied 

In a proceeding to terminate respondent father's parental rights to his four out-of-wedlock children (see Social Services Law § 384-b), respondent demonstrated that Domestic Relations Law § 111 (1) (d), which sets forth the criteria necessary for an unwed father's consent to be required for the adoption of his children, is unconstitutional as applied to him to the extent that the statute would only entitle him to notice and an opportunity to be heard as to the best interests of the children. Respondent, who obtained orders of filiation for his children and had sole custody of them prior to his incarceration from 1998 to 2003, did not satisfy the statutory requirements for a "consent father" of providing financial support and either visiting or communicating with the children on a regular basis. However, respondent otherwise demonstrated a relationship with his children that was sufficiently "substantial" or "significant" to merit constitutional protection of his rights. Granting the right to consent to married fathers without having to demonstrate a "substantial" or "significant" relationship to their children, while imposing such a requirement on unwed fathers, discriminates against men who cannot unilaterally change their married/unmarried status, and constitutes the denial of equal protection based on sex and marital status.

 

1570 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Karen W. v. Roger S.


XXX


FAMILY COURT OF NEW YORK, DUTCHESS COUNTY


8 Misc. 3d 285; 793 N.Y.S.2d 693; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3052; 2004 NY Slip Op 24563; 233 N.Y.L.J. 30



December 22, 2004, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent father moved to dismiss petitioner mother's custody action; the father claimed, inter alia, that the provisions of N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 75(d), as applied to him, violated his constitutional rights.


OVERVIEW: The mother was a United States citizen, and the father was a German citizen. The older child had lived in New York for one year, and the younger child was born in New York and had lived there his entire life. The father was personally served in New York. Although a divorce action had been filed in Germany, it was filed a month after the mother's custody petition. The court held that New York was clearly the home state of the children. Because the Uniform Child Custody Jurisdiction Enforcement Act (UCCJEA), N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 76, applied fully to international cases, New York had subject matter jurisdiction. The father was not enticed into the jurisdiction by fraud and deceit for the purpose of obtaining service upon him. The father had actual notice of the proceedings and would have an opportunity to be heard. Consequently, no procedural due process claim existed. No substantive due process claim existed either because the UCCJEA rationally and neutrally determined which forum was most appropriate to hear the custody issue.


OUTCOME: The motion to dismiss was denied, and the matter was set down for further proceedings.


HEADNOTES

Parent and Child -- Custody -- Uniform Child Custody Jurisdiction and Enforcement Act -- Constitutionality of Act as Applied to Resident of Foreign Country 

In a custody proceeding, Family Court obtained personal jurisdiction over respondent father, a resident of Germany, by petitioner mother's personal service upon him in New York while he was in this state on business and to visit his children. New York, as the "home state" of the parties' two children, had subject matter jurisdiction under the Uniform Child Custody Jurisdiction Enforcement Act (UCCJEA) to make an initial custody decision (see Domestic Relations Law § 76 [1]). Furthermore, the UCCJEA, was not unconstitutional as applied to respondent. The exercise of jurisdiction would not deprive respondent of his parental rights without due process. Respondent had actual notice of the proceeding and will have an opportunity to be heard. If respondent chooses not to come to New York, due process would not be offended by requiring him to proceed in accordance with the UCCJEA by either appearing through his attorney and testifying telephonically or by audiovisual means, or by testifying in a German court and having the transcript forwarded to Family Court (see Domestic Relations Law §§ 75-j, 75-k).

 

1571 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Chantel R.


0528/03


SURROGATE'S COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


6 Misc. 3d 693; 791 N.Y.S.2d 324; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2839; 2004 NY Slip Op 24533



December 17, 2004, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Later proceeding at In re Chantel R., 2005 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13941 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, Dec. 6, 2005)
Later proceeding at In re R., 2006 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 130 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, Jan. 3, 2006)
Later proceeding at In re Chantel R., 2006 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3927 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, Mar. 28, 2006)



DISPOSITION: Petition for guardianship granted. Amended letters of guardianship issued.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner mother sought to be appointed under N.Y. Surr. Ct. Proc. Act art. 17-A as guardian of her mentally retarded adult daughter. Respondent activist entity objected to the appointment of the mother to the extent that the guardianship included the authority under the 2002 New York Health Care Decisions Act for Persons with Mental Retardation (HCDA), N.Y. Surr. Ct. Proc. Act Law § 1750-b, to withhold or withdraw life sustaining treatment.


OVERVIEW: After the court determined that the daughter was incapable of making her own health care decisions, the entity alleged that the HCDA was unconstitutional. The court held that because N.Y. Surr. Ct. Proc. Act Law § 1750(2) required the court to determine the daughter's incapacity to make health care decisions before appointing a guardian, and because the court held a hearing, the HCDA did not violate due process. Because the daughter did not understand the nature and consequences of end-of-life decisions, the HCDA did not violate her equal protection rights by not allowing her to make her own irrational health care or end-of-life decisions although persons capable of understanding such decisions had a right to make irrational decisions. Because N.Y. Surr. Ct. Proc. Act Law § 1750-b(4)(b)(ii) imposed an "extraordinary burden" standard on a guardian before life support could be withdrawn, and because a guardian had other statutory duties, the HCDA was not unconstitutionally vague. Because the daughter was incompetent, did not lodge a competent objection to the appointment of a guardian, and trusted her mother, the mother was entitled to be appointed guardian in all respects.


OUTCOME: The court granted the mother's petition for guardianship in its entirety.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Guardianship of Mentally Retarded Persons -- Health Care Decisions -- Termination of Life Support 

SCPA 1750-b, which authorizes the guardian for a mentally retarded person to make "any and all" health care decisions for such person, including decisions to withhold and withdraw life-sustaining treatment, is not violative of due process or otherwise unconstitutional. Under the statute, a finding by the court of incapacity to make health decisions is a condition precedent to granting a guardian authority to make those decisions on behalf of a mentally retarded person. The test imposed by the statute in determining incapacity--whether a mentally retarded person facing end-of-life decisions is capable of understanding the medical choices available and the potential consequences of the decisions made--does not violate equal protection principles. The court's authority to deny the appointment of a guardian and the statutory standard to which the court is held in making the appointment offer sufficient due process protection to an incapacitated objecting ward. Furthermore, the statute's standard for the termination of life support is not unconstitutionally vague. The statute requires the guardian to make a thorough analysis of all aspects of the patient's medical condition, prognosis and expressed wishes before permitting the rejection of life-sustaining treatment.

 

1572 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Hauptman v. Elmira Hgts


2004-1567


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, CHEMUNG COUNTY


23 Misc. 3d 439; 876 N.Y.S.2d 314; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3179; 2004 NY Slip Op 24572



December 16, 2004, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent village made a motion, pursuant to CPLR § 7804(f), for an order dismissing a CPLR art. 78 petition filed by petitioner, a former chief of police, seeking separation pay pursuant to General Municipal Law § 207-m and seeking damages for violation of his civil rights under 42 U.S.C.S. §§ 1983 and 1988.


OVERVIEW: The chief notified the village of his intention to retire on January 30, 2004. Prior to his retirement, the chief requested separation pay for 120 days of unused sick time, 2 weeks of unused vacation time, and 471 hours of compensation time earned before his promotion to chief; no separation pay was given to the chief. The court granted the motion to dismiss in part and denied it in part. The benefits at issue were not specifically referred to in § 207-m and had not been specifically excluded from consideration as salary or other compensation; thus, the chief could pursue his claim as the benefits sought fell within the expansive definition of other compensation. The benefits had been earned during the course of the chief's employment and had been afforded to the chief's subordinates and his predecessor. However, the chief failed to establish a substantive due process violation as while he had a cognizable property interest under § 207-m, the village's conduct was not so egregious as to implicate constitutional law as it was arguable as to whether the subject benefits fell within the scope of the statute.


OUTCOME: The court denied the village's motion to dismiss the chief's claim under § 207-m but granted the motion to dismiss as to the § 1983 claim of denial of due process. The court also granted the motion to dismiss as to the § 1983 claim of denial of equal protection rights but did so without prejudice to service of an amended petition setting forth factual allegations supporting that claim.


HEADNOTES

Public Officers -- Compensation and Benefits -- Village Police Chief

1. Accrued unused compensatory, vacation and sick time earned by a retiring police chief when he was a patrolman and sergeant in a collective bargaining unit, and unused vacation time earned as chief, were benefits within the expansive definition of "other compensation" contained in General Municipal Law § 207-m, which was enacted to prevent salary "compression" between the heads of police departments and certain of their subordinates who were members of collective bargaining units. "Other compensation" is defined by way of a noninclusive list of benefits encompassing most employer fringe benefits; other benefits such as overtime, holiday and shift differential pay are specifically excluded unless a municipality affirmatively elects to include them within a police chief's employment benefit package. The fact that the benefits in question were earned prior to the chief's promotion or that such benefits afforded his subordinates were not increased following the promotion did not exclude them from the ambit of the statute. Excluding such benefits would be contrary to the purpose of the statute.

Parties -- Standing -- Claim for Separation Pay by Village Police Chief

2. A village police chief seeking separation pay based on accrued unused compensatory, vacation and sick time earned while a patrolman and sergeant in a collective bargaining unit had standing to assert a claim under General Municipal Law § 207-m, which was enacted to prevent salary "compression" between the heads of police departments and certain of their subordinates who were members of collective bargaining units. The provisions of the statute were applicable to the chief's claim and it was undisputed that petitioner held the position of Chief of Police at the time of his retirement. Accordingly, he qualified as a member of the class specifically protected by the statute and was entitled to seek redress thereunder.

Limitation of Actions -- Four-Month Statute of Limitations -- Claim for Separation Pay

3. A proceeding brought by a retiring village police chief seeking separation pay based on accrued unused compensatory, vacation and sick time earned prior to his promotion to chief was not barred by the applicable four-month statute of limitations under CPLR 217, since the chief's claim for his retirement/separation package benefits did not accrue until such time as he was denied such benefits. Petitioner retired in January 2004 and did not receive written confirmation from the respondent that his request for separation pay had been rejected until February 5, 2004. Even if January 7, 2004, the date the petitioner first received written notice from the respondent of a reduced separation package, were deemed the date of accrual, the commencement of the proceeding on May 3, 2004 was timely.

Constitutional Law -- Due Process of Law -- Police Chief's Claim for Separation Pay

4. A municipality's denial of its retiring police chief's request for certain separation pay based on accrued unused compensatory, vacation and sick time earned prior to his promotion to chief did not constitute an unconstitutional violation of the chief's substantive due process rights. Although the chief had a cognizable property interest in his separation pay, he failed to demonstrate that the municipality's action was wholly without legal justification, since the subject of the chief's claim was not specifically referred to or identified by General Municipal Law § 207-m, upon which the chief's right to his separation pay arose. It was arguable whether the claimed benefits fell within the scope of the statute. Therefore, the municipality's conduct was not so egregious as to implicate constitutional law.

People v. Barton


04-12665


CITY COURT OF NEW YORK, ROCHESTER


8 Misc. 3d 291; 795 N.Y.S.2d 423; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3058; 2004 NY Slip Op 24565



December 14, 2004, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
Reversed by People v. Barton, 12 Misc. 3d 322, 816 N.Y.S.2d 853, 2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 586 (2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to dismiss a charge against him of aggressive panhandling, in violation of Rochester, N.Y., Code § 44-4-H.


OVERVIEW: Defendant walked into traffic on the inner loop of an exit ramp and solicited money from motorists. He was charged with violating § 44-4-H, and he sought dismissal, claiming that the ordinance was unconstitutional as violative of U.S. Const. amend. I and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 8. The court initially determined that defendant had direct and vicarious standing to raise the constitutional challenges. The court held that § 44-4-H was unconstitutional, in that it was not narrowly tailored to serve the significant government interest of promoting the safety and convenience of its citizens on public streets. Rather, the ordinance allowed for the prosecution of those who were guilty of passive solicitation, such as peacefully asking for assistance. The court compared the ordinance herein to others which were struck down in similar situations. Accordingly, the court did not review whether alternative means of communication were provided in the ordinance, or whether defendant's other challenges had merit.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's motion to dismiss.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Freedom of Speech -- Aggressive Panhandling Ordinance 

A municipal "aggressive" panhandling ordinance that prohibits any "person on a sidewalk or alongside a roadway" from soliciting "from any occupant of a motor vehicle that is on a street or other public place" unconstitutionally infringed on freedom of speech and was thus invalid under the overbreadth doctrine. The ordinance, although content-neutral, was not "narrowly tailored" to achieve the governmental purpose of promoting the safety of motorists and pedestrians using public streets and highways. By prohibiting passive solicitations from the sidewalk, the ordinance had a chilling effect on expressive behavior that was not directed toward motorists and also impermissibly threatened individuals engaged in nondisruptive solicitation with the possibility of incarceration.

Mayor of City of New York v. Council of City of New York


405082/01


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


6 Misc. 3d 533; 789 N.Y.S.2d 860; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2359; 2004 NY Slip Op 24475



November 30, 2004, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Local Law 20 of 2001 declared void and enforcement permanently enjoined.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff Mayor of the City of New York filed a motion for summary judgment in the Mayor's action that sought a declaration that New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 6-124 was invalid. Defendant Council of the City of New York filed a cross motion for summary judgment, seeking dismissal of the Mayor's complaint.


OVERVIEW: New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 6-124, enacted by the Council without a referendum, sought to prevent the City of New York from purchasing uniforms manufactured in sweatshops, either in New York, or in other states or countries. To that end, § 6-124 barred the City's contracting agencies from purchasing apparel or textiles from any entity other than a responsible manufacturer. When presented with information indicating that a contractor or subcontractor was violating the law, the New York City Comptroller had to determine whether there was a violation. Upon finding a violation, the Comptroller had to present evidence of the violation to the contracting agency, which then had to take appropriate action. The New York City Mayor's authority over the award of City of New York contracts, New York City, N.Y., Charter § 335, was almost exclusive. Because New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 6-124 gave the Comptroller the authority to award contracts that previously resided with the Mayor, New York City, N.Y., Charter § 38(5) and N.Y. Mun. Home Rule Law § 23(2)(f) required New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 6-124 to be submitted for a referendum.


OUTCOME: The Mayor's motion was granted.


HEADNOTES

Local Laws -- Validity -- Separation of Powers -- Apparel Procurement -- Impermissible Curtailment of Mayor's Authority over Award of City Contracts 

1. A local law of the City of New York that barred the City's contracting agencies from purchasing apparel or textiles from any entity other than a "responsible manufacturer" in order to prevent the City from purchasing uniforms manufactured in sweatshops (Administrative Code of City of NY § 6-124), was invalid in the absence of a referendum of the City's voters because it impermissibly curtailed the Mayor's executive authority over the award of city contracts (Municipal Home Rule Law § 23 [2] [f]; New York City Charter §§ 38, 335). The local law adopted a different definition of "responsible" from that intended by the City Charter and generally accepted in municipal contract law. The local law thus violated the separation of powers doctrine by impermissibly encroaching on the Mayor's function and the discretion of the municipal agencies vested with the duty to determine whether a bidder is "responsible."

Local Laws -- Preemption by State -- Apparel Procurement 

2. A local law of the City of New York that barred the City's contracting agencies from purchasing apparel or textiles from any entity other than a "responsible manufacturer" in order to prevent the City from purchasing uniforms manufactured in sweatshops (Administrative Code of City of NY § 6-124), was preempted by a state law that occupied the apparel procurement field (State Finance Law § 162 [4-a]). The state law imposes conditions on prospective vendors for participating in state contracts. The statute affords municipalities the option of either adopting the state requirements in their own bid specifications for the purchase of garments or textiles or having no requirements. The state law explicitly supercedes any inconsistent local laws (State Finance Law §162 [9]), and thus leaves no room for alternative requirements in local bid specifications.

 

1578 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Mazyck


6990/03


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


6 Misc. 3d 209; 787 N.Y.S.2d 843; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2267; 2004 NY Slip Op 24456



November 18, 2004, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Motion to dismiss denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Grand Jury indicted defendant on one charge of robbery in the first degree under N.Y. Penal Law § 160.15(3). Defendant moved to dismiss the charge on the grounds that the indictment was repugnant and therefore defective under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 210.20(1)(a).


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that the Grand Jury was inconsistent because the People presented it with the option of indicting him on the lesser included charges that contained the same elements as robbery in the first degree, but the Grand Jury indicted him only upon robbery in the first degree. Defendant contended that the rules regarding repugnant verdicts at trial were to apply to the Grand Jury. The trial court stated that if this was a petit jury, this inconsistency would have been grounds for setting aside the verdict. However, the laws governing the functions and duties of the Grand Jury and the petit jury were necessarily different. What appeared to be a repugnancy at the indictment stage could simply have been a permissible decision that the Grand Jury made, for whatever reason, including the exercise of leniency, to indict on only one count.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss the charge.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Indictment -- Doctrine of Repugnancy Inapplicable to Grand Jury Process 

An indictment in which the grand jury found sufficient evidence for the charge of robbery in the first degree, but did not indict defendant on lesser included charges that contained the same elements, was not subject to dismissal under the doctrine of repugnancy. The rules regarding repugnant verdicts at trial do not apply to the grand jury, whose function is different than that of a petit jury. A grand jury, whose purpose is to determine whether there is sufficient evidence to establish the material elements of a crime, has discretion to indict for one crime, but not for a lesser included charge.

 

1579 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Lombardo v. County of Nassau


8387/04


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NASSAU COUNTY


6 Misc. 3d 836; 791 N.Y.S.2d 292; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2960; 2004 NY Slip Op 24553; 233 N.Y.L.J. 2



November 18, 2004, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Plaintiff's motion to amend the Notice of Claim denied; Defendants' motion to dismiss the Notice of Claim as untimely denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff vehicle owner sued defendant county and its police department for seizing and retaining his vehicle pursuant to a county ordinance which was found to be unconstitutional. The owner moved to amend his notice of claim pursuant to N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 50-e(6), and the county cross-moved to dismiss the notice of claim pursuant to N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 50-e(1)(a).


OVERVIEW: The ordinance under which the vehicle was seized was held unconstitutional. His claim did not arise when the vehicle was seized as the ordinance then precluded recovery. It was unreasonable to make the owner immediately challenge the ordinance's constitutionality or lose any recovery. The ordinance was void ab initio, so the seizure and retention were unlawful when committed. The claim's accrual was delayed, as the owner's right to sue depended on the jural event of an appellate court finding the ordinance unconstitutional. His claim had to be filed within 90 days of that decision, notwithstanding an appeal, but the ordinance's uncertain status, given the county's appeal, and a question as to when his claim arose, gave a reasonable excuse for the claim's delay. The county knew the claim's facts, and was no more prejudiced than it would have been had the claim been timely. The owner's proposed amendment corrected no mistake, but sought different damages, requiring different evidence, and substantially altered the claim by stating theories of which the county had no adequate notice. The proposed amendment was prejudicial and outside the purview of N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 50-e(6).


OUTCOME: The owner's motion to amend his notice of claim was denied, and the county's motion to dismiss the notice of claim was denied.


HEADNOTES

Limitation of Actions -- When Cause of Action Accrues -- Claim Accrues upon Declaration That Law is Invalid Despite Pending Appeal 

In an action by plaintiff seeking recovery for property damage and costs incurred as a result of defendant County's seizure and retention of plaintiff's vehicle pursuant to a county forfeiture ordinance, defendant was not entitled to a dismissal, pursuant to General Municipal Law § 50-e (1) (a), of plaintiff's untimely notice of claim, filed almost 5 years after the seizure, 11 months after an Appellate Division decision declaring the ordinance unconstitutional, and almost 3 months after the Court of Appeals affirmance of that decision. A claim based on the unlawful infringement of an individual right by a government entity acting under color of law accrues when it is judicially determined that the law is invalid. Here, the claim accrued upon the determination by the Appellate Division that the law was invalid, despite the appeal of that decision. Although the notice of claim was untimely, a late notice of claim was allowed pursuant to General Municipal Law § 50-e (5). The uncertain status of the ordinance constituted a reasonable excuse for the eight-month delay, and the defendant, having custody of the vehicle, had knowledge of the facts constituting plaintiff's claim and was not prejudiced by the delay.

People v. Norman


6435/03


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


5 Misc. 3d 1016(A); 798 N.Y.S.2d 712; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2274; 2004 NY Slip Op 51392(U)



November 16, 2004, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] THIS OPINION IS UNCORRECTED AND WILL NOT BE PUBLISHED IN THE PRINTED OFFICIAL REPORTS.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Later proceeding at People v. Norman, 6 Misc. 3d 1035[A], 800 N.Y.S.2d 353, 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3029 (2004)



DISPOSITION: Defendant's motion to dismiss the indictment denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, who was a state assemblyman, challenged the grand jury proceedings, among other things, and sought dismissal of an indictment charging him with grand larceny, N.Y. Penal Law § 155.35; falsifying business records, N.Y. Penal Law § 175.10; offering a false instrument for filing, N.Y. Penal Law § 175.35; and criminal liability under N.Y. Elec. Law § 126(2) for violations of N.Y. Elec. Law §§ 14-122, 14-102, and 14-130.


OVERVIEW: A committee had been formed to receive contributions and make expenditures on behalf of defendant's re-election campaign. The grand jury evidence established that defendant came into possession of a check in the amount of $ 5,000 and payable to the committee, but that defendant deposited the check in his personal checking account. The court first held that because the evidence was sufficient to establish that defendant stole the check from the committee, it was also legally sufficient to establish that he failed to report it to the treasurer, caused her to fail to report it to the Board of Elections, and converted its proceeds to his unlawful personal use, acting "knowingly and willfully." In addition, defendant failed to show prosecutorial abuse for refusal to grant immunity to defendant's witness. However, the indictment failed to adequately allege that defendant acted "knowingly and willfully" under N.Y. Elec. Law § 14-126 and, thus, was insufficient with respect to the counts alleging violations of the Election Laws. And, finally, the court held that the interests of justice did not require dismissal of the indictment under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 210.40.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed one count charging defendant with falsifying the business records and the counts charging defendant with Election Law violations, but it denied the motion to dismiss as to the remaining counts of the indictment and denied defendant's other motions with limited exceptions.


HEADNOTES

 [**712]  [*1016A] Crimes--Larceny. Crimes--Indictment--Sufficiency of Evidence before Grand Jury.

B&L Assocs. v. Wakefield


50296/04


CIVIL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


6 Misc. 3d 388; 785 N.Y.S.2d 681; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2361; 2004 NY Slip Op 24473



November 3, 2004, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Petitioner granted a judgment of possession.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landlord sought to evict respondent tenant based on the tenant's drug-related activity.


OVERVIEW: After the police executed a "no knock" search warrant at the premises, they found "twisties" of crack cocaine, a gun, and ammunition. The packaging of the crack cocaine in "twisties" and the number of "twisties" and their location indicated that they were being held for distribution and not for personal consumption. The lease provided in pertinent part that any drug-related criminal activity at the premises by a guest or other person under the tenant's control was a ground for terminating the lease. The court held that the landlord proved a prima facie case that there was drug-related criminal activity at the premises. The tenant was bound by her lease. Her tenancy was subject to the "no-fault" standard of 42 U.S.C.S. § 1437d(l)(6). Her defense that she did not know of the drug-related activity and did not have reason to know of it was not cognizable. Therefore, the landlord was entitled to a judgment of possession under N.Y. Real Prop. Acts. Law § 753.


OUTCOME: The court grants petitioner a judgment of possession.


HEADNOTES

Public Housing - Eviction of Nondesirable Tenant - Drug-Related Criminal Activity by Guest of Tenant 

Respondent tenant was properly evicted from her section 8 housing apartment for violating the lease provision that prohibited drug-related criminal activity by a guest or other person under the tenant's control. Under 42 USC §1437d (l) (6), a public housing lease must provide a ground for eviction for drug-related activity of a tenant or the tenant's household members or guests, regardless of whether the tenant is aware of the activity. Here, a clear showing of drug-related activity was made by credible testimony and evidence that respondent had an invited guest and drugs and ammunition in her apartment, and that after being arrested at respondent's apartment the guest pleaded guilty to attempted possession of a controlled substance. Respondent's claim that she was unaware of the drug-related activity was immaterial.

 

1583 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Skya


750N-04


COUNTY COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


6 Misc. 3d 188; 789 N.Y.S.2d 403; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2158; 2004 NY Slip Op 24442



October 29, 2004, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Later proceeding at People v. Skya, 2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4005 (2006)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendant's motion granted in part and denied in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was indicted on five counts related to attempts and the actual dissemination of indecent material to minors. Defendant filed various pre-trial motions, including dismissal of the indictment, a motion for a bill of particulars, motion for discovery, motion for suppression of statements, inspection and release of grand jury minutes, a Sandoval hearing, and a reservation of rights for further motions.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the four counts of attempted dissemination of indecent material to minors needed to be dismissed because the elements of the crime as set forth under N.Y. Penal Law § 235.22 required the transmittal of "depictions." Defendant contended that the use of the word "depict" in the statute required the transmittal of a pictorial image and not just words, as allegedly used by defendant. The court held that conversation alone, which described sexual conduct, was sufficient to allege a prohibition of N.Y. Penal Law § 235.22. Section 235.22 clearly put an offender on notice that sexually oriented computer communication with a minor for the purpose of inducing or inviting that child to engage in sexual conduct with an adult was prohibited. Defendant's motion for a bill of particulars was denied because it did not comply with the procedure set forth in N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 240.40. There were no statements made by defendant that the state intended to use at trial, and no hearing on the motion to suppress was warranted. The grand jury proceedings were not defective, and there were no grounds for releasing the minutes to defendant, based on the court's in camera review.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss the indictment, denied the motion for a bill of particulars, denied the motion for discovery, denied the motion to suppress statements, denied the motion for release of the grand jury proceedings, outlined the procedures needed for a Sandoval hearing, denied the motion for a hearing request on the arrest, and granted the motion for a hearing on the search of the home.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Disseminating Indecent Material to Minors -- Sexually Explicit On-Line Communications without Pictures 

Defendant, who allegedly had sexually explicit conversations with an undercover police officer posing as a 14-year-old boy via computer messages, was not entitled to a dismissal of the counts of the indictment charging him with attempted dissemination of indecent material to a minor in the first degree in violation of Penal Law § 235.22 merely because the communications did not include pictorial images of sexual conduct. Penal Law § 235.22 prohibits the use of a computer to engage a minor in communication that depicts actual or simulated nudity, sexual conduct or sadomasochistic abuse with the intent to invite or induce such minor to engage in sexual contact with the offender. The word "depict," as used in the statute, is not limited to visual images, but rather includes words that describe sexual conduct.

Matter of M.B.


D


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NASSAU COUNTY

 [*1021A]  [**161] Crimes-Selective Prosecution. Infants--Juvenile Delinquents.

 

1585 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Avella v. Batt


5945-04


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, ALBANY COUNTY


6 Misc. 3d 158; 785 N.Y.S.2d 305; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2113; 2004 NY Slip Op 24433



October 14, 2004, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
Subsequent appeal at Matter of Avella v Batt, 820 N.Y.S.2d 332, 2006 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9459 (N.Y. App. Div. 3d Dep't, July 20, 2006)



DISPOSITION: Proceeding ruled upon; matter referred to the Albany County District Attorney and the New York State Attorney General for action.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, five enrolled voters, filed a special proceeding pursuant to N.Y. Elec. Law §§ 14-116 and 2-126, seeking a declaration that respondents, the state and a county board of elections and various political committees and entities, had engaged in illegal conduct. The voters also sought an order directing the return of contributions.


OVERVIEW: The voters contended that various respondents made contributions that were in excess of the corporate contribution limit, that campaign funds were illegally spent to aid a particular campaign, and that several respondents entered into a conspiracy for such illegal spending. The court initially found that the voters had standing, as they alleged violations of the legislative mandates of the New York Election Law. Further, N.Y. Elec. Law § 2-126 did not violate the voters' First Amendment rights, as they were still able to endorse a candidate's nomination and it protected an important governmental interest. The claim against a political action committee (PAC), even if there was jurisdiction over it, did not show that it was a corporation that was bound by the $ 5,000 limit, nor that it had exceeded the $ 150,000 limit for PACs. Further, there was no showing that the candidate or his committee engaged in illegal conduct. However, there was evidence that expenditures were made that violated N.Y. Elec. Law § 2-126 in attempting to encourage voters to elect the candidate. The court found that injunctive relief was moot and financial fines were within the board of elections' domain.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the claims against various respondents, including the PAC, the candidate, and his political committee. The court declared that expenditures were made in violation of the New York Election Law with respect to the primary election, and it forwarded the matter to the county district attorney's office for determination of what remedy was appropriate due to the violations.


HEADNOTES

Parties -- Standing -- Challenge to Legality of Campaign Contributions made in Primary Election 

1. Petitioners had standing, as qualified voters under Election Law § 16-114 (3), to challenge the campaign contributions made to aid a candidate in the 2004 Albany County Democratic primary for District Attorney as being violative of the $ 5,000 corporate contribution limit (Election Law § 14-116) and the prohibition against a political party making contributions to candidates "to be voted for at a primary election" (Election Law § 2-126). Petitioners' underlying challenge involved legislatively mandated requirements of the Election Law, and was not directed to the internal affairs or operating functions of any political party.

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Restrictions on Expenditures of Funds by Political Party 

2. Election Law § 2-126, which prohibits a political party from making contributions to candidates "to be voted for at a primary election," was not unconstitutional as applied to respondents to challenge the campaign contributions they made to aid a candidate in the 2004 Albany County Democratic primary for District Attorney. The statute does not unconstitutionally inhibit First Amendment rights or prohibit a party's endorsement of a candidate for nomination. The statute serves a substantial government interest in removing both actual corruption and the appearance thereof from the electoral process.

Elections -- Primary Elections -- Restrictions on Campaign Contributions 

3. Petitioners' challenge to the campaign contributions made to aid a candidate in a primary as being violative of the $ 5,000 corporate contribution limit (Election Law § 14-116) was dismissed, because they failed to demonstrate that respondent political action committee made any corporate donation in excess of $ 5,000. Furthermore, while respondent political party violated Election Law § 2-126 by illegally spending money to promote a candidate in the primary election, injunctive relief was not warranted. The expenditures had already been made and any injunctive relief with respect to the primary was moot. Moreover, attempting to restrain the party from financially supporting any candidate in the general election would have violated the First Amendment rights of the party members.

 

1586 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Patricia Ann Cottage Pub, Inc. v. Mermelstein


005806/2004


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, SUFFOLK COUNTY


6 Misc. 3d 492; 786 N.Y.S.2d 283; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2467; 2004 NY Slip Op 24488



October 13, 2004, Decided


NOTICE:

 [***1] THIS OPINION WAS VACATED BY THE COURT


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal dismissed by, Vacated by, Petition granted by, in part, Petition denied by, in part Matter of Patricia Ann Cottage Pub, Inc. v. Mermelstein, 2007 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 750 (N.Y. App. Div. 2d Dep't, Jan. 23, 2007)



DISPOSITION: Petition granted in part and denied in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, three bar/restaurants, commenced a proceeding under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78, seeking: 1) review of respondent board of health services' findings that each restaurant was guilty of violating N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 1399-o; 2) to vacate and annul the board's decision and order; and 3) to find the New York State "Clean Indoor Act" unconstitutional due to its vagueness as to the enforcement of N.Y. Pub. Health Law §§ 1399-o and -t.


OVERVIEW: The board held three separate administrative hearings and found each restaurant guilty of violating the Clean Indoor Act, N.Y. Pub. Health Law art. 13-E, based on testimony from an investigator that there was smoking in the establishments. Although the restaurants indicated that they had posted "No Smoking" signs and removed all ashtrays, the investigator indicated that there were liquid-filled cups that were used for cigarette butts. On review, the court found that the determination by the board was erroneous. It was undisputed that the restaurants had complied with the requirements of the sign posting. Further, the investigator had testified that when she entered each restaurant, patrons were already smoking. Accordingly, there was no indication whether the bartenders had notified the patrons to stop smoking before she entered the premises. The court concluded that the determination by the board was therefore arbitrary and capricious, and not supported by the evidence. The challenge as to the constitutionality of the Act was not reviewed, as there was no indication that the attorney general had been served with notice of the petition, as required by N.Y. Exec. Law § 71.


OUTCOME: The court granted the petition to the extent of vacating and annulling the board's findings of fact, decision, and order. The court denied the petition to the extent that it challenged the constitutionality of the Act, with leave to renew.


HEADNOTES

Health -- No Smoking Regulations -- Enforcement by Bars and Restaurants 

A determination that petitioners, bars and restaurants, violated the Clean Indoor Air Act (Public Health Law §1399-o) cannot be sustained. The act requires the owners of bars and food service establishments to post "No-Smoking" signs and to inform individuals who are smoking in an area where smoking is prohibited that they are in violation of the law. It is undisputed that "No-Smoking" signs were properly posted at petitioners' establishments. Therefore, the determination was based solely on the investigator's testimony that she observed people already smoking when she entered each establishment and that, during the 10 to 25 minutes she was in each establishment, she did not observe the bartenders take any action to prohibit smoking. However, inasmuch as the investigator was not present before the smokers began smoking, she had no actual knowledge of what action the bartenders may or may not have taken prior to her arrival. The mere fact that patrons continued to smoke in defiance of the law was not ipso facto evidence of the establishment's failure to comply with the law, as the law does not require that bar or restaurant owners refuse service to patrons who refuse to comply with the law.

 

1587 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Dittmer v. Terzian


7370/02


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, ROCKLAND COUNTY


6 Misc. 3d 590; 787 N.Y.S.2d 617; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2465; 2004 NY Slip Op 24492



October 1, 2004, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Plaintiffs' motion to preclude testimony by defendant's expert granted in part. Minor plaintiff's failure to wear helmet, as mandated by statute, cannot be used as a defense or in mitigation of minor plaintiff's damages. Minor plaintiff's failure to wear helmet may properly be considered in determining his parents' claims. Plaintiffs' application for unified trial denied. Plaintiffs' application to depose defendants' investigator granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff parents, individually and on behalf of their 14-year-old son, filed an action against defendants, a driver and a car company, which sought to recover for personal injuries allegedly sustained by their son when he was struck by the driver's car while roller blading. The parents sought an order precluding testimony from a defense expert during the liability phase of the trial.


OVERVIEW: The parents' 14-year-old son was roller blading without a helmet when he was struck by the driver's car. He suffered traumatic head injuries. The parents filed a personal injury suit, and defendants sought to introduce testimony from a doctor who was expected to testify that had the boy been wearing a helmet when his head struck the street, he would not have sustained head injuries. The court granted the parents' motion to preclude such testimony in the liability phase, finding that with respect to the boy's cause of action, his failure to have worn a helmet, as mandated by N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1238, could not be used in any way as a defense or in mitigation of his damages. The court found also that the statute was constitutional, noting that the legislature had a legitimate objective in enacting it, which was to protect children who were sustaining serious head injuries in alarming numbers with the increased popularity of in-line skating. Lastly, the court ruled that the boy's failure to wear a helmet could properly be considered by the jury in determining whether each parent had the right to recover in their claims for the boy's loss of services.


OUTCOME: The court granted the parents' motion for an order precluding testimony by a doctor during the liability phase as to preventable injuries and found that with respect to the boy's cause of action, his failure to have worn a helmet, as mandated by statute, could not be used in any way as a defense or in mitigation of his damages.


HEADNOTES

Trial -- Evidence -- Evidence of Child's Failure to Wear Safety Helmet while In-Line Skating Precluded 

1. In a personal injury action to recover damages for head injuries allegedly sustained by the infant plaintiff when he was struck by defendant's vehicle while he was roller blading on a street, defendant was precluded by statute from introducing into evidence during the liability phase the testimony of an expert biomechanical engineer relative to the infant plaintiff's failure to wear a helmet. Although persons under the age of 14 are required by law to wear safety helmets while using in-line skates (Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1238 [5-a]), the failure to comply with this statutory requirement "shall not constitute contributory negligence or assumption of risk, and shall not in any way bar, preclude or foreclose an action for personal injury or wrongful death by or on behalf of such person, nor in any way diminish or reduce the damages recoverable in any such action" (Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1238 [7]). Consequently, the infant plaintiff's failure to wear a helmet, as mandated by statute, could not be used in any way as a defense or in mitigation of his damages. However, the statute was not applicable to the parents' derivative action, and the jury was thus entitled to hear that evidence solely for the purpose of determining whether each parent had the right to recover for the loss of their child's services.

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Preclusion of Evidence of Child's Failure to Wear Safety Helmet while In-Line Skating 

2. In a personal injury action to recover damages for head injuries allegedly sustained by the infant plaintiff when he was struck by a vehicle while he was roller blading on a street, defendants failed to demonstrate that Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1238 (7), which provides that a child's failure to wear a safety helmet while in-line skating as required by law cannot be used in any way in a civil action as a defense or in mitigation of the child's damages, was unconstitutional as violative of due process. Defendants failed to demonstrate that the statute was not reasonably related to protecting children in the manner intended. The statutory requirement that persons under the age of 14 wear safety helmets while in-line skating (Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1238 [5-a]) serves the legitimate public purpose of protecting children from sustaining head injuries while engaging in that activity. It was within the Legislature's prerogative to limit the penalties for the failure to comply with the statutory requirement.

Trial -- Bifurcated Trial -- Amnesia Regarding Facts Surrounding Accident 

3. In a personal injury action to recover damages for head injuries allegedly sustained by the infant plaintiff when he was struck by a vehicle while he was roller blading on a street, plaintiffs were not entitled to a unified trial combining the issues of liability and damages merely because of the child's alleged amnesia regarding the facts surrounding the accident. Plaintiffs failed to demonstrate that the nature of their child's injuries was inextricably intertwined with the issue of liability or that his injuries were probative in determining how the accident happened.

Trial -- Instructions -- Amnesia Regarding Facts Surrounding Accident 

4. In a personal injury action to recover damages for head injuries allegedly sustained by the infant plaintiff when he was struck by a vehicle, plaintiffs were not entitled to a Noseworthy charge based upon the child's alleged amnesia regarding the facts surrounding the accident. The infant recalled certain facts, to which he had testified, and he executed an errata sheet with respect to his examination, having changed certain of his testimony, explaining the change based upon his having been confused at the time of his deposition or as a result of his having misspoken. Under these circumstances, a Noseworthy charge was not warranted.

Attorney and Client -- Representation of Multiple Defendants by Single Defense Counsel 

5. In a personal injury action to recover damages for the head injuries allegedly sustained by the infant plaintiff when he was struck by a leased vehicle, defendants were not required to have only one defense counsel participate in the trial. Defendant driver had private insurance, defendant leasing company was self-insured, and there was a cross claim for contractual indemnification. Thus, defendants were not so united in interest as to warrant a single defense.

People v. Kenner


00-1289


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, WESTCHESTER COUNTY


5 Misc. 3d 1018(A); 799 N.Y.S.2d 163; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2301; 2004 NY Slip Op 51440(U)



September 14, 2004, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] THIS OPINION IS UNCORRECTED AND WILL NOT BE PUBLISHED IN THE PRINTED OFFICIAL REPORTS.



DISPOSITION: Defendant's motion to dismiss denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to dismiss count two of the indictment that charged defendant with murder in the second degree pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 125.25(2).


OVERVIEW: Defendant previously filed a motion to dismiss in the same court under the same indictment due to the alleged insufficiency of the evidence adduced before the grand jury. The trial court refused to dismiss second degree murder charge. Upon completion of the trial, defendant was convicted of second degree murder. The conviction was reversed on grounds unrelated to the instant application. Defendant then filed the instant motion seeking dismissal of the second degree murder count, arguing that the evidence adduced before the grand jury and before the petit jury was insufficient to support the second degree murder charge. Defendant also argued that N.Y. Penal Law § 125.25(2) was unconstitutionally vague. The motion, if treated as a motion seeking reargument, was untimely under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 2221(d)(3), as it was not filed within 30 days following service of the order denying the prior motion. If the trial court considered the motion as a de novo application, the law of the case precluded defendant from raising the issues in the motion before the trial court, as they were raised in the previous motion and that motion was denied.


OUTCOME: The motion was denied.


HEADNOTES

 [*1018A]  [**163] Courts--Law of Cases--Upon retrial, law of the case precluded dismissal of count charged in indictment due to insufficiency of evidence presented to grand jury. Civil Practice Law and Rules--§ 2221 (d) (Motion affecting prior order; motion to leave to reargue).

 

1589 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Millhollen


03-40681


CITY COURT OF NEW YORK, ITHACA


5 Misc. 3d 810; 786 N.Y.S.2d 703; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1642; 2004 NY Slip Op 24371



September 2, 2004, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Motion to dismiss at close of the People's case granted; case dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with one count of trespass in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 140.05, and one count each of disorderly conduct in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 240.20(6) and (7), arising from her tree-sitting as a form of protest. She sought dismissal of the charges.


OVERVIEW: Defendant climbed a tree on a university campus and refused to descend after being told to do so by university police officers and officials. One officer testified that he ordered defendant to climb down because he was concerned that she might fall or that the tree might blow over. However, defendant was tied to the tree by a safety line. A university official read the campus regulations to defendant, but it was noted that there was no specific prohibition against tree climbing or tree-sitting. The court found that it was unclear whether tree-sitting was a prohibited First Amendment activity if done during the time that defendant engaged in the activity. However, it was clear that the "trespass" was an integral part of defendant's speech, in that the act of sitting in the tree actually was the "speech." As there was no showing that her presence in the tree was incompatible with the university's normal activities, the charge lacked merit. The disorderly conduct charges also required dismissal, as there was no showing of public inconvenience or the likelihood of same, and there was no hazard to anyone but defendant. Further, her action had a legitimate purpose.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's motion to dismiss the charges against her.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Trespass -- Tree Sitting on University Campus to Protest Creation of Parking Lot 

1. Defendant's conduct in tree sitting on the grounds of the university where she was an enrolled student to protest the felling of trees to create a parking lot on the campus, despite being told to descend by university officials, did not constitute trespass (Penal Law § 140.05) in the absence of any proof that her presence in the tree was incompatible with the university's normal activities of teaching and research. Defendant was well-equipped and used safety gear to prevent injury. Defendant's tree sitting constituted protected First Amendment activity, especially in view of the university's own student policies encouraging peaceful protest as being essential to academic freedom and the function of the university as an educational institution. There was insufficient evidence that defendant's tree sitting was any different from picketing on the ground.

Crimes -- Disorderly Conduct -- Tree Sitting on University Campus to Protest Creation of Parking Lot 

2. Defendant's conduct in tree sitting on the grounds of the university where she was an enrolled student to protest the felling of trees to create a parking lot on the campus, despite being told to descend by university officials, did not constitute disorderly conduct in the absence of evidence of the likelihood of public inconvenience or the creation of a hazardous or physically offensive condition (Penal Law § 240.20 [6], [7]). Defendant was well-equipped and used safety gear to prevent injury. Furthermore, defendant's conduct could not be classified as an action without legitimate purpose, especially in view of the university's own student policies encouraging peaceful protest as being essential to academic freedom and the function of the university as an educational institution.

 

1590 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of C.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


FAMILY COURT OF NEW YORK, QUEENS COUNTY


6 Misc. 3d 357; 789 N.Y.S.2d 610; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1809; 2004 NY Slip Op 24337



August 31, 2004, Decided



DISPOSITION: Mr. S.'s motion to compel denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner couple brought an action to adopt two children who were borne out of wedlock. After being served a notice of adoption, respondent children's biological father appeared and moved to compel respondent adoption agency and respondent pre-adoptive parents to provide him with discovery materials. The children's law guardian and the agency opposed the motion. The motion implicated N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 111-a.


OVERVIEW: The determinative issue in deciding whether the father had a right to discover materials was whether the father was a "party." The court concluded that the father was not a party, but he was an interested person. Section 111-a provided the father with a right to be heard concerning the children's best interests as an interested person. Section 111-a did not confer on the father any additional rights. The United States Supreme Court's decision in Stanley v. Illinois, 405 U.S. 645 (1972), held, in effect, that the father had a constitutional due process right to be heard concerning the best interests of the child; Stanley did not hold that the father had substantive rights. Stanley did hold that a statute declaring that the father of an illegitimate child was deemed unfit to be a parent was unconstitutional as a violation of the Equal Protection Clause. Since Stanley, the New York Legislature had not provided any substantive rights to the father other than the right to be heard. Therefore, since the father did not have the requisite substantive rights to be a "party," he did not have a right to discovery. N.Y. C.P.L.R. § 3120(1) provided that only parties had a right to discovery.


OUTCOME: The court denied the father's motion to compel discovery.


HEADNOTES

Adoption -- Notice to Putative Father -- Discovery 

A putative father entitled to notice of the adoption proceeding regarding his two children pursuant to Domestic Relations Law § 111-a was not entitled to utilize any discovery devices otherwise available to parties in the proceeding (see CPLR 3120). The sole purpose of the statutory notice is to afford a putative father the right to be heard and present evidence relevant to the best interests of his child. The right to notice made the putative father an interested person, but did not elevate him to the status of a party in the adoption proceeding.

 

1591 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. NYTAC Corp.


HUTO 506-04


DISTRICT COURT OF NEW YORK, THIRD DISTRICT, SUFFOLK COUNTY


5 Misc. 3d 304; 783 N.Y.S.2d 775; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1276



August 27, 2004, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Corporate defendant's application to appear in the proceeding via its president (in lieu of counsel) granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a corporation, moved for an order waiving the requirements of N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 600.20 for the purpose of allowing the corporation to be arraigned and to participate and defend the action. The People did not interpose opposition to the requested relief.


OVERVIEW: The corporation was charged with violating a municipal ordinance that prohibited engaging in the collection of solid waste without a permit. The corporation, which was small and closely held, sought to allow the prosecution and disposition of the matter without an attorney. Pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 600.20, a corporation could only appear in a criminal matter by counsel. The court held that N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 600.20 was unconstitutional, as it denied corporations equal protection of the law. Under United States Supreme Court rulings, corporations were persons under the Equal Protection Clause of the United States Constitution. It was settled law that defendants in criminal proceedings had a right to defend pro se. The court found no compelling state interest achieved by forcing a particular class of person, corporations, to appear by counsel. The court found numerous inconsistencies regarding the rights of corporations in New York law. Corporations were allowed in commercial actions to bring actions without counsel, and the requirement for counsel in civil actions had been relaxed.


OUTCOME: The corporation's application to appear in the proceeding via its president was granted.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Equal Protection of Laws -- Requirement That Corporate Criminal Defendants Appear Only by Counsel 

Defendant, a closely held private corporation charged with violating a local ordinance that prohibits the collection of solid waste in the township without a permit, had the right to appear in the criminal proceeding by its president without counsel. Corporations, as persons under the 14th Amendment, have a fundamental right to defend pro se by a corporate officer in a criminal proceeding. CPL 600.20, which requires that corporate criminal defendants appear only by counsel, is unconstitutional as violative of equal protection under the law under either a strict scrutiny or rational basis analysis. This requirement serves no compelling state interest, especially in view of the relaxation of the appearance by counsel rule in certain civil cases (see CPLR 321; UDCA 1809).

United for Peace & Justice v. Bloomberg


111893/04


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


5 Misc. 3d 845; 783 N.Y.S.2d 255; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1754; 2004 NY Slip Op 24389



August 25, 2004, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at Forbes v. City of New York, 2008 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 63021 (S.D.N.Y., Aug. 12, 2008)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Plaintiff's application for declaratory relief and an injunction denied, and the underlying action is hereby dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff organization filed an action against defendants, a city, its mayor, a parks commissioner, and a police commissioner, which sought declaratory and injunctive relief with regards to the denial of a permit for a demonstration.


OVERVIEW: The organization sought a permit to allow for a rally in a park, despite the fact that it had previously agreed to hold the event in a different location. After the permit was denied, the organization filed an action seeking injunctive and declaratory relief based on a violation of N.Y. Const. art. I, § 8. In denying relief, the court determined that the request for a special events permit was properly denied because the elements for an injunction were not met. The New York City Parks Department appropriately applied the content-neutral regulations contained in New York City, N.Y., R. tit. 56, § 2-08 while leaving the organization with a reasonable alternative site suitable with ample means of communication. The city was not required to grant the permit exactly as requested. The right to free speech did not guarantee the organization the best location for its communication. Moreover, by seeking to invoke the court's equity jurisdiction just days prior to the event, the organization foreclosed an opportunity for the city to formulate an appropriate plan to ensure the safety of the public and protect the park from irreparable damage.


OUTCOME: The relief sought was denied, and the underlying action was dismissed.


HEADNOTES

Injunctions -- Preliminary Injunction -- Denial of Special Events Permit 

Plaintiff was not entitled to a preliminary injunction enjoining defendant City of New York and its Department of Parks and Recreation from denying its request for a special events permit for a 215,000-person rally in Central Park on the eve of the 2004 Republican National Convention. The Parks Department properly applied content-neutral factors (see 56 RCNY 2-08) in denying a permit for the Central Park location, while leaving plaintiff with a reasonable alternate site to stage the rally without unnecessarily burdening plaintiff's right to free speech. Plaintiff failed to demonstrate a constitutional violation or a balancing of the equities in its favor. Plaintiff had "unclean hands" because it was guilty of inexcusable and inequitable delay and was thus not entitled to any equitable relief.

Hernandez v. Robles


103434/2004

Matter of Chavis v. N.Y. Temp. State Comm'n on Lobbying


959-04


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, ALBANY COUNTY


6 Misc. 3d 917; 787 N.Y.S.2d 821; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2880; 2004 NY Slip Op 24539



August 9, 2004, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
Reversed by Chavis v. New York Temp. State Comm'n on Lobbying, 16 A.D.3d 886, 791 N.Y.S.2d 707, 2005 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2641 (2005)



DISPOSITION: Guidelines issued by the Commission declared a nullity; portions of statute declared unconstitutional.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, a coalition and its administrative coordinator, filed a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding against respondent New York Temporary State Commission on Lobbying seeking to compel it to adopt rules governing adjudicatory proceedings and a declaration that a finding by the Commission that petitioners wilfully failed to file a client semiannual report was null and void.


OVERVIEW: The Commission argued that the coalition lacked standing. The trial court held that the coalition was not a proper petitioner, but that the coordinator was a de facto officer performing equivalent functions and had standing to bring the suit in a representative capacity. The Commission was directed to determine whether anyone had knowingly and wilfully violated the New York Lobbying Act (Act), 1981 N.Y. Laws 1040, without giving that person notice and a hearing. The penalty hearings authorized were not adjudicative hearings under the New York State Administrative Procedures Act (SAPA), N.Y. A.P.A. § 101 et seq. Thus, the Commission was not an agency and was not subject to the SAPA. The statutory scheme did not provide due process as it allowed a lobbyist to be deprived of property without notice and the opportunity to be heard. The Commission's guidelines could not correct the defect in § 1-n(b) and (c) of the Act. Further, the Commission lacked the statutory authority to issue the guidelines as the statute directed the Commission to make its ruling before holding a penalty hearing. The guidelines created notice and a hearing, were inconsistent with the statute, and were a nullity.


OUTCOME: To the extent necessary, the proceeding was converted to a declaratory judgment action. A declaratory judgment was granted to petitioners declaring that the challenged statute was unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Parties -- Standing -- Unincorporated Association 

1. Petitioner, the administrative coordinator of an unincorporated association facing penalties for allegedly failing to comply with the filing requirements of the Lobbying Act (Legislative Law § 1-c [c]), had standing to challenge the constitutionality of the statutory scheme on behalf of the association even though any action brought on behalf of an unincorporated association must normally be maintained by the association's president or treasurer (see General Associations Law § 12). Since the association had no president or treasurer, petitioner, as the de facto officer performing the equivalent functions and responsibilities of those positions, had the necessary capacity to sue on the association's behalf.

Legislature -- Lobbying -- Adjudicatory Proceedings 

2. Respondent New York Temporary State Commission on Lobbying is not subject to the requirements of the State Administrative Procedure Act in conducting adjudicatory proceedings under the Lobbying Act. The Commission is not an "agency" authorized to make rules or make final decisions in adjudicatory proceedings (see State Administrative Procedure Act § 102 [1]).

Legislature -- Lobbying -- Due Process Requirements 

3. Portions of the Lobbying Act are unconstitutional as violative of due process to the extent they fail to provide for notice and the opportunity for a hearing prior to a violation determination. The statute directs respondent New York Temporary State Commission on Lobbying to unilaterally determine whether there has been a knowing and willful violation of the Lobbying Act without affording the alleged violator notice and the opportunity for a hearing. The only hearing authorized under the statute is a hearing to determine the amount of the penalty to be assessed against the violator (see Legislative Law § 1-n [b], [c]). Under these circumstances, the procedural guidelines adopted by respondent providing for written notice to the violator and the opportunity for a hearing upon the charges are insufficient to remedy the constitutional defects in the statutory scheme.

Matter of Waugh v. Nowicki


10349/04


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NASSAU COUNTY

1597 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


563 Grand Med. P.C. v. New York State Ins. Dept.


44825/03


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


5 Misc. 3d 952; 787 N.Y.S.2d 613; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1915; 2004 NY Slip Op 24415



July 30, 2004, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed by, Remitted by 563 Grand Med., P.C. v. New York State Ins. Dep't, 24 A.D.3d 413, 805 N.Y.S.2d 643, 2005 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13740 (2d Dept 2005)



DISPOSITION: Defendants' motion granted and complaint dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff arbitration applicants sued defendants, the New York State Insurance Department and its Superintendent, seeking a judgment declaring that N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 11 § 65-4.5(o)(1) violated the Due Process Clauses of the United States and New York Constitutions and restraining arbitrators from invoking it at arbitration hearings regarding no-fault insurance benefits. The Department and Superintendent moved to dismiss.


OVERVIEW: The applicants said N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 11, §§ 65-4.5(o)(1) and 65-4.2(b)(3) precluded proof responding to new issues arbitrators raised, violating procedural due process. They said § 65-4.2(b)(3)(iv), giving arbitrators discretion to accept additional written proof, did not cure the violation as it was discretionary and no supplemental oral argument was guaranteed. Further, the same regulations bound the master arbitrators to whom the applicants could appeal. Due process required a chance to be heard and to submit evidence. The regulations provided that opportunity and ensured that arbitration decisions comported with due process by allowing appeals to a master arbitrator and judicial review. N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 11, §§ 65-4.5(o)(1) and 65-4.2(b)(3) allowed supplementation of a claimant's submissions and could constitutionally be applied in tandem, so the applicants' facial challenge had to be dismissed. The applicants did not exhaust administrative remedies by seeking a master arbitrator's review, nor did they challenge a specific regulatory application, so they had no standing to challenge the regulations as applied.


OUTCOME: Defendants' motion to dismiss was granted.


HEADNOTES

Insurance -- No-Fault Automobile Insurance -- Validity of No-Fault Regulations Permitting Submission of Supplementary Papers

No-fault arbitration regulations, which authorize the arbitrator to permit supplementation of the claimant's original submissions, but do not guarantee a right to supplemental oral argument (11 NYCRR 65-4.2 [b] [3]), are not facially unconstitutional because they prevent a claimant from submitting additional proof in response to new issues raised by arbitrators. The regulations as a whole provide procedural safeguards through the appellate process. Claimants may appeal adverse decisions to master arbitrators and may further seek judicial review of those decisions to ensure that arbitration decisions comport with constitutional due process requirements (see, Insurance Law § 5106 [c]; 11 NYCRR 65-4.10). Furthermore, plaintiffs lacked standing to bring an "as applied" due process challenge, since they failed to exhaust their administrative remedies.
