1599 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Cooper


9860/04


DISTRICT COURT OF NEW YORK, FIRST DISTRICT, NASSAU COUNTY


4 Misc. 3d 788; 781 N.Y.S.2d 201; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1116; 2004 NY Slip Op 24265



July 15, 2004, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendant's motion to dismiss denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought to dismiss charges against him of Aggravated Harassment in the Second Degree, in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 240.30(1), a misdemeanor criminal offense.


OVERVIEW: The complainant's husband had allegedly made harassing telephone calls to defendant's wife. Defendant allegedly called the complaining witness and left a message on her cell phone telling the complainant that if the complainant's husband did not stop calling his wife, defendant would "bury her," and that she had "been warned." Defendant was charged with violating § 240.30(1) and sought to dismiss the charges. The court first noted that a decision of the U.S. District Court for the Southern District of New York declared that § 240.30(1) violated the First Amendment but that a decision of the Appellate Term, Second Department held that the provision withstood constitutional scrutiny. The court then denied the motion. Whether the alleged "threat" met the applicable constitutional or statutory standards was "usually a question of fact," and on a motion to dismiss for facial insufficiency, the court held the People to a less strict standard than would be the case at trial. However, the court noted that arguably defendant's phone call was not within the statutory definition because it was an isolated, angry outburst not reasonably to be taken seriously as a threat.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Aggravated Harassment 

Defendant was not entitled to dismissal, on constitutional grounds, of the aggravated harassment charge filed against him based upon an allegedly harassing message left on the complaining witness's cell phone in which defendant threatened to "bury her" if her husband did not stop making harassing telephone calls to defendant's wife. The aggravated harassment statute, which proscribes communicating "with another person . . . by telephone . . . in a manner likely to cause annoyance or alarm" when the communication is made "with intent to harass, annoy, threaten or alarm" such other person (Penal Law § 240.30 [1]), is not unconstitutional on its face as being violative of the First Amendment right to free speech. The factual issue of whether defendant's message constituted a proscribable genuine threat, or was merely an isolated, angry outburst not reasonably to be taken seriously as a threat, could not be determined on a motion to dismiss for facial insufficiency.

 

1600 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Greenleaf


04030294


JUSTICE COURT OF NEW YORK, TOWN OF NEW PALTZ, ULSTER COUNTY


5 Misc. 3d 337; 780 N.Y.S.2d 899; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1121



July 13, 2004, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Motion to dismiss the charges granted; charges dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, Unitarian-Universalist ministers, moved to dismiss on constitutional grounds their prosecutions for violating N.Y. Dom. Rel. § 17, which made it a misdemeanor to perform a marriage without being presented with a marriage license.


OVERVIEW: Section 17 made it a misdemeanor to perform marriages without being presented with a license. When local officials in a New York town refused to issue licenses to same-sex couples, two ministers proceeded to perform religious ceremonies in front of the courthouse. The court dismissed the charges against the ministers. The prosecution had not shown any rational relationship between the prohibition against issuing licenses to same-sex couples and the legitimate state interest in providing a favorable environment for procreation and child-rearing, and traditional views of marriage were no justification at all. Furthermore, New York statutory, regulatory, and case law were replete with examples indicating a supportive attitude toward stable same-sex unions. Under the circumstances, there was no rational basis for § 17 as applied to the ministers. Prosecution of the ministers violated both federal constitutional principles and New York public policy.


OUTCOME: The charges against the ministers were dismissed.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Equal Protection of Laws -- Unauthorized Performance of Marriage Ceremonies for Same-Sex Couples

Defendants, two ordained ministers, were entitled to dismissal of misdemeanor charges arising from their performance of marriage ceremonies for same-sex couples who did not have valid marriage licenses (see Domestic Relations Law § 17). Defendants demonstrated that it was unconstitutional as violative of equal protection to prohibit same-sex couples from obtaining marriage licenses. Consequently, it was also unconstitutional to charge defendants with a crime for marrying same-sex couples who were unable to obtain marriage licenses. The justifications of "tradition" and procreation proffered by the prosecution for limiting marriage to opposite-sex couples were inadequate to sustain the constitutionality of defendants' criminal prosecution under the rational basis test. "Tradition" is not a legitimate state interest and did not justify unconstitutional treatment. Furthermore, prohibiting same-sex couples from marrying is not rationally related to furthering the State's legitimate interest in providing a favorable environment for procreation and child rearing.
